# Jeju 4·3 Incident Truth Investigation Report
## Chapters IV & V — Conclusion and Appendices

> **English Translation** — Original source: 제주4·3사건 진상조사보고서 (2003)
> Original pages: pp. 443–615 (173 pages)
> **Chapter IV**: Post-incident measures and redress processes (pp. 443–532)
> **Chapter V**: Conclusion of the Truth Investigation Report (pp. 533–541)
> **Appendices**: Presidential statement, chronology, legal documents (pp. 543–615)
> See [README.md](./README.md) for translation principles and [GLOSSARY.md](./GLOSSARY.md) for terminology.

---

**[p. 443]**

1) Defendants in General Trials

① Pre-4·3 Period (March 1, 1947–April 2, 1948)

During the commemorative gathering and demonstrations marking the 28th anniversary of the March 1 Independence Movement in 1947, six fatal shootings occurred, and among those who participated in the subsequent March 10 general strike, the principal instigators were indicted. Military courts-martial began on April 3, 1947. On April 14, 1947, the military courts-martial were transferred to the Korean people's courts, and jurisdiction over trials of defendants related to the March 1 incident in Jeju was assigned to the Cheju District Trial Court. Accordingly, continuous trials of March 1 incident-related cases began at the Cheju District Trial Court from April 21. Ultimately, the public trial of the March 1 incident cases that commenced on April 3, 1947, concluded on May 23. Among the defendants upon whom sentences were handed down, there were 52 persons sentenced to imprisonment, 52 given suspended sentences, 56 given fines, and a total of 328 persons given other dispositions such as prosecution suspension and non-prosecution.[^122] Among those who received actual sentences, the highest was one year imposed on Hŏ Tu-mun, while the others received sentences of one year or less. These individuals were mostly imprisoned at Mokpo Prison, where they served their terms. The trial court also stipulated that those who received fines but failed to pay them were to be detained in a labor facility at a conversion rate of 125 won per day.[^123]

Even after the March 1 incident trial concluded, political and social friction continued between civilians and U.S. Military Government police in the Cheju region in the latter half of 1947 and the first half of 1948. Representative incidents are as follows:

**1947:**
- June 2: Collective school boycott by Cheju girls' middle school students
- June 6: Minchŏng members in Jongdalri brutally assaulted three police officers
- June–early July: Distribution of pamphlets by elementary school teacher training students, Chochŏn middle school students, and village youth
- July 3: Confrontation and labor dispute between school authorities and students at Cheju Agricultural Middle School
- July end: Village residents at Myŏngwol-ri in Hallim-myŏn refused grain collection
- August 8: Young men in Tonggwang-ri, Andŭk-myŏn, brutally assaulted three grain collection officials
- August 13: Collision between police and residents at Pugchon-ri, Chochŏn-myŏn; casualties on both sides
- August 14: Arrest and detention of Park Kyŏng-hun and other key figures of the Cheju Democratic Front

**[p. 444]**

**1948:**
- February 7–10: "February 27 Struggle" unfolded throughout the island; armed clashes with police
- February 9: Young men in Sagye-ri, Andŭk-myŏn, beat police officers
- March 6: Chochŏn middle school student Kim Yong-chŏl brought to Chochŏn Police Box, died under torture
- March 14: Yang Ŭn-ha from Yŏngnak-ri, Taejŏng-myŏn, detained at Mosulpo Police Box, died under torture

In relation to these incidents, a considerable number of residents were arrested by police and referred for trial.

From trial judgments related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident obtained by the Jeju 4·3 Committee from the Government Records Preservation Office, including the March 1 incident trials conducted at the Cheju District Trial Court from March 1, 1947, to April 3, 1948, the total number of defendants was 480. Classified by sentencing:
- 82 persons sentenced to imprisonment
- 150 persons given suspended sentences
- 248 persons given simple fines

As in the previous trials following the March 1 incident, no defendants received harsh sentences of one year or more. Therefore, defendants tried in 1947 and early 1948 were released from prison by the end of 1948 and either returned to Cheju or fled to other regions of Japan, so there were no cases of death in prison as a result of these trials.

② Post-4·3 Period (April 3, 1948–September 21, 1954)

Beginning with the armed guerrillas' assault on April 3, 1948, the incident escalated into mutual armed clashes between police and armed guerrillas. Passing through a lull from late April to early May, the armed guerrillas resumed anti-May 10 election struggle and attacked police, right-wing organizations, and election officials. When the May 10 election was declared void in Jeju's two electoral districts, the U.S. Military Government concluded that the Jeju situation was deteriorating further and strengthened reinforcements of police and suppression troops. Naturally, the number of criminal case suspects arrested by police increased markedly. Consequently, there were too many criminal cases to handle in the Jeju region, and both the local trial court and prosecutors could not process cases expeditiously. Eventually, on May 26, the central judicial authorities dispatched special judges and prosecutors specifically to the Jeju region. The dispatched personnel included Judge Shim Dong-gu from the Seoul High Trial Court, Chief Judge Yang Won-il from the District Trial Court, Judges Choi Dong-uk, Hwang Sŏng-hui, and Kim Sang-muk, together with three clerks; and prosecutors Park Kŭn-yŏng, Yi Chu-shin, and Ahn Hui-kyŏng, together with three clerks.[^124] These personnel commenced duties on May 31 and proceeded with public trials expeditiously. In a single day on the 31st,

**[p. 445]** they completed trial review of four Jeju 4·3 incident-related cases, with the prosecution seeking sentences including one execution, one 10-year sentence, two 7-year sentences, and one 5-year sentence.[^125] The twelve judges and prosecutors dispatched from Seoul handed down verdicts on 42 cases involving 37 defendants before returning to Seoul on June 11. The content of these verdicts was as follows:[^126]

- Violation of Proclamation No. 2: 32 cases (maximum 3 years imprisonment, minimum 6 months)
- Murder: 2 cases (life imprisonment, 10 years)
- Attempted murder: 2 cases (life imprisonment)
- Aiding and abetting murder: 1 case (3 years)
- Arson: 1 case (life imprisonment)
- Preparation for murder: 1 case (2-year suspended sentence)
- Preparation for arson: 1 case (2-year suspended sentence)
- Obstruction of passage: 2 cases (2 years)

**Total:** 42 cases with sentences of one year or more; 37 defendants

One defense lawyer who came to Jeju for the trials stated: "Not a single person responsible for instigating the rioting was apprehended; those referred for trial in these proceedings are young men aged 19 to 25. They are people who acted mechanically, without understanding anything."[^127]

Jo Dŏk-song, dispatched to Cheju in June 1948 as a correspondent, wrote the following account:

> Military courts are now being held to judge the rioters. From among the numerous prisoners brought in, only those with criminal allegations are clarified in court. The defendants are mostly young men around 20 years old. Their families and relatives have turned the courtroom daily into a sea of tears, and the passionate arguments of the defense counsel are moving prosecutors. "These are mostly people who moved mechanically and acted without any consciousness. If such people must be punished, would there not be thousands of captured people who should be tried in court? I do not desire severe punishment for those who instigated and taught. However, these virtuous islanders and islanders suffering unjust sacrifice must not be confused with these people." This is part of the defense counsel's argument.[^128]

Furthermore, the judges and prosecutors who came to Jeju reported to the U.S. Military Government that the trials in Jeju were difficult due to surrounding pressure, and the U.S. Military Government accepted this and transferred jurisdiction of trials related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident to the Kwangju District Court for continued proceedings. These circumstances are confirmed in the following document left by the U.S. Military Government:

> Trials related to the disturbance on Cheju Island continued to attract the attention of the courts. A team consisting of two judges and the Attorney General went to Cheju in July (error for June) and, after concluding the majority of cases arising from the disturbance, returned to Seoul. Some 250 persons are still being detained in police detention facilities. The judges reported that the atmosphere in Cheju was "inappropriate" for trials of disturbance cases. The judges themselves suffered from continuous fear for their personal safety. They feared that if they handed down severe sentences on the rioters, leftist violence would befall them, but if they handed down light sentences, rightist violence would befall them. They recommended that serious disturbance cases be transferred for trial in Mokpo or Kwangju. The Military Governor approved the Judicial Council's authority to transfer any case from Cheju for trial in Mokpo or Kwangju at his discretion.[^129]

According to records in the U.S. Forces in Korea Command, as of July 1948, 216 persons had been indicted and convicted, and 115 of them were in custody.[^130] Furthermore, according to the report of Chu Chŏl-sun, a police inspector and commander of the Third Cheju Island Suppression Operations, upon his return on August 20, 1948, between April 3 and August 3, 977 "rioters" were arrested, of which 436 were turned over to the prosecutors.[^131]

Even after the judges and prosecutors dispatched from Seoul completed their trials and returned, the Cheju District Court alone could not adequately handle hundreds of suspects. Moreover, since the safety and fairness of trials could not be secured and there were recommendations to move cases involving important suspects to Kwangju for trial, some defendants were transferred to the Kwangju District Prosecutors' Office. On August 23, 1948, 32 Jeju 4·3 Incident suspects undergoing interrogation at the Cheju Prosecutors' Office were transferred to the Kwangju District Prosecutors' Office and detained in Kwangju Prison. On September 4, Assistant Prosecutor Kim Yŏng-chŏn of the Kwangju District Prosecutors' Office issued a decision on orders from the Supreme Court to transfer these cases from the Cheju District Court to the Kwangju District Court.[^132] An additional 40 persons were transferred to Kwangju on September 3.[^133]

**[p. 446]**

In the second half of 1948, a total of 131 defendants (74 cases) related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident were transferred from Jeju. Public trials for these individuals were continued at the Kwangju District Court from October 1, 1948, to December 29, 1948, spanning approximately three months. Listed by date, the trial proceedings were as follows:

- October 1: 12 cases, 18 defendants, fact-finding and prosecution arguments
- October 8: 8 defendants, prosecution arguments
- October 13: 21 defendants, verdicts handed down
- October 14–15: 17 defendants, prosecution arguments
- October 23: Verdicts were to be handed down but indefinitely postponed due to the Yŏsu-Sunchŏn Incident
- November 26: 22 defendants, prosecution arguments
- November 30: 22 defendants, verdicts handed down
- December 7: 36 defendants, prosecution arguments
- December 8: 12 defendants, prosecution arguments
- December 16: 6 defendants, verdicts handed down
- December 17: 13 defendants, verdicts handed down
- December 21: 9 defendants, verdicts handed down
- December 29: 17 defendants, verdicts handed down

The verdicts for these 131 defendants (74 cases) included: one execution, five life sentences, 82 sentences ranging from 8 months to 15 years, eight suspended sentences, and 19 acquittals.

Since martial law was in effect in the Jeju region from November 17, 1948, to December 31, 1948, no civilian trials were conducted outside military courts-martial. The continuation of civilian trials at the Kwangju District Court was because these cases had already been indicted by prosecutors for conduct prior to the martial law proclamation. After martial law ended in 1949, defendants related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident again underwent trials at the Cheju District Court.

From 1949 until the end of the Jeju 4·3 Incident in 1954, records of civilian trials related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident are largely absent from various newspaper articles, making them difficult to ascertain. However, based on trial judgments related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident obtained by the Jeju 4·3 Committee from the Government Records Preservation Office, among 599 total defendants from 1949 to 1954, 361 were sentenced to actual imprisonment (prison terms), and 238 received suspended sentences. Among those sentenced to actual imprisonment were: one execution, two 10-year sentences, one 8-year sentence, seven 7-year sentences, one 6-year sentence, twelve 5-year sentences, twelve 4-year sentences, 42 sentences of 3–3.5 years, 46 sentences of 2–2.5 years, and so on.

In summary, regarding civilian trials related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident, the long-term prisoners sentenced to two years or more are estimated at approximately 40 from the Seoul-dispatched Cheju District Court verdict, approximately 80 from the Kwangju District Court verdict, and approximately 80 from the Cheju District Court verdict from 1949 to June 1950—a total of approximately 200 persons. Therefore, at the time of the outbreak of the June 25 War, the number of persons imprisoned in prisons does not deviate significantly from approximately 200, accounting for various variables. Most of these persons were detained in Mokpo and Kwangju prisons and became victims of loss of life during the war by disappearing.

**2) Defendants in Military Courts-Martial**

During the Jeju 4·3 Incident, military courts-martial targeting military personnel and military-affiliated employees were held several times. However, documents remain indicating that military courts-martial targeting civilians were conducted twice—in December 1948 and July 1949 (hereinafter referred to as the "1948 military courts-martial" and "1949 military courts-martial"). Specifically, the military courts-martial orders held by the Government Records Preservation Office contain information about military courts-martial and lists of persons referred to such courts. Appended to the military courts-martial orders are rosters of 2,530 military court-martial defendants (871 from the 1948 courts-martial and 1,659 from the 1949 courts-martial). The orders contain establishment orders, trial locations, charges (criminal category and criminal facts), actions by the reviewing officer, and actions by the confirming officer, all printed. The appended rosters of defendants are formatted as tables divided by categories such as defendants' personal information, pleas, verdicts, dates of sentencing, and custodial facilities (prisons). Separate rosters of those sentenced to death are also included.[^134]

**[p. 447]**

These military court-martial defendants were dispersed and detained in prisons throughout the country—in Seoul, Inchŏn, Taejŏn, Taegu, Chŏnju, and Mokpo. When the June 25 War broke out in 1950, many were summarily executed by gunshot in each prison according to purge policies for "undesirable elements." Some scattered in various directions as prison gates were opened, disappearing without a trace; their fate remains unknown to this day.

① The "1948 Military Courts-Martial"

In July 1948, after the Ninth Regiment, which had been attached to the Eleventh Regiment, was reorganized, the Ninth Regiment (under Colonel Song Yo-ch'an) took the lead in operations on Jeju Island. On October 11, 1948, the Jeju Island Defense Headquarters (Commander Colonel Kim Sang-gyŏm, Deputy Commander Colonel Song Yo-ch'an) was established, and large-scale joint military-police suppression operations were launched. With Colonel Kim Sang-gyŏm, a brigadier commander of the Fifth Brigade in Kwangju, becoming the commander of the Jeju Island Defense Headquarters, the command of the Jeju suppression forces was elevated from regimental to brigadier level, and suppression efforts were further strengthened. The Jeju Island Defense Headquarters exercised overall command of the Ninth Regiment, one battalion of the Fifth Regiment, one battalion of the Sixth Regiment, naval forces, and the Jeju Police Security Contingent.[^135] Furthermore, the Jeju Island Defense Commander was also granted "authority over military courts-martial during purge operations."[^136] The Defense Headquarters Command further ordered the First Battalion of the Fourteenth Regiment stationed in Yŏsu, which was under the Fifth Brigade, to be dispatched to Jeju Island. This strengthening of suppression operations on Jeju Island was set back by the outbreak of the Yŏsu Incident on October 19, but strong suppression operations led by the Ninth Regiment were nevertheless carried out without exception. In accordance with a proclamation issued by Ninth Regiment Commander Song Yo-ch'an on October 17, an evacuation order was issued for civilians in Jeju's midland regions, and large-scale suppression operations were launched. The targets of these operations included not only armed guerrillas but also ordinary residents living in villages. Moreover, martial law was proclaimed in Jeju on November 17, 1948. Until its lifting on December 31, the Ninth Regiment adopted a "mass execution plan" targeting all midland village residents "on the assumption that they were providing assistance and convenience to guerrillas to eliminate all resistance."[^137]

During this process of collective killing, a considerable number of residents who escaped death were captured by military units and temporarily detained in holding facilities such as the Jeju Agricultural School. In mid-October, the Defense Headquarters arrested 800 persons during operations. These persons were detained in holding facilities to determine whether they were connected with the armed guerrillas.[^138] Many of these were brought to military courts-martial, as recorded in the military courts-martial orders. In the case of Kŭmdŏk-ri, Aewŏl-myŏn, at around 6 a.m. on October 11, 1948, the Ninth Regiment surrounded the village and arrested youth, detaining them at the Jeju Agricultural School.[^140] According to the military courts-martial orders, in early December 1948, 25 residents of Kŭmdŏk-ri were referred to military courts-martial and received prison sentences, being transferred to prisons throughout the country.

From late October 1948 onward, military and police suppression authorities launched large-scale arrests targeting civil servants, legal professionals, journalists, and educators who had participated in the March 1 incident-related demonstrations and strikes in 1947. Furthermore, arrests and interrogations of those who had incurred the resentment of the Sŏbuk Youth Corps intensified. Property management office employees managing enemy-seized assets—Han Byŏng-t'aek, Kim In-ji, Kang Sun-hyŏn, Kim T'ae-ho, Yang Mun-su, Kang Ch'ang-u, Kim Hak-nim—were arrested by military authorities and detained in tents at the Agricultural School where the Ninth Regiment headquarters was located. Among these, Kang Sun-hyŏn, Kim T'ae-ho, and Kang Ch'ang-u appear in the military courts-martial orders, having been transferred to prisons in Sŏdaemun, Taegu, and Mokpo respectively.[^141] Similarly, food distribution agency employees Park Se-wŏn and Park Sang-hun are also confirmed in the military courts-martial orders. Yang Si-u and Ahn Tu-wŏn, who worked in the Cheju Shinbo newspaper office, Cheju District Court secretary general Song Tu-hyŏn, and court staff members Yang Sŏng-tu, Hong In-p'yo, and others were also detained at the Agricultural School, processed through military courts-martial, executed or confined to prisons such as Mokpo and Mapo, as recorded in the military courts-martial orders. Beyond these examples, suspects related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident arrested by military and police authorities throughout Jeju were detained at various police stations and holding facilities for interrogation. The Ninth Regiment Commander reported that 1,383 persons were captured between October 1 and November 20.[^143] Some of these underwent civilian trials at the Cheju or Kwangju district courts. However, those detained as unconvicted detainees until martial law was proclaimed on November 17 or those arrested after martial law was proclaimed were virtually all referred to the December 1948 military courts-martial.

**[p. 448]**

According to the military courts-martial orders, the "1948 military courts-martial" was held a total of 12 times from December 3 to December 27, 1948, with the ostensible purpose of martial law military courts-martial.[^144] At this military courts-martial, 871 civilians were uniformly found guilty of violating Article 77 of the old Criminal Code for the crime of rebellion. The contents of the military courts-martial orders are as follows:

---

**Jeju Island Martial Law District High Military Courts-Martial Order No. 20**

1. Establishment Order: By Special Order No. 29 dated December 1, 4281 (1948) and amended by Special Order No. 39 dated December 25, 4281 (1948)

2. Defendants: Per attached roster

3. Charges and Criminal Facts: Violation of Criminal Code Article 77; Crime of Rebellion

4. Trial Location and Dates:

| Session | Date | Session | Date |
|--------|------|--------|------|
| 1st | 12.3 | 7th | 12.10 |
| 2nd | 12.4 | 8th | 12.11 |
| 3rd | 12.5 | 9th | 12.12 |
| 4th | 12.7 | 10th | 12.15 |
| 5th | 12.8 | 11th | 12.26 |
| 6th | 12.9 | 12th | 12.27 |

Jeju District Court

5. Pleas: All defendants pleaded not guilty to the charges and criminal facts.

6. Verdict: All defendants found guilty of the charges and criminal facts.

7. Sentences:
   1) Death: Kim Yun-taek and 99 others
   2) Life Imprisonment: Yang Hae-gil and 101 others

8. Date of Sentencing: From December 3, 4281 (1948) to December 27, 4281 (1948)

9. Action by Reviewing Officer: Approved the verdicts and ordered appropriate execution.

10. Action by Confirming Officer:
    1) Reduced the death sentences of Kim Yong-kyŏ and 60 others to life imprisonment.
    2) Reduced the life sentences of Yang Hae-gil and 96 others to 20-year terms and ordered appropriate execution.
    Executions to be carried out by February 28, 4282 (1949); method and location to be designated by the Jeju Island Martial Law District Commander (or by the Second Regiment Commander upon lifting of martial law).

11. Custodial Facilities: Adults to Mokpo Prison, Mapo Prison, and Taegu Prison; juveniles to Inchŏn Juvenile Prison; females to Chŏnju Prison

December 29, 4281 (1948)
Jeju Island Martial Law District Commander
Colonel Ham Byŏng-sŏn [Seal]

---

Initially, the sentences were 100 death and 102 life imprisonments, but in the final determination of sentences, 62 death sentences were reduced to life imprisonment and 97 life sentences were reduced to 20-year terms, as recorded.[^145]

According to the documents, those sentenced to death in the "1948 military courts-martial" were 39 persons (4.4%), life imprisonment 67 persons (7.7%), the remainder being 20-year sentences 97 persons (11.1%), 15-year sentences 262 persons (30.0%), 5-year sentences 222 persons (25.5%), 3-year sentences 4 persons (0.5%), 1-year sentences 180 persons (20.7%), and so on. The rosters of 39 persons sentenced to death do not appear in the appended rosters of the military courts-martial orders but are separately printed with High Military Courts-Martial Order No. 1. The executions of these individuals took place at 2:30 p.m. on February 27, 1949, in the vicinity of Hwabuk-ri, Cheju-up. The related order was as follows:

---

**Army Headquarters Second Regiment Headquarters High Military Courts-Martial Order No. 1**

Pursuant to Jeju Island Martial Law District High Military Courts-Martial Order No. 20, dated December 29, 4281 (1948), execution of death sentences is ordered as follows:

1. Death Convicts to be Executed: Kim Yun-taek and 38 others (per attached roster)

2. Date of Execution: 2:30 p.m., February 27, 4282 (1949)

3. Place of Execution: A location designated by the execution officer near Hwabuk-ri, Cheju-up, North Cheju County, Jeju Island

4. Execution Officers: Captain Jo Yŏng-gu, Second Lieutenant Chŏng Se-jin

5. Witnessing Officers: Military Physician Major Park Sin-bŏm, Intelligence Officer Second Lieutenant Park T'ae-wŏn

6. For matters not provided for in this order regarding execution methods and other details, the basic methods for military executions shall apply.

February 22, 4282 (1948)
Army Headquarters Second Regiment
Colonel Ham Byŏng-sŏn [Seal]

---

Although the date of execution is known, the exact location is difficult to confirm, as it is stated only as "near Hwabuk-ri." The bodies were buried after shooting, so bereaved families did not know the location at the time. Some family members heard that it was between Hwabuk and Samyang, near hills in the Hwabuk area, along the coastal road between Hwabuk and Samyang, in fields west of the Hwabuk-ri entrance, or in fields along the Hwabuk coastal road.[^146] On August 12, 2002, the Jeju 4·3 Committee's on-site testimony investigation team met with a witness who had personally recovered the remains of his father Kang Yun-gyŏ from the execution site and confirmed the location directly. The witness, Kang Il-su, had learned the burial location from a police relative and visited the site a month later with his uncle. His father had been shot twice with carbine bullets in the abdomen, and the bullets were confirmed; the body was loaded on a hand truck and buried at Cheju-up Todu-ri cemetery. The location is a field on the left upon entering near the entrance to Tongjewŏn on the right side of the main entrance to Ohyŏn High School in Cheju's Hwabuk-dong district, currently remaining as vacant lot.[^151]

**[p. 449]**

The 871 military court-martial defendants listed in the military courts-martial orders, excluding the 38 executed on Jeju Island, were dispersed and detained in Mokpo, Mapo, Sŏdaemun, Taegu, Inchŏn, and Chŏnju prisons respectively. The 67 persons sentenced to life imprisonment do not have custodial facilities listed in the military courts-martial orders, so their prison detention situation is unknown. However, 24 of them are recorded in the March 4, 1949, roster of detainees at Taegu Prison as having been transferred to Taegu Prison in 1949; these 67 persons are estimated to have been detained at Mokpo Prison for approximately two months before being transferred to Taegu and other prisons. The 97 persons listed with 20-year sentences likewise do not show custodial facilities in the military courts-martial orders. However, examination of the 1949 Mapo Prison detainee roster confirms that most of these were detained at Mapo Prison.

A total of 453 persons have Mokpo Prison listed as their custodial facility in the military courts-martial orders. These include 202 sentenced to 15 years, 157 to 5 years, 4 to 3 years, and 90 to 1 year. However, the 202 persons sentenced to 15 years were transferred to Taegu Prison shortly after being detained at Mokpo Prison, as recorded in the 1948–1949 Taegu Prison detainee rosters showing these persons as detained there.

Juveniles aged 19 and under have Inchŏn Prison listed as their custodial facility. A total of 166 were detained, including 56 sentenced to 15 years, 58 to 5 years, and 52 to 1 year. Female detainees were imprisoned at Chŏnju Prison, totaling 48 persons, including three sentenced to 15 years, seven to 5 years, and 38 to 1 year.

Analysis of regional distribution among "1948 military courts-martial" defendants listed in the military courts-martial orders shows that midland village residents constituted a large proportion.[^152] However, the number from Cheju-up villages such as Kŏnip, Yongdam, and Ido-ri, as well as from coastal villages including Todu, Hwabuk, Aewŏl, Hagwi, Sinch'on, Chochŏn, and Kangju-ri, was substantial. Considering the strong implementation of evacuation operations in midland regions after October 1948, the regional distribution of military court-martial defendants reveals that evacuation operations in midland regions were not smoothly executed. Residents of Kŭmdŏk-ri, Aewŏl-myŏn, a midland village, were exceptionally numerous in being arrested by the military and 25 were referred to the "1948 military courts-martial"; however, residents of other regions did not comply with military evacuation operations. This is evidence that more residents escaped to the mountains to evade forced evacuation and as a result of the armed guerrillas' inducement to enter the mountains. Residents of Sŏnhŭl-ri, Chochŏn-myŏn, do not appear among military court-martial defendants; instead, on November 25–26, 1948, they concealed themselves in a cave, and when military and police suppression forces surrounded the cave, 94 residents were collectively executed by gunshot.

The fact that few persons subject to legal processing emerged during this period of large-scale evacuation operations in midland regions is, conversely, evidence that many cases were summarily executed on the spot rather than through orderly relocation to coastal areas or detention. Meanwhile, the large number of residents from Cheju-up and coastal villages is seen as resulting from sustained surveillance and arrests by military intelligence personnel, police, and the Sŏbuk Youth Corps. While the military led operations in midland regions, police conducted arrests of those suspected of communicating with armed guerrillas or those who had previously participated and been active in leftist organizations. The large-scale arrest of prominent figures in Cheju-up was carried out in these circumstances. The fact that the "1948 military courts-martial" defendants included many with occupations such as civil servants, teachers, company employees, and transportation workers suggests an intention to preemptively sever possible connections with armed guerrillas through preventive detention of these occupational groups.

The "1948 military courts-martial" appears to have been established as a means of preventive detention of residents in midland regions who escaped collective mass killing following evacuation operations, residents of Cheju-up and coastal regions, and employees in various occupations who might possibly be connected with armed guerrillas. Moreover, because extraordinary martial law was in effect from November 17 to December 31, 1948, forced detention and military courts-martial of civilians were possible. These military court-martial defendants would, had it not been for martial law circumstances, have been released after investigation or referred for ordinary civilian trial, following normal legal procedures. The forced application of military courts-martial to these persons, together with field summary executions carried out at the time without legal procedures, reveals another reality of victimization in the Jeju 4·3 Incident.

② The "1949 Military Courts-Martial"

On March 2, 1949, the government established the Jeju Island District Combat Headquarters (Commander Colonel Yu Jae-hung) to quell the Jeju situation before the May 10 reelection. Upon his arrival, Yu Jae-hung sent the Second Regiment, previously stationed in coastal areas, directly against the armed guerrillas, moving them to the mountains. Consequently, the Second Regiment's First Battalion was stationed at "Benddichimbat" (near Suak Bridge), the Second Battalion at Kwanŭm Temple, the Third Battalion at Kyorye-ri, and special units near Noru Peak. Yu Jae-hung optimistically projected that he would completely sweep out the "bandits" by mid-April. He planned to "form a line with military units and Civil Defense Units (Minbodan) completely traversing the island, sweep the mountains like raking them, drive the bandits to the opposite side of the island, and push them toward the coast where police are stationed."[^153]

On the other hand, he adopted an amnesty plan, halting indiscriminate killing of midland residents during operations. "Regardless of whether they were arrested during operations or voluntarily surrendered, all persons coming down from the mountain were detained."[^154] From early March, pamphlets encouraging surrender were distributed to residents hiding in the mountains. At that time, approximately 20,000 civilian residents from midland regions were hidden in Hallasan, in addition to armed guerrillas.[^155] These were people who had fled to survive after evading the Ninth Regiment's forced evacuation operations. Residents of Hannam-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, climbed to "Kornaennidoen-pat" (high-altitude fields) on Hallasan. Over 90 percent of the village's approximately 400 residents collectively went up and, as families, built makeshift shelters and lived in hiding.[^156] Residents of Nohyŏng-ri, Cheju-up, fled to a deep location called "Chŏkŭn-dreo-rum's 'Ch'ŏngsan'ido," far up Hallasan.[^157] In these villages, residents collectively prepared hiding places on Hallasan and lived in refuge. Kim Saeng-min, who was serving as a political officer for the South Korean Workers' Party's Jeju Island branch while in hiding on Hallasan and held responsibility for controlling hidden residents, testified that "thousands of elderly, children, and women from the Mujŏk, Sŏngsan, and Namwŏn-myŏn areas of South Cheju County were hidden at Mul-cat Peak."[^158]

**[p. 450]**

Following these persuasion operations, many mountain-refuge residents came down successively. Approximately 1,500 came down in the month of March alone. According to a report given to the United Nations Commission on Korea team visiting Cheju on May 11 by Commander Yu Jae-hung, 3,600 prisoners were captured between March 25 and April 12.[^159] By April 21, "prisoners and surrenders" had surged to 5,817.[^160] According to the announcement of operational results from Vice-Minister of Home Affairs on May 22, from March 5 to May 14, the total number of surrenders was 6,014, comprising 2,974 males and 3,040 females; released persons numbered 4,163, and current detainees numbered 1,851.[^161] All of these were confined in holding facilities in Cheju-up and Sŏgwipo. At the time, the detention locations for those coming down were, in Cheju-up, the alcohol distillery warehouse (Oriental Development Company) being the largest, with others including the Agricultural School, Ildo-ri Public Hall, and Yongdam-ri holding facility dispersed among them. In Sŏgwipo, the Chŏngbang Waterfall above the sweet potato warehouse and storage facilities near Ch'ŏnji Yŏn served as holding facilities. In a visit to the alcohol distillery on May 11, the United Nations Commission on Korea's inspection report noted:

> Approximately 2,000 detainees were found living in an old warehouse in the holding facility. The number of women was roughly three times that of men, and there were many babies in arms and children. The detention facility superintendent stated that 90 percent of the detainees had surrendered after hiding in the mountains, while the remainder had been captured by military suppression forces.[^162]

Surveillance police established separate investigation squad offices at the alcohol distillery and conducted interrogations. A police officer who served as special investigation squad section chief at the time and conducted interrogations at the alcohol distillery testified as follows:

> When we tortured, we tried to avoid it because they were people from our hometown. Police from the mainland engaged in much torture. There were various torture methods; some police stripped women completely and hung them from the ceiling to torture them.[^163]

Police inflicted all manner of torture on detainees, forcing them to confess to connections with armed guerrillas and extracting statements, which were then turned over to the military.[^164] At the time, an alcohol distillery employee testified as follows: "Hundreds of people were confined in a sweet potato storage room on a hill in the alcohol distillery. I saw people from the same village; they were simply arrested while living in the village—there was not a single person one could call a 'rioter.' Military personnel and police occasionally came and went from the holding facility, but most security was handled by members of the Korean Youth Corps."[^165]

**[p. 451]**

The "1949 military courts-martial" is recorded as having been held for civilians from the high mountain areas who had descended from hiding on Hallasan and were being confined in various holding facilities throughout Jeju. According to the military courts-martial orders, the "1949 military courts-martial" was convened a total of 10 times from June 23 to July 7, 1949, ostensibly as high military courts-martial.[^166] At this military courts-martial, 1,659 civilians were uniformly found guilty of violating Articles 32 and 33 of the National Defense Security Law for the crimes of "supplying information and communicating with the enemy and espionage."

The military courts-martial orders contain High Military Courts-Martial Orders No. 1 through No. 18, with appended rosters for each order number. The content of Order No. 1 is as follows:

---

**High Military Courts-Martial Order No. 1**

July 3, 4282 (1949)
Commander, Second Regiment, Infantry Division
Colonel Ham Byŏng-sŏn [Seal]

1. Establishment Order: Pursuant to Army Headquarters Second Regiment Headquarters Special Order No. 71, dated June 1, 4282 (1949), Section 1

2. Trial Location: Cheju District Court, Jeju Island

3. Defendants: Per attached roster

4. Charges and Criminal Facts: Violation of National Defense Security Law Articles 32 and 33; Crimes of supplying information and communicating with the enemy and espionage

5. Pleas: See attached roster, Section 3

6. Verdict: Same

7. Judgment: Same

8. Date of Sentencing: Same

9. Action by Reviewing Officer: Approved the verdicts and ordered appropriate execution.

10. Action by Confirming Officer: [blank]

11. Custodial Facilities: Prisons

---

**[p. 452]**

According to the documents, among the "1949 military courts-martial" 1,659 defendants, 345 (20.8%) were sentenced to death and 238 (14.3%) to life imprisonment, with the remainder sentenced to: 308 (18.6%) to 15 years, 706 (42.6%) to 7 years, 13 to 5 years, 25 to 3 years, 22 to 1 year, and 2 unconfirmed. Overall, the "1949 military courts-martial" handled cases more severely than the "1948 military courts-martial" under martial law. Among the 345 persons recorded as sentenced to death, executions by firing squad of 249 persons took place on October 2, 1949. No official execution order has been located for this. However, the U.S. records contain the following related information:

> On October 2, 1949, at 9:00 a.m., 249 armed guerrillas detained at Cheju Police Station were executed at the Cheju Airfield coastline with presidential approval. The execution officer was Major Jo Yŏng-gu, chief of the Cheju Military Police. Witnessing officers included the battalion intelligence officer, legal officer, and other battalion officers. Among those executed were one Ninth Regiment officer and five non-commissioned officers. Also included were 15 additional soldiers who had defected from the Ninth Regiment, which had been stationed on Jeju Island previously (in 1948), and joined Jeju's armed guerrillas.[^167]

Of the 345 persons recorded as sentenced to death, 249 were executed by firing squad, while the remainder were commuted during the confirming officer's confirmation process or reconsideration and escaped execution. Confirmation that persons such as Kang Tu-sam, Pu Ŭl-saeng, Kim Nam-hae, Hŏ Nam-hong, Kim T'ae-kyŏng, Kang Wŏn-gi, Hong Ch'ŏn-p'yo, Yang Kyŏng-t'aek, and Pu Sŏng-u—all recorded as sentenced to death—were detained in Mapo Prison serving life sentences establishes this.[^168] Similarly, Kim Min-hak, recorded as sentenced to death, was held in Mapo Prison, released, returned to his hometown to live, and died there 15 years ago.[^169]

Hŏ Kyun, a first independent battalion platoon commander who directly witnessed the execution, testified to the circumstances of the scene as follows:

> My company was stationed at Cheju Airfield, and one day I witnessed the execution of the bandits in the open field of Cheju Airfield. Approximately 200–300 persons were executed by firing squad with their eyes bound, and when the graves in which they were buried were examined somewhat later, [

**[p. 453]**

The execution took place on February 27, 1949, at 2:30 p.m. in the vicinity of Hwabuk-ri, Jeju-ŭp. The relevant command was as follows.

Army Headquarters 2nd Regiment Headquarters High Military Court Order No. 1

○ Pursuant to Cheju District Military Government High Military Court Order No. 20 dated December 29, 1948, the following execution of death sentences for convicts is commanded:

1. Convicts to be executed: Kim Yun-taek and 38 others (as listed in attached schedule)
2. Date of execution: Danki year 4282, February 27, at 2:30 p.m.
3. Place of execution: As designated by the execution officer at a location in the vicinity of Hwabuk-ri, Jeju-ŭp, North Cheju County, Cheju Island
4. Officers executing death sentence: Army Captain Cho Young-gu, Army Second Lieutenant Jung Se-jin
5. Witnessing officers: Military physician, Army First Lieutenant Park Shin-bŏm; Intelligence officer, Army Second Lieutenant Park Tae-wŏn
6. Matters concerning execution method and other details not specified in this order shall be conducted in accordance with the basic execution procedures for military death sentences

Danki year 4282, February 22
Army Headquarters 2nd Regiment
Regiment Commander, Army Lieutenant Colonel Ham Byung-sŏn (seal)

The date of execution is known, but the place of execution was stated only as "in the vicinity of Hwabuk-ri," making it difficult to confirm the exact location. Since they were shot and buried secretly, the bereaved families did not know the location at the time. Some bereaved families heard it was "between Hwabuk and Samyang,"[^1] "mountain foothills near Hwabuk,"[^2] "roadside between Hwabuk and Samyang,"[^3] "field west of Hwabuk-ri entrance,"[^4] "field on the Hwabuk roundabout road,"[^5] and so forth. On August 12, 2002, the on-site testimony investigation team of the Cheju 4·3 Incident Committee met with a witness who had directly recovered the remains of his father, Kang Yun-kyu, from the shooting site and confirmed the location directly. Witness Kang Il-su heard about the burial place from a relative working in the police and, one month later, went directly to the site with his uncle. His father had two carbine bullets lodged in his abdomen, and the bullets were confirmed. The remains were placed on a pushcart and buried in the Dodu-ri graveyard in Jeju-ŭp. The location was a field to the left immediately upon entering the Dongjewŏn entrance on the right side of the main gate of O-hyŏn High School in eastern Hwabuk, Jeju City, and it currently remains as an empty lot.[^6]

[^1]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kim Hang-yun.
[^2]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kang Sŏng-bo.
[^3]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Jin Kyŏng-man.
[^4]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kang Yun-kyu.
[^5]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kim Pong-su.
[^6]: Kang Il-su (age 63, Ildo 2-dong, Jeju City, elementary school student at the time, recorded August 12, 2002), testimony.

**[p. 454]**

The 871 individuals listed in the military court order who were subject to court-martial proceedings in Cheju, excepting the 38 sentenced to death, were scattered and imprisoned in penitentiaries in Mokpo, Mapo, Sŏdaemun, Taegu, Inchŏn, and Chŏnju. For the 67 individuals sentenced to life imprisonment, the penitentiary where they would serve their sentences was not specified in the military court order, so the circumstances of their imprisonment are unknown. However, records show that 24 of these individuals were transferred to the Taegu Penitentiary on March 4, 1949, according to the 1949 list of inmates at the Taegu Penitentiary. Thus, it is estimated that these 67 individuals were held in the Mokpo Penitentiary for approximately two months before being transferred to the Taegu Penitentiary and other facilities. The 97 individuals listed as sentenced to 20 years imprisonment also do not have their penitentiary assignments clearly indicated in the military court order. However, examination of the 1949 list of inmates at the Mapo Penitentiary confirms that most of them were imprisoned there.

A total of 453 individuals are listed in the military court order with Mokpo Penitentiary as their place of imprisonment. These consist of 202 sentenced to 15 years, 157 to 5 years, 4 to 3 years, and 90 to 1 year. However, the 202 individuals sentenced to 15 years were transferred from the Mokpo Penitentiary to the Taegu Penitentiary shortly after their arrival, as records from the 1948–1949 Taegu Penitentiary inmate lists show them as being held there.

Youths aged nineteen and under were designated for imprisonment at the Inchŏn Penitentiary: 56 sentenced to 15 years, 58 to 5 years, 52 to 1 year, for a total of 166 individuals. Women were imprisoned at the Chŏnju Penitentiary: 3 sentenced to 15 years, 7 to 5 years, 38 to 1 year, for a total of 48 individuals.

Analysis of the regional distribution of individuals listed in the "1948 Military Court" proceedings in the military court order shows that residents of the midaltitude villages constituted a large proportion overall. However, the number of residents from villages in Jeju-ŭp itself—such as Kŏnip, Yongdam, and Ido-ri—and from coastal villages such as Todu, Hwabuk, Aewŏl, Ha-kwie, Sinch'ŏn, and Choch'ŏn, and from Kangjŏng-ri, was not negligible. Given that intensive relocation operations targeting midaltitude regions were carried out after October 1948, the regional distribution of military court proceedings subjects indicates that the relocation operations in the midaltitude regions were not conducted smoothly.

The residents of Kumdog-ri, Aewŏl-myŏn, a midaltitude village, were exceptional in that a considerable number were captured by military forces and 25 of them were brought before the "1948 Military Court"; however, residents in other areas did not comply with the military's relocation operations. This corroborates the fact that forced relocation operations and the inducement of armed guerrillas to take to the mountains resulted in even more residents fleeing to the hills. Among the residents of Sŏnhŭl-ri, Choch'ŏn-myŏn, not a single individual appears in military court proceedings; instead, on November 25–26, 1948, they hid in caves while military, police, and suppression forces surrounded the cave and collectively massacred 94 residents. The fact that the number of persons subject to legal proceedings was small during this period when major relocation operations were being conducted in the midaltitude regions suggests instead that many instances of summary execution at the scene occurred without smooth relocation or detention in coastal villages.

Meanwhile, the reason for the large number of residents from coastal villages, including those in Jeju-ŭp proper, appears to be a result of sustained surveillance and arrests by military intelligence personnel, police, and members of the Northwest Youth Association. While the military led operations in the midaltitude regions, the police conducted arrests of those suspected of collusion with armed guerrillas or who had previously participated in and engaged in leftist organizations. Large-scale arrests of prominent citizens in Jeju-ŭp were carried out in this context. The fact that the occupational composition of "1948 Military Court" defendants includes many civil servants, teachers, company employees, and transportation workers appears to reflect an intention to sever possible connections with armed guerrillas through preventive detention of these groups.

The "1948 Military Court" appears to have been established as a measure of preventive detention, in the context of the military and police's shift to hardline policies after October 1948 and the subsequent relocation operations in the midaltitude regions, targeting residents who escaped collective massacres, residents of Jeju-ŭp proper with possible connections to armed guerrillas, and residents of coastal areas. Moreover, because martial law was declared from November 17 through December 31, 1948, forced detention and the execution of military courts against civilians became possible. Had the situation not been under martial law, these military court subjects would have been released after investigation or referred to ordinary civilian courts and would have followed normal legal procedures. The forceful application of military courts to these individuals, together with on-scene summary executions without legal procedure at the time, reveals another dimension of the victimization in the Jeju 4·3 Incident.

② "1949 Military Court"

On March 2, 1949, the government established the Jeju Island Combat Command (Commander Colonel Yu Jae-hung) in order to bring peace to the Jeju situation before the 5·10 re-election. Upon taking office, Yu Jae-hung dispatched the 2nd Regiment, which had been stationed in coastal areas, to take direct positions against armed guerrillas in the mountains. Accordingly, the 1st Battalion of the 2nd Regiment was stationed near "Bendichibat" (Suak Bridge vicinity), the 2nd Battalion at Kwaneumsa, the 3rd Battalion at Kyorrae-ri, and special forces units near Noru Oreum. Yu Jae-hung optimistically projected that the "bandits" would be completely subdued by mid-April. He intended to "form a line completely traversing the island with military units and militia, sweep the mountains as if combing them, position the bandits on the opposite side of the island, and drive them toward the police side."[^7]

At the same time, he adopted an amnesty plan to halt indiscriminate killing of midaltitude region residents. He implemented operations that involved "detaining all people coming down from the mountains, whether captured during operations, voluntarily surrendering, or otherwise."[^8] From early March, leaflets encouraging surrender were distributed to residents hiding in the mountains.

On Halla Mountain at that time were not only armed guerrillas but also approximately 20,000 civilian residents from midaltitude regions who had fled for survival. These were people who had sought refuge to escape the forced relocation operations of the 9th Regiment. Residents of Hannam-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, fled all the way up to a place called "Konnaeida field" in Halla Mountain. More than 90 percent of Hannam-ri's approximately 400 residents collectively ascended and pitched temporary wooden shelters in family units to live in hiding.[^9] Residents of Nohyŏng-ri, Jeju-ŭp, fled deep into the mountains to a place called "Chŏgŭn Tulae Oreum south Chŏngsan-i-do."[^10] In the cases of these villages, residents collectively established hideouts on Halla Mountain and lived in refugee status. Kim Saeng-min, who at the time served as a political section member of the South Chosŏn Labor Party's Jeju Provincial Committee and supervised those who had fled to Halla Mountain, testified that "thousands of elderly, children, and women from the Sŏngsan, Namwŏn-myŏn, and surrounding areas of South Cheju County were hidden on Mul-chat Oreum."[^11]

As a result of these pacification operations, many mountain refugees came down successively. In just one month of March, about 1,500 came down. According to a report by Commander Yu Jae-hung to the United Nations Commission on Korea visiting Jeju on May 11, 3,600 prisoners had been captured between March 25 and April 12.[^12] By April 21, "prisoners and voluntary surrender personnel" had surged to 5,817.[^13] According to the announcement of operational results by the Vice Minister of the Interior on May 22: "The total number of those who surrendered from March 5 to May 14 was 6,014, of which 2,974 were men and 3,040 were women; the number released was 4,163, and the current number detained was 1,851."[^14] All of these individuals were confined in holding facilities in Jeju-ŭp and Sŏgwipo. The detention locations at the time were, in Jeju-ŭp proper, the alcohol factory warehouse (Oriental Development Company) being the largest, with additional dispersed locations including an agricultural school, Ildo-ri public hall, and the Yongdam-ri holding facility. In Sŏgwipo, the warehouse above Chŏngbang Waterfall and the warehouse near Chŏnji Pond were utilized as holding facilities.

In their inspection report of May 11 visit to the alcohol factory, the United Nations Commission on Korea noted the following:

The holding facility had some 2,000 detainees living in an old warehouse. The number of women was roughly three times that of men, and there were many infants in arms and children. The facility director stated that 90 percent of the detainees had surrendered after hiding in the mountains, while the remainder had been captured by military suppression forces.[^15]

Police surveillance personnel established separate interrogation offices in the alcohol factory for investigating these individuals. A police officer who served as the chief of the special investigation unit at the time and conducted interrogations at the alcohol factory testified as follows:

When we tortured, we tried to avoid it since they were fellow countrymen. Police who came from the mainland did a lot of torturing. There were various torture methods—some police would strip women completely naked, hang them from the ceiling, and torture them.[^16]

Police subjected detainees to all manner of torture to force false confessions of connections with armed guerrillas and prepared statements that they delivered to the military.[^17] A staff member at the alcohol factory testified: "Several hundred people were detained in a sweet potato storage shed on the hill above the alcohol factory. I saw people from the same village—they were just living in the village before being arrested; there was no one who could be called a 'bandit.' Military personnel and police occasionally came to the facility, but security was mainly handled by members of the Korean Youth Association."[^18]

**[p. 455]**

The "1949 Military Court" was held, as described above, targeting civilians who had come down from Halla Mountain and were confined in various holding facilities throughout Jeju. According to the military court order, the "1949 Military Court" was convened a total of ten times from June 23 to July 7, 1949, nominally as a "high military court."[^19] In these military court proceedings, 1,659 civilians were all without exception convicted, sentenced with violations of Article 32–33 of the National Defense Security Act, the charge being "communication with and espionage for the enemy."[^20] The military court order contains high military court orders numbered 1 through 18, with attached schedules accompanying each order number. The content of Order No. 1 is as follows:

High Military Court Order No. 1
Danki Year 4282, July 3
Capital Defense Command Infantry 2nd Regiment
Regiment Commander, Army Colonel Ham Byung-sŏn (seal)

1. Installation order: In accordance with Army Headquarters Infantry 2nd Regiment Special Order No. 71, dated June 1, Danki year 4282, item 1
2. Court location: Jeju District Court, Jeju Island
3. Defendants: As listed in attached schedule
4. Charges
   Crime: Violation of Article 32–33, National Defense Security Act
   Criminal facts: Communication with and espionage for the enemy
5. Defense: See attached schedule, item 3
6. Determination: Same as above
7. Verdict: Same as above
8. Date of verdict pronouncement: Same as above
9. Review officer's actions
   Approves this verdict and orders appropriate execution
10. Confirmation officer's actions
11. Place of imprisonment: Penitentiary

**[p. 456]**

This was a major military court operation connected to the massive relocation operations of the 9th Regiment. However, this period marked a significant shift in policy, as the commander Yu Jae-hung adopted an amnesty plan to halt indiscriminate killing, and individuals who came down from the mountains were detained rather than executed on the spot. The vast numbers processed through military courts reflected the scale of the crisis but also the increasing judicialization of the suppression effort.

[^8]: Ibid.

At the end of June, 2nd Regiment Commander Ham Byung-sŏn told reporters, "We have obtained 8,000 voluntary surrender personnel, and the number of those who surrendered remaining in the holding facilities is no more than 2,000" (Kyŏnghyang Sinmun, June 29, 1949).

**[p. 457]**

They were imprisoned in detention facilities in Cheju Town and Sŏgwipo. The detention locations for those who had surrendered at the time were scattered: in the case of Cheju Town, the largest was the liquor distillery warehouse (Tongch'ŏk Company), but others included the Agricultural School, Il-do-ri Public Hall, and Yongdam-ri detention facility. In Sŏgwipo, the warehouse near Chŏngbang Falls and the facility near Ch'ŏnji Pond were utilized as detention centers.

According to the inspection report of the United Nations Korean Commission that visited the liquor distillery on May 11, the following was noted:

The detention facility was found to have approximately 2,000 detainees living in old warehouses. The number of women was roughly three times greater than men, and there were many infants and small children held in mothers' arms. The facility commander stated that 90% of the detainees had surrendered after hiding in the mountains, and the remainder had been arrested by military suppression forces.[^162]

Police officers in charge of surveillance established a separate investigation office at the liquor distillery and conducted interrogations. The police officer who was serving as head of the special investigation unit and conducting interrogations at the liquor distillery testified as follows:

When we tortured, we tried to avoid it because they were people from our hometown. The police who came from the mainland tortured a lot. There were various torture methods—some police stripped women completely naked, hung them from the ceiling, and tortured them.[^163]

The police subjected detainees to all kinds of torture, forcibly extracting confessions of connections with armed units and creating statements which they passed on to the military.[^164] A distillery employee at the time testified: "Hundreds of people were detained in a storage shed for sweet potatoes called 'chŏlgan gŏguma' on the ridge above the distillery. I saw people from the same village—they were just arrested while living in the village; there was no one you could call a 'rebel.' Military personnel and police occasionally came and went from the detention facility, but security was mainly handled by members of the Korean Youth Corps."[^165]

News, June 4, 1949; Chosŏn Central Daily News, June 28, 1946). By late June, Major Sŏnggŭm of the 2nd Regiment stated to journalists, "We have secured 8,000 surrendering persons, and the number of surrendering persons remaining in the detention facility amounts to no more than 2,000" (Kyŏnghyang News, June 29, 1949).

**[p. 458]**

The "1949 Military Court Martial" was held targeting civilians who, as described above, had been hiding in Hallasan and then surrendered, and were subsequently detained in various detention facilities throughout Cheju Island. According to the Military Court Martial Directives, the "1949 Military Court Martial" was held a total of ten times from June 23 to July 7, 1949, and its ostensible purpose was a higher military court martial.[^166] At this military court martial, 1,659 civilians were convicted without exception on charges of violating Articles 32-33 of the National Defense Security Law—"providing aid and communication to the enemy and espionage"—and handed down guilty verdicts. The Military Court Martial Directives contain Higher Military Court Martial Directives Nos. 1 through 18, and each directive number is accompanied by an attached roster. The content of Directive No. 1 is as follows:

**Higher Military Court Martial Directive No. 1**

Tanki 4282, July 3
Capital Defense Command, Infantry 2nd Regiment
Regiment Commander, Army Colonel Ham Byŏng-sŏn (Official Seal)

1. Establishment Order: In accordance with Item 1 of Special Order No. 71 from the Infantry 2nd Regiment Headquarters, Army General Headquarters, dated the 1st of Tanki 4282, June.

2. Courtroom Location: Cheju District Court, Cheju Island

3. Defendants: As listed in attached roster

4. Charges
   Crime: Violation of Articles 32-33 of the National Defense Security Law
   Criminal Facts: Providing aid to the enemy, communication with and espionage for the enemy

5. Objections: See roster in Item 3

6. Judgment: Same as above

7. Verdict: Same as above

8. Date of Verdict Pronouncement: Same as above

9. Actions by Reviewing Official
   Approves the verdict and orders appropriate execution

10. Actions by Confirming Official

11. Place of Sentence Execution: Prison

Indicted: 1,652 civilians, 47 military personnel. Verdicts handed down: 345 death sentences, 238 life sentences, 311 sentences of 15 years, 705 sentences of 7 years or less, 54 acquittals, 46 releases." (U.S. Far East Command Joint Weekly Summary and Analysis No. 9, August 12, 1949).

[^140]: Fourth National Assembly Report on Investigation of Civilian Massacre Incident, Reported Case from Kŭmdŏk-ri, Aewŏl-myŏn, North Cheju County, 1960; Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report Reception No. 88, Kang Hu-sŏn.

[^141]: Choe Kil-du (age 85, Samdo 1-dong, Cheju-si, government official at the time, recorded June 22, 2001) testimony.

[^142]: Ahn Tu-wŏn (age 75, Samdo 1-dong, Cheju-si, Cheju Daily News public affairs staff at the time, recorded April 2, 2002) testimony.

[^143]: RG 338: Records of U.S. Army Commands, Entry: Provisional Military Advisory Group (1948–1949) and Korean Military Advisory Group (1949–1953), Box 4. NARA.

[^144]: U.S. records contain the following description of the "1948 military courts-martial": "Military courts-martial were held during this period to try all leftist elements arrested by the Defense Headquarters and police. So far, more than 700 have been tried. Of these, 96 have been sentenced to death and 330 have received various prison sentences. Those receiving prison sentences are to be detained at Mokpo Prison. Of the 96 sentenced to death, 23 were Defense Headquarters personnel charged with aiding the enemy and instigating. Trials are still in progress, with approximately 170 awaiting trial." (Dispatch No. 11, with Attached Document No. 1, January 7, 1949, from the U.S. Embassy in Korea to the Secretary of State, marked "Urgent.")

[^145]: The military courts-martial orders contain military courts-martial order No. 20, recorded as having determined sentence reductions dated December 29, 4281 (1948). However, the attached rosters show earlier dates—December 3, 4, 5, 7, and 8—for verdict pronouncements. If existing verdict dates were recorded in the appended rosters, existing sentence amounts should have been recorded as well; however, the final commuted sentence amounts are recorded instead, which is inexplicable. Moreover, these commutations were never communicated to the defendants or their custodial facilities. For example, in the case of Kim Ch'un-bae detained at Mapo Prison, the military courts-martial orders show a 20-year commutation, but the 1949 Mapo Prison detainee roster records him as "perpetual detention." In the case of Kang Sun-hyŏn, likewise reduced to 20 years, he himself believed he was serving a life sentence when detained at Sŏdaemun Prison. (Testimony of Kang Sun-hyŏn [Yongdam 2-dong, Cheju-si, property management office employee at the time], Chemin Daily News 4·3 Investigation Team, "4·3 Speaks," IV, p. 167.) This passage reveals problems in contemporary law enforcement.

[^146]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kim Hang-yun.

[^147]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kang Sŏng-bo.

[^148]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Jin Kyŏng-man.

[^149]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kang Yun-gyŏ.

[^150]: Jeju 4·3 Incident Victim Report, Kim Pong-su.

[^151]: Kang Il-su (age 63, Ildo 2-dong, Cheju-si, elementary school student at the time, recorded August 12, 2002) testimony.

[^152]: See Park Ch'an-sik, "The Reality of Mass Killings of Jeju 4·3-Related Convicts During the Korean War," 4·3 and History, First Issue, Jeju 4·3 Research Institute, 2001, pp. 50–51.

[^153]: Hq. USAFIK, G-2 Periodic Report, No. 1097, April 1, 1949.

[^154]: Ibid.

[^155]: Yu Jae-hung (age 82, Nonhyŏn-dong, Kangnam-gu, Seoul, Commander of Jeju Island District Combat Headquarters at the time, recorded September 6, 2002) testimony.

[^156]: Ko Pong-wŏn (age 72, Hannam-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, ordinary resident at the time, recorded December 14, 2000) testimony.

[^157]: Hyŏn Sang-ji (age 71, Yŏn-dong, Cheju-si, mountain refuge resident at the time, recorded July 27, 2001) testimony.

[^158]: Kim Saeng-min (age 77, Yŏn-dong, Cheju-si, South Korean Workers' Party Jeju Island branch cadre at the time, recorded July 13, 2001) testimony.

[^159]: Transmitting Excerpt from UNCOK Report on Visit to Cheju Island, Despatch No. 358, June 17, 1949, American Embassy, Korea.

[^160]: Kyunghyang Daily News, April 26, 1949.

[^161]: Kyunghyang Daily News, May 22, 1949. After June 1949, newspapers reported that the total number of surrenders was some 8,000, of which some 5,000 were released and some 3,000 remained in detention (Chosun Daily News, June 4, 1949; Chosun Central Daily News, June 28, 1946). On June 29, 1949, Second Regiment Commander Ham Byŏng-sŏn told reporters: "We have obtained 8,000 surrenders, and only 2,000 surrenders remain in the holding facility" (Kyunghyang Daily News, June 29, 1949).

[^162]: Transmitting Excerpt from UNCOK Report on Visit to Cheju Island, Despatch No. 358, June 17, 1949, American Embassy, Korea.

[^163]: Kim Pyŏng-taek (age 77, Yongdam 1-dong, Cheju-si, police officer at the time, recorded February 8, 2002) testimony.

[^164]: According to testimony from those who survived imprisonment, all consistently point to severe police torture.

[^165]: Kang Tong-hwa (age 81, Samdo 1-dong, Cheju-si, alcohol distillery employee at the time, recorded July 18, 2002) testimony.

[^166]: U.S. records contain the following description of the "1949 military courts-martial": "From June 3 to July 12, 1949, trial results for communists and sympathizers on Jeju Island were as follows: Indicted: 1,652 civilians, 47 military personnel. Sentenced: 345 to death, 238 to life imprisonment, 311 to 15 years, 705 to 7 years or less, 54 acquitted, 46 released." (U.S. Far East Command September Weekly Intelligence Analysis No. 9, August 12, 1949).

[^167]: KMAG, G-2 Periodic Report, No. 192, October 6, 1949; Joint Weekly, No. 17, September 30 – October 7, 1949.

[^168]: These detainees' names are confirmed in 1950 Mapo Prison detainee rosters and Mapo Prison prisoner identification records.

[^169]: Jeju 4·3 Research Institute, "Now We Speak," Vol. I, Hanul Publishing, 1989, pp. 47–48.

**[p. 460]**

I remember being sat down. The field command was conducted by Military Police Captain Yun Ki-yŏl. (omitted) I heard that the 2nd Regiment conducted suppression operations without mercy, and my heart ached seeing many people crammed into the narrow confines of the police station detention room. I witnessed executions at the Jeju airfield—it would have truly been difficult to distinguish the worthy from the unworthy at that time.[^170] The bereaved families of 249 executed victims do not know the concrete locations and dates of the deaths. Some bereaved families have only heard rumors that on October 2, 1949, they were executed by gunfire near the Jeju town airfield and then buried in pits. Currently, not a single person among these bereaved families has recovered the bodies. The 1,659 military court-martial subjects listed in the military court-martial orders, excluding 249 executed on Jeju Island, were dispersed and imprisoned in the Mapo, Daegu, Daejeon, Mokpo, Incheon, and Jeonju prisons respectively. Of those with Mapo Prison listed as the place of confinement in the military court-martial orders, 223 people were all sentenced to life imprisonment. To this, 96 people whose death sentences were commuted to life imprisonment and transferred to Mapo Prison should be added, bringing the total number of inmates in Mapo Prison to 319. In Daegu Prison, 297 people were imprisoned with 15-year sentences; in Daejeon Prison, 300 people with 7-year sentences; and in Mokpo Prison, 215 people with 7-year sentences. Young people under age nineteen were confined in Incheon Prison with place of confinement listed, numbering 12 with life sentences, 12 with 15-year sentences, 170 with 7-year sentences, totaling 194 people. Women were confined in Jeonju Prison, totaling 84 people: 3 with life sentences, 21 with 7-year sentences, 13 with 5-year sentences, 25 with 3-year sentences, and 22 with 1-year sentences.

Looking at the regional distribution of the '1949 military court-martial' subjects listed in the military court-martial orders, those from Jeju town proper or coastal villages are scarcely visible, while the proportion of residents from mountainous villages is overwhelming. Villages such as Ora, Sŏnhŭl, Wasan, Myŏngwol, Ŭigwi, Hannam, Sumang, Kasi, Nansan, and Sinpyŏngri had almost no records from the '1948 military court-martial,' but the '1949 military court-martial' subjects numbered dozens. These are representative villages situated in the mountainous regions, and it appears that when strong suppression operations were carried out after October 1948, the residents collectively fled into the mountains as a result. After March 1949, when propaganda operations were implemented, they descended in large numbers and became subjects of military court-martial proceedings. The military court-martial subjects at that time, aside from some armed guerrillas, were ordinary residents who had wandered through the mountains after October 1948 to preserve their lives by avoiding the fierce suppression of the military and police.[^170]

[^170]: Testimony of Hŏ Kyun (age 75, Chamwon-dong, Seocho-gu, Seoul; platoon leader, 1st Independent Battalion at the time; recorded Sept. 27, 2001).

**[p. 461]**

These were civilians who complied with the suppression forces' propaganda operations policy of "come down and you will be spared." However, the government ignored the amnesty policy toward them and consistently pursued a hard-line approach. The amnesty policy of the military authorities at that time was limited, stipulating that "women, children, and the elderly be classified as refugees, while men of combatant age be thoroughly searched and educated before refugee status is granted."[^171] Any youth or young adult from the mountainous regions aged fifteen or older who had taken refuge in the mountains and then came down was, without exception, branded as a "rebel" or "bandit," investigated, and made subject to military court-martial. The government and military authorities, rather than executing these people summarily as before, sought to manage them separately; however, since Jeju Island lacked prisons and collective detention facilities, they wished to isolate them from Jeju Island by confining them to prisons throughout the country. The only means available at the time to send more than two thousand people to prisons at once was through application of Articles 32–33 of the National Defense Security Act. Ultimately, the subjects of the '1949 military court-martial' were people sent to prisons and lost by the government and military authorities seeking to rapidly suppress the Jeju 4·3 Incident.

3) Legal Review of Military Court-Martial Proceedings

Hearing from survivors of the military court-martial subjects who were imprisoned in prisons and miraculously survived to return and remain alive today regarding the actual state of the military court-martial proceedings that are said to have been held at that time, one can see that there were many problems in the procedures. Survivors testify that they were imprisoned without receiving a trial or that the proceedings were conducted formally without legal procedures. If the military court-martial did not exist at all or if it was a formal trial in violation of legal procedures, then calling the military court-martial subjects "convicts" is also problematic, and their confinement in prisons should be viewed as "unlawful detention." This is not only a critical investigative matter in the victim selection review of the 1,443 military court-martial convicts reported as victims according to the current Jeju 4·3 Special Act, but also an important matter in ascertaining the truth regarding the legal application of state power during the Jeju 4·3 Incident, and therefore must be investigated and reviewed.[^171][^172]

[^171]: Hq. USAFIK, G-2 Periodic Report, No. 1097, April 1, 1949.

[^172]: The Jeju 4·3 Incident Committee's Victim Selection Subcommittee (Chair: Pak Chae-sŭng) at its 16th meeting (Oct. 25, 2002) heard a report on the factual investigation results regarding 'military court-martial proceedings' from the committee's legal specialist. This factual investigation report was prepared while sharing various materials and analyses collected and analyzed by the Committee's Truth Investigation Planning Division during the truth investigation process. Therefore, it should be noted that the legal review of military court-martial proceedings was based substantially on this factual investigation report.

**[p. 462]**

Accordingly, the Jeju 4·3 Incident Committee conducted testimony investigations of the parties who had been imprisoned in prisons regarding the military court-martial proceedings and related matters during the Jeju 4·3 Incident. Moreover, testimony investigations were conducted in parallel with military and police officials involved in the military court-martial at that time. Documentary materials included legal records of military court-martial proceedings, police documents, prison records, National Assembly minutes, government records, newspaper articles, and U.S. materials, which were all comprehensively reviewed. Furthermore, while reviewing these materials, attention was paid to whether the relevant provisions of the National Defense Security Act, which stipulated the procedures for military court-martial proceedings during the Jeju 4·3 Incident, were observed.

① Testimony Investigations

As a result of testimony investigations of survivors who had been confined in prisons—Kang Sang-mun and 29 others—the content was classified into three categories. First, cases where persons underwent interrogation by military and police, followed by imprisonment without any trial proceedings, and then were notified of charges and sentences while imprisoned.[^173] Second, cases where persons underwent interrogation by military and police, were summoned in groups to the courtroom where they were called by name, and then after transfer to prison were notified of charges and sentences while imprisoned.[^174] Third, cases where persons underwent interrogation by military and police, were summoned in groups to the courtroom where they were called by name, were given sentence pronouncements, and then were imprisoned.[^175] Comprehensively, survivors consistently testified to the absence of trials and formal trial procedures.

Next, investigations of military commanders at that time revealed that Sŏ Chong-ch'ŏl, the vice regimental commander of the 9th Regiment, and Kim Chŏng-mu, the 9th Regiment's supply staff officer, both claimed to be unaware of military court-martial proceedings against civilians during the '1948 military court-martial.'[^176] Chŏn Pu-il, who was the 2nd Regiment's 1st Battalion Commander during the '1949 military court-martial,' testified that while he had processed three Youth Corps members through military court-martial, he was unaware of military court-martial proceedings against 1,659 civilians.[^177] The Jeju 4·3 Incident Committee requested the Ministry of National Defense to investigate records and personal information of judges, judges' assessors, prosecutors, defense counsel, preliminary investigation officers, legal advisors, and others involved in military court-martial proceedings, but received a response that relevant records did not exist.[^178] The Committee itself also traced the personal information of military-related personnel, but was unable to confirm them due to death and other reasons.

Investigation of police officials involved in military court-martial proceedings was conducted targeting one police officer who directly interrogated the military court-martial subjects and two police officers who were responsible for escorting them to prison. The interrogating police officer testified that he classified people confined at the sojŏng factory as grades A, B, C, and so forth, and then sent them to prison all at once without a trial.[^179] The two police officers responsible for escort testified that upon arrival at the prison, they witnessed the prison warden or military personnel notify the military court-martial subjects of charges and sentences as they were lined up.[^180] This police testimony is consistent with that of the survivors and shows that major procedures stipulated in the National Defense Security Act for military court-martial proceedings were disregarded.
