**[p. 463]**

② Documentary Material Investigation

Documentary materials investigated included legal records of military court-martial proceedings, prison records, police documents, National Assembly minutes, government records, newspaper articles, and U.S. materials. First, legal documents that could prove the establishment of military court-martial proceedings—such as indictments, witness statements, preliminary investigation reports, trial records, and court judgments—were sought, but none could be verified at all. When inquiring with the Ministry of National Defense regarding the existence of relevant legal documents, the response was that they "could not be found within the military" and "the exact reasons for non-existence could not be confirmed."[^181] The Government Records Management Service likewise responded that "the court judgments of the high military court-martial related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident are not preserved."[^182]

Furthermore, since the personal record cards of prison inmates (inmate cards) contain all records including individual court judgments for each inmate, the Jeju 4·3 Incident Committee investigated the personal record cards of military court-martial subjects kept in the Masan Prison at the Government Records Management Service. As a result, it was confirmed that the personal record cards for Ko U-gyŏng, Ko Tae-song, Mun Cha-ŏn, Yang Si-u, and Chin Tal-kŭn contained certification documents (dated Dec. 18, 1975) stating "no certified copies of court judgments are preserved at this facility." Generally, all prison records of correctional institution inmates are preserved as a complete set, and when transferred to another facility, the prison records should be transferred intact to the receiving institution. Therefore, the fact that the Masan Prison did not preserve court judgments from that time indicates that court judgments were not attached from the time of sentence execution.

Furthermore, among the prison records, in the 1948–1949 Mokpo Prison list of released leftist inmates, numerous Jeju-origin military court-martial subjects are confirmed. When examining the column describing criminal facts in this list, in cases of ordinary trial convicts, concrete criminal facts are recorded—these may be considered to have been written based on court judgments. However, in cases of military court-martial subjects from Jeju, every single person without exception has this column left blank. This can only be understood as resulting from the fact that court judgments were not drawn up at the time of sentence execution.

Another piece of evidence that military court-martial judgments did not exist is the case of Kim Ch'un-bae, an inmate in Mapo Prison. He was a subject of the '1948 military court-martial,' imprisoned without trial in Mapo Prison in December 1948, received notification of a twenty-year sentence in the prison courtyard, remained imprisoned, and was released during the Korean War in 1950. However, in 1962, he was arrested by soldiers for reasons of serving the remainder of his sentence and was reimprisoned.[^183] His family filed an objection to the remainder sentence execution with the Army Headquarters' Military Court-Martial, and the military court-martial (Aug. 20, 1963) canceled the sentence execution for the following reasons:

> Although in cases where the original judgment document is lost due to natural disasters or other causes and it is impossible to attach a certified copy or transcript to the sentence execution directive, other certification materials sufficient to specifically clarify the type and scope of the sentence should be attached in executing the sentence, the case records and sentence execution directive did not have attached other certification materials sufficient to specifically clarify the criminal facts. Therefore, the Prosecutor's disposition to execute the remainder sentence is improper.

Examining police documents related to military court-martial proceedings, it is difficult to view military court-martial trial procedures as having been conducted normally, as there is no evidence that the military authorities responsible for court-martial held preliminary investigations and hearings. Specifically, from June 6 to August 3, 1949, the Special Investigation Division of the Jeju Police Bureau submitted 1,021 prisoners of war and surrendered persons to the 2nd Regiment, creating a secret document "Prisoner Status Report" in which the disposition grades for military court-martial subjects were divided into A, B, C, D, Kap, and Ŭl. In a secret document sent on June 5, 1949, by the Mosŭlp'o Police Station Chief to the Jeju Police Bureau Chief, it explicitly stated "Record A is death sentence, B is life imprisonment opinion."[^181] Given that the submission period corresponds with the time period when the '1949 military court-martial' is said to have been held, it is presumed that without preliminary investigation, the military authorities adopted the police opinions and predetermined the judgment and verdict contents.

[^181]: Ministry of National Defense, Response regarding re-request for cooperation with materials related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident (Law No. 33031-655, Sept. 11, 2002).

[^182]: Government Records Management Service, Response to inquiry about high military court-martial records (Administrative No. 12307-3987, Dec. 26, 2002).

**[p. 464]**

Notably, the two military court-martial proceedings in Jeju were not mentioned at all by the Korean government or the National Assembly, and no related articles appeared in contemporary domestic newspapers. When examining National Assembly minutes, Cabinet minutes, and presidential authorization documents, while various discussions of the Jeju 4·3 Incident are found, no contents related to military court-martial proceedings appear. In domestic newspapers, all related articles about civilian trials and general military court-martial proceedings were reported without exception, yet not a single article on military court-martial proceedings targeting civilians in Jeju can be found. In the case of the Yŏsu Rebellion, the Ministry of National Defense made official announcements of military court-martial implementation, and not only did over 60 National Assembly members including Kim Chang-ryŏl submit an urgent motion to the National Assembly stating that innocent civilians had been unjustly processed in the court-martial and recommended the President for retrial and leniency,[^184] but contemporary newspapers also reported on it in detail. Although a total of 2,530 civilians received guilty verdicts in civilian-targeted military court-martial proceedings, with death sentences and life sentences numbering 384 and 305 respectively—an unprecedentedly large number in judicial history for a single incident—the fact that it was not discussed in the National Assembly or government and was not reported in newspapers at all raises questions. This suggests suspicion that the Jeju Martial Law Command and the 2nd Regiment, which oversaw the military court-martial, did not actually conduct trials or consistently conducted formal proceedings in disregard of legal procedures.

The records state that in the '1948 military court-martial,' 871 persons received guilty verdicts across twelve hearings, and in the '1949 military court-martial,' 1,659 persons received guilty verdicts across ten hearings, all with uniform guilty verdicts. It is highly doubtful whether it was possible to complete the trial procedures for over one hundred or two hundred or more defendants who universally maintained their innocence in a single day and deliver guilty verdicts. Particularly, on June 28, 1949, 200 people received death sentences; on June 29, 132 people; on June 30, 13 people; and on July 1, 238 people received life sentences. In a trial where defendants are guaranteed the opportunity for argument and counter-argument regarding the prosecuted facts, presentation of evidence and counter-evidence, and defense—can one deliver uniform severe sentences such as death and life imprisonment in a single day's proceedings? This is highly questionable.

Regarding these circumstances, attention must be paid to the timing of the military court-martial proceedings. In the case of the '1948 military court-martial,' twelve hearings were held from December 3 to 27, 1948, and the '1949 military court-martial' is recorded as conducting ten hearings from June 23 to July 7, 1949. Given that the 9th Regiment began withdrawing from Jeju on December 29, 1948, and the 2nd Regiment on July 7, 1949,[^185] the two military court-martials appear to have been established to avoid summary executions and to process in bulk the remaining civilians before the suppression forces withdrew. Accordingly, the two court-martials processed hundreds of people through hasty procedures without preliminary investigations, hearings, sentence determinations, or judgments. This constitutes specific circumstances showing that these cannot be viewed as trials observing National Defense Security Act procedures.

[^184]: Seoul Sinmun, April 22, 1949.

[^185]: KMAG, G-2 Periodic Report, No. 192, October 6, 1949.

**[p. 465]**

Particularly, the '1949 military court-martial' uniformly convicted 1,659 civilians within ten days without proper examination of guilt or innocence, and moreover processed them more severely than the '1948 military court-martial.' One cannot but question whether Articles 32–33 of the National Defense Security Act regarding "providing aid communication and espionage to the enemy" could be uniformly applied to mountainous region residents who had taken refuge in the mountains and subsequently came down.

The execution of those recorded as death sentences in the two military court-martials was handled non-publicly, and the fact that the bodies were buried in secret graves also raises suspicion about the legitimacy of this trial. U.S. materials indicate that the 249 death row inmates from the '1949 military court-martial' were executed "with presidential authorization,"[^185] but related documents are not found in the presidential authorization documents file held in the Government Records Management Service. Article 66 of the 1948 Constitutional Law for the Founding of the State stipulates that "all government acts of the President must be in written form, and all documents must have the countersignatures of the Prime Minister and relevant government ministers. Military matters are the same." Given that documents based on this provision are absent, it is difficult to maintain confidence in the military court-martial proceedings and sentence execution.

Ultimately, the '1948–1949 military court-martial' conducted in Jeju cannot be proven to have followed normal legal procedures prescribed by law—no trial records have been found, namely judgment documents, trial records, or preliminary investigation reports. Particularly noteworthy is the case of the Army Headquarters Military Court-Martial judgment (Aug. 20, 1963) regarding Kim Ch'un-bae, which canceled the remainder sentence execution because military court-martial trial basis documents did not exist. Moreover, through the testimony of military personnel, police, and victims that trials either did not occur or that sentence notifications only came upon arrival at prison—testimony indicating formal procedures only; through various materials showing that judgment documents, which are permanent preservation targets under relevant laws, were not created from the start; and through circumstances such as processing hundreds of persons per day without hearings, pronouncing death sentences on 345 persons in four days, yet having this fact reported in no domestic media—one can see that trial procedures were not observed.

Therefore, the '4·3 Incident military court-martial' cannot be viewed as a trial following normal legal procedures prescribed by law.

**[p. 466]**

4) The Outbreak of the Korean War and Prison Inmate Casualties

① Transfer of Prison Inmates

Due to the Jeju 4·3 Incident, persons who received prison or detention sentences through ordinary trials and military court-martial proceedings were scattered and imprisoned in penitentiaries throughout the country. Ordinary trial convicts were primarily imprisoned in Mokpo and Gwangju prisons. Military court-martial subjects were immediately imprisoned in Sŏdaemun, Mapo, Daejeon, Daegu, Mokpo, Incheon, and Jeonju prisons. Mapo, Daejeon, and Incheon Prison inmates experienced almost no transfers, but inmates at other prisons were transferred to different prisons depending on the conditions of the prison and other circumstances.

Among those imprisoned in Mokpo Prison from the '1948 military court-martial,' 24 life-sentence prisoners were transferred to Daegu Prison on March 4, 1949.[^186] The 202 prisoners with 15-year sentences were transferred to Daegu Prison shortly after being imprisoned in Mokpo Prison.[^187] Some of those with 10-year and 5-year sentences were transferred to Gimcheon Prison and then moved to Bucheon Prison on April 28, 1950.[^188] Some prisoners were also transferred from Mokpo Prison to Jinju Prison.[^189] About ten prisoners in Daegu Prison were transferred to Busan Prison on January 14–17, 1950.[^190] Some Jeonju Prison inmates were transferred to Andong Prison in April 1949 because the prison became too crowded.[^191]

Most of the 300 inmates imprisoned in Daegu Prison from the '1949 military court-martial' were transferred to Busan Prison on January 17 and 20, 1950.[^192] After the war broke out, about 150 more were moved to Masan Prison on October 3.[^193] About two hundred female inmates confined in Jeonju Prison were transferred to Sŏdaemun Prison within a month of confinement.[^194]

Ordinary trial convicts were mostly imprisoned in Mokpo and Gwangju prisons, and transfers to other prisons were rare. After June 25, 1950, none of those imprisoned in these two prisons survived. However, as exceptions, Yang Kyu-sŏk and Ko Yun-sŏp were transferred from Gwangju Prison to Daegu Prison and then, when the Korean War broke out, to Busan Prison. Against all odds, these two preserved their lives during the war, completed their sentences, and returned to their hometown.

[^186]: 1949 Daegu Prison inmate roster (held by Government Records Management Service).

[^187]: 1948–1949 Daegu Prison inmate roster (held by Government Records Management Service) lists as confined in Daegu Prison.

[^188]: Postcard sent by Ko Tu-jŏng of Iho-ri, Jeju town, to his father Ko Byŏng-ho, May 22, 1950 (Jeju 4·3 Institute, "4·3 and History" inaugural issue, p. 234); postcard sent by Ko Kyŏng-ok of Tonam-ri, Jeju town, to his wife Kang Tŏk-hwa, June 18, 1950 (ibid., p. 250).

[^189]: Hyŏn Kwi-ch'an (also known as Hyŏn Sŏng-ch'an), from Hwabuk-ri, Jeju town, sent postcards from Mokpo Prison and later from Jinju Prison according to report (Jemin Ilbo 4·3 Reporting Team, "4·3 Speaks" ④, p. 102). Kim Byŏng-no, from Odŭng-ri, Jeju town, was also transferred from Mokpo Prison to Jinju Prison (4th National Assembly Committee, Report on Inquiry into Incidents of Massacre of Innocent Civilians, Odŭng-dong, Jeju City report, 1960).

[^190]: Confirmed through prison records: An Tu-sŏn, Pak Chung-don, Yi Wŏn-hwa, Shin Chae-un, Kim Samhyŏn, Kim Ch'i-ok, Hyŏn Myŏng-ha, Hyŏn Po-ha, Pak Mun-ok, Yi Yŏ-bae, Yi Il-man (Government Records Management Service, Daegu Prison Life Sentence Register; Daegu Prison inmate roster; Busan Prison inmate roster).

[^191]: Previous testimony of Kang Chŏng-sun and Song Sun-hŭi.

[^192]: Daegu Prison inmate roster; Busan Prison inmate roster (Government Records Management Service).

[^193]: Masan Prison personal record cards (Government Records Management Service). The Government Records Management Service holds personal record cards of 134 persons transferred from Busan Prison to Masan Prison.

[^194]: Government Records Management Service holds personal record card rosters of Sŏdaemun Prison confirming names such as Yi Chae-ok, Yi Chŏng-suk, and Yi Chŏng-saeng. Hŏ Tu-hŏn reached Jeonju Prison and returned alive; he directly witnessed the transfer. Testimony of Hŏ Tu-hŏn (age 72, Tonam-dong, Jeju; student at the time; recorded Dec. 13, 2000).

**[p. 467]**

② Mokpo Prison Escape Incident

On September 14, 1949, at 5 p.m., about 400 inmates at Mokpo Prison attacked the prison armory and escaped armed. According to an announcement made by the Joint Military-Police Command on September 15, "at the time of the incident, the number of confined persons was 1,421, and 1,000 participated in the escape riot, after which 500 were immediately suppressed by joint military-police attack and 353 completely escaped."[^195] Contemporary domestic newspapers and materials sent by the American Embassy to the State Department described "most of the escapees as political prisoners serving terms for the Jeju Island rebellion incident."[^196]

When the incident broke out, two Army companies and local police, along with a naval detachment, jointly pursued inmates who had escaped the prison.[^197] According to matters reported by the Minister of Justice to the Cabinet after the incident was suppressed, as of September 19, there were 3 self-surrenders, 286 shot (228 outside the prison, 58 inside), 85 arrested, and 37 not yet captured. Among prison officials, 6 died and 5 were critically wounded.[^198] Justice Minister Kwŏn Sŭng-ryŏl, in his response to the National Assembly on October 4, 1949, pointed to the causes of this incident as follows:

> The first cause of the Mokpo Prison incident is the poor treatment of prisoners. Mokpo Prison is a facility that can accommodate only 600 people, yet at that time it was confining 1,421. Because of this situation, people in the prison could only sit to sleep and could not lie down. Instances exist of 30–40 people in a single cell. So those people thought, "We cannot sleep well, we cannot eat well, in these circumstances we are facing death anyway, so we might as well do anything," and for this reason they committed these acts.[^199]

The overcrowding of prisons due to excess capacity was not a problem unique to Mokpo Prison. As of August 1949, approximately 24,000 inmates were confined in nineteen prisons throughout the country. A Seoul Sinmun reporter visited seven prisons in South Korea—Daegu, Busan, Gwangju, Mokpo, Jeonju, Gunsan, and Daejeon—and found that all exceeded capacity by 40–50%, with 6–8 inmates per cell per pyŏng. In the case of Daegu Prison, 3,068 people were confined in a facility designed for 1,500, utilizing halls, warehouses, and workshops as prison cells.[^200] American officials assessed that "Mokpo Prison is not Korea's only overcrowded prison, and if improvements to prison conditions are not implemented, the incident demonstrates the possibility of similar uprisings occurring anywhere. There is a thought that political prisoners should be separately confined."[^201]

There were approximately 600 Jeju-origin inmates in Mokpo Prison.[^202] The exact number of those from this group who were killed in the escape incident is difficult to confirm due to lack of precise collective data. Currently, examining the Mokpo Prison terminus personal record cards and the Mokpo Prison released leftist inmate list held in the Government Records Management Service and Mokpo Prison respectively, identifying Jeju-origin inmates released on the day of the incident, the total reaches 51 people. These are presumed to be Jeju 4·3 Incident-related victims executed after the escape.

[^195]: Han-sŏng Ilbo, September 17, 1949.

[^196]: Kyŏnghyang Sinmun, September 16, 1949; Intelligence Summary, No. 2573, September 25, 1949, GHQ, Far East Command, RG 6: Records of General Headquarters, Far East Command, reel 657, MacArthur Memorial Library, U.S.A.

[^197]: Joint Weeka, No. 14, September 9–16, 1949.

[^198]: "84th Cabinet Council Minutes," September 20, 1949. According to a final tally announced by Mokpo Police Station on September 23, among 413 escaped convicts: 85 arrested, 298 shot, 10 self-surrenders, 23 not captured, 10 firearms recovered (Honam Sinmun, September 24, 1949).

[^199]: National Assembly stenographic record, 5th Session, No. 13, October 4, 1949.

[^200]: Seoul Sinmun, August 7, 1949.

[^201]: "Summary of Political Affairs of the Republic of Korea, September 1949," Enclosure 1 to Despatch No. 621, dated October 7, 1949, from American Embassy to State Department, Seoul, Korea.

[^202]: Previous National Assembly stenographic record.

**[p. 468]**

③ Collective Executions Immediately After the Korean War

When the Korean War broke out in 1950, the government issued an urgent telephone communication to all police departments on the afternoon of June 25 at 2:25 p.m. in the name of the Security Bureau Chief, regarding "apprehension of nationwide watch-list personnel and nationwide prison security."[^203] However, the government was unable to establish security measures for prison inmates in Seoul, Incheon, and other areas and fled southward. However, in the process of the government and military forces moving south, prison inmates in the area south of Pyŏngtaek, Kyŏnggi Province, became targets of collective execution.

At the time of the war's outbreak, the total number of prison inmates nationwide was 37,335, of which 20,229 were in prisons south of Pyŏngtaek.[^204] Regarding Jeju 4·3 Incident-related inmates transferred from Jeju, approximately 200 ordinary trial convicts and, excluding those who had completed their sentences among the two rounds of military court-martial subjects,[^205] approximately 2,350 others were imprisoned when the Korean War broke out. Most of these approximately 2,500 people did not return to Jeju and disappeared without a trace. The following are the results of investigations into the actual circumstances of deaths and disappearances at each prison:

**□ Sŏdaemun, Mapo, and Incheon Prisons**

People confined in Seoul's Sŏdaemun and Mapo prisons and Incheon Prison were released as North Korean People's Army took control of the prisons and scattered to various places. Kim Sang-nyŏn, a Mapo Prison inmate survivor, was forcibly drafted into the Volunteer Army, served, and after the recovery of Seoul was brought to Gwangju Police Station but was released. Later he maintained his survival by enlisting as an active-duty soldier.[^206] Chŏng Ki-sŏng was confined in Mapo Prison with his older brother; after release, his older brother volunteered for the Volunteer Army while he came down to Mokpo where he lived. Five years later he went to Jeju. He was reimprisoned in Jeju and spent an additional 15 years in prison.[^207] When Incheon Prison staff evacuated to Suwŏn on June 30, approximately 300 Jeju-origin inmates were dispersed. A considerable number of inmates who went down to Suwŏn were reconfined in prison by military order. When the North Korean People's Army occupied Incheon on July 3, staff again evacuated and North Korean-descended prison officials were assigned to the prison. Most of the remaining inmates were drafted into the Volunteer Army.[^208] During the September 28 recovery of Seoul, some Incheon Prison inmates followed the North Korean People's Army northward, but some returned to Jeju, and a considerable number disappeared without a trace. Pak Kyŏng-ju arrived in Jeju on the morning of the day of arrival and was taken into custody by police where he was executed with suspected persons awaiting arrest.[^209]

[^203]: "Those Who Do Not Return" (Jeju KBS documentary), aired March 30, 2000.

[^204]: Defense Ministry Political Education Bureau, War History Compilation Committee, "One Year of the Korean War" (1951), cited in Sŏ Chung-sŏk, "Cho Bong-am and the 1950s, Part II," Yŏksa Pip'yŏng Press, 1999).

[^205]: Among '1948 military court-martial' subjects, those with 1-year sentences were 90 in Mokpo Prison, 52 in Incheon Prison, and 38 in Jeonju Prison, totaling 180.

[^206]: Testimony of Kim Sang-nyŏn (age 76, Tohwa 2-dong, Mapo-gu, Seoul; recorded March 26, 2002).

[^207]: Testimony of Chŏng Ki-sŏng (age 79, Sinryŏ 1-ri, Namwŏn-ŭp; recorded Dec. 14, 2000).

**□ Daejeon Prison**

Daejeon Prison held 300 subjects of the '1949 military court-martial.' However, not a single person among these returned alive to Jeju after the Korean War. At the time of the war's outbreak, approximately 4,000 people were confined in Daejeon Prison, including political and ideological prisoners and common criminals related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident and the Yŏsu Rebellion.[^210] All of these were executed in mass killings over three days in the first week of July at Nangwŏl-dong Kol-ryŏng-gol in Sangnae-myŏn, Tŏk-gun, Chungnam (present-day Nangwŏl-dong, Tong-gu, Daejeon Metropolitan City). Eighteen on-site execution photographs excavated and made public by the U.S. National Archives directly show the brutal conditions of that time. These photographs were taken by U.S. Far East Command liaison officer Captain Everett, and an attached document contains a report written by U.S. Military Attaché Major Bob Edwards of the Embassy of the United States in Korea. In it, he states: "Orders for executions undoubtedly came from the highest levels. The execution of 1,800 political prisoners at Daejeon took three days and occurred in the first week of July 1950." This fact is also confirmed through testimony of prison official Kim Hyŏng-sik and Sangnae village residents Yi Kyu-hŭi and Kim Chong-sŏk. Kim Hyŏng-sik directly witnessed the massacre, having handed the inmates to the military and attended the execution site. Kim Chong-sŏk was conscripted along with village residents to dig pits.[^211] Additionally, British Observer correspondent Philip Deane's account "I Was a Prisoner of War in Korea" (1953) contains related testimony. In it are accounts from French priest Cadart and Daily Worker correspondent Warrington, whom he met while held as a prisoner of war in North Korea, regarding the Daejeon massacre. Father Cadart testified that he witnessed 1,700 Daejeon Prison inmates being loaded onto trucks and executed by gunfire near his church. Warrington visited the execution site immediately after the killings and testified that more than 7,000 male and female bodies were buried in six pits, with the largest pit spanning 200 yards. He testified that the inmate mass killings occurred on July 4–6.

[^208]: Testimony of Chŏng P'ae-jong (age 71, Sinryŏ 1-ri, Namwŏn-ŭp; recorded Dec. 14, 2000), Pak Tong-su (age 68, Yongdam-dong, Jeju; recorded Dec. 15, 2000), Yi Po-yŏn (age 72, Iho 1-dong, Jeju; recorded Aug. 3, 2001).

[^209]: Testimony of Yi Po-yŏn.

[^210]: Justice Ministry, "Korean Penal History," 1987, p. 515.

[^211]: Shim Kyu-sang, "Remember July 8, 1950, Nangwŏl-dong," Monthly Mal, February 2002 issue.

**[p. 469]**

The 300 Jeju-origin inmates are confirmed to have been confined in Daejeon Prison until just before the war, but all disappeared without a trace; therefore, as revealed above, they are presumed to be secretly buried in Nangwŏl-dong Kol-ryŏng-gol.

**□ Daegu Prison**

At the time of the war's outbreak, approximately 4,000 people, including approximately 200 Jeju 4·3 Incident-related inmates, were confined in Daegu Prison. Among these, 1,402 inmates confined for crimes including sedition, murder, violations of the National Security Act, and the National Defense Security Act were transferred from the prison to the military from July 7–30, 1950. In the 1950 Daegu Prison inmate roster kept at Daegu Prison, the names of 1,402 inmates carry a stamp reading "handed over to military and police."[^212] According to a report of results investigated from June 2–6, 1960, by the National Assembly's Special Investigative Committee on Incidents of Massacre of Innocent Civilians, the investigation concluded that "all disposition of the 1,402 Daegu Prison inmates after being handed over is completely unknown."

At that time, Daegu Daily News, which published the entire list, discussed the collective execution of these inmates as follows:

> The roster of 1,402 people who were confined in Daegu Prison as decided and accused prisoners immediately after the June 25 incident and handed over to military and police is revealed. If those people taken by military and police from prison officials are not currently alive, they are known to have been executed in mass killings in the Daegu vicinity, and the date when they were handed over from the hands of prison officials to military and police is known to be their death anniversary. If all of these people were executed, they received death sentences without judicial pronouncement.[^213]

[^212]: Upon closer examination of this roster, a stamp reading "handed over to military and police" is covered by an additional stamp reading "transferred to Jinju." One suspects that Daegu Prison officials stamped an additional stamp to conceal the fact of handover to military and police. Kim Ch'ang-su, a Daegu Prison victim, received from Daegu Prison in 1999 an official document stating "transferred to Jinju Prison on July 23, 1950," which does not accord with the facts.

[^213]: Taegu Daily News, June 7, 1960.

**[p. 470]**

Among the 1,402 people handed over to military and police, after that date no one's survival has been confirmed. These people are believed to have been mostly shot and killed at Kach'ang-gol or Kobaltu mines in Kyŏngsan, Kyŏngbuk Province.

| Military Court-Martial Date | July 9 | July 27 | July 28 | July 30 |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 1948 Military Court-Martial | Kim Yong-gyu, Kim Yong-sik, Pak Pong-gyu, Chin Sŏng-bo, Kang Kun-p'yo, Kang Gwon-do, Kang Mun-gu, Kang Po-yŏ, Kang Sang-ŏn, Kang Su-man, Kang Yŏng-gu, Kang Yŏng-ok, Kang Wi-mun, Kang Wi-su, Kang Wi-ch'un, Kang Ik-man, Kang Chŏng-su, Kang Ch'an-p'yo, Kang Pang-ok, Kim Byŏng-gu, Kang Ch'ang-ha, Kang P'yŏng-ryong, Ko Myŏng-sun, Ko Byŏng-u, Ko Sŏn-jo, Ko Ye-bong, Ko Wŏn-an, Kim Sang-hwa, Kim Sŏng-sik, Ko Yun-ju, Ko In-p'yŏng, Ko Ch'ang-un, Ko T'ae-hung, Kim Yŏ-hŭp, Kim Mun-su, Kim Sang-ho, Kim U-gyu, Kim Wŏn-sŏk, Kim Sŏk-gu, Kim Sŏk-ryong, Kim Sŭng-man, Kim Yang-saeng, Kim Yŏng-du, Kim Yŏng-ok, Kim Yŏng-ch'an, Kim Ch'an-ok, Kim Wŏn-ha, Kim Ŭn-hu, Kim Ŭng-su, Kim Chae-jun, Kim Chae-ha, Kim Chung-yŏn, Kim Ch'ang-su, Kim Ch'ang-su (Chungmun) (Ara), Kim Ch'i-gwan, Kim T'ae-ho, Kim P'aeng-sik, Kim Hae-bong, Kim Hŭi-kil, Mun Tu-sŭng, Song Kap-chŏng, Song Tu-sŏn, Mun Byŏng-hŭi, Mun Suk-hyŏn, Mun Wŏn-do, Mun Chae-ŏn, Mun Chung-hŭi, Mun T'ae-bo, Mun Ha-ryong, Song Tu-ha, Yang Kŭn-si | Pak Ki-yŏng, Pak Tu-sin, Pak Sang-u, Pak Chi-ho, Pak Ch'ang-ju, Pak Hong-gi, Pyŏn Gwang-hŭi, Yang Nam-gi, Yang Po-hyŏn, Pu Ŏn-jong, Sŏ Sang-ch'i, Sŏ Yong-ŭn, Son Sŏk-ju, Song Tu-rim, Song Tu-ch'ang, Song Tu-haeng, Yang Sŏk-bong, Yang Sŏk-chung, Song Byŏng-mu, Yang Kun-sam, Yang Kun-sŏk, Yang Mun-hwa, Yang Sang-wŏn, Yang Chŏng-sam, O Yŏng-gwon, Yang Ch'i-baek, Yang T'ae-ho, O Yong-du, O Im-kil, O Chŏng-gyu, O T'ae-hae, O Hyŏng-ryul, O Hyŏng-yun, O Hyŏng-un, O To-gyun, O Hyŏng-man, Yi Kun-hyŏng, Yi Kŭn-taek, Yi Tŏk-sin, Yi Man-hung, Yi Sang-hyŏp, Yi Sŏk, Yi Si-ch'un, Han T'ak-sŏp, Han Ch'i-hwa, Yi Hong-bae, Im Ch'ang-yun, Im T'ae-hun, Chŏn Ki-nam, Chŏn Ki-jip, Chŏn Byŏng-bu, Chŏn In-bong, Hyŏn Tŏk-hong, Hyŏn Sang-ho, Chŏn Chun-bu, Chin Hŭi-in, Han Sang-sŏp, Han Chin-saeng, Han Hung-bŏm, Hŏ Sŭng-ik, Hŏ In-ch'ŏl, Hong Wŏn-p'yo, Hong Ch'ang-sik | | Ko Ki-wŏn, Kim Ch'ang-hŭi |
| 1949 Military Court-Martial | | | | |

Among the 1,402 names listed, 142 Jeju 4·3 Incident-related inmates are identified: 116 subjects of the '1948 military court-martial' and 26 subjects of the '1949 military court-martial.' The dates of handover to military and police were July 9, 27, 28, and 30, spanning four days. Among the 142 listed above, not a single person's survival has been confirmed after the date of military and police handover. These people are mostly believed to have been executed at Kach'ang-gol or Kobaltu mines near Kyŏngsan, Kyŏngbuk Province. As testified in 1960 by Yi Bok-nyŏng, who participated in the National Assembly's Special Investigative Committee on Incidents of Massacre of Innocent Civilians, Kach'ang-gol, where he visited for on-site investigation, was filled with abandoned bones, with countless spent cartridges scattered about, and thousands of skeletal remains were buried beneath the ground.[^214]

[^214]: Kunmin Ilbo, April 11, 2002.

**[p. 471]**

**□ Busan Prison**

Most of the 300 Jeju-origin inmates who went to Daegu Prison from the '1949 military court-martial' were transferred to Busan Prison on January 17 and 20, 1950. According to the Busan Prison inmate admission/release date table and Busan Prison correctional statistics table held in the Government Records Management Service, approximately 2,000 inmates in January–July 1950 increased to approximately 4,000 at the end of August and 5,500 by the end of September. A space of only 3–4 pyŏng was confining 42 people.[^215]

When the war broke out, inmates sentenced to three or more years of imprisonment were assembled in the prison courtyard, loaded onto trucks, taken away, and executed.[^216] The Jeju-origin inmates transferred from Daegu Prison were confined in Block 2, and except for Cells 28, 29, and 30, which were filled exclusively with Jeju-origin inmates, inmates in the remaining cells were all taken out of the prison.[^217]

Examining the Busan Prison inmate roster, a total of 255 Jeju-origin inmates are identified. Among these, 162 survived the war, with dates of transfer to Masan and Jinju prisons and deaths recorded. However, the remarks column for An Tu-sŏn, Pak Mun-ok, and Han Sŭng-taek carries a stamp reading "handed over to Military Police," and the remaining 90 names carry no entries. Furthermore, examining the inmate statistical report file, the count of National Defense Security Act inmates (Jeju 4·3 Incident-related) was 231 at the end of June 1950, but declined to 119 by the end of July, meaning 112 disappeared.

In the Busan Prison inmate admission/release date table (1950), the numbers of National Defense Security Act inmates are shown to have decreased on July 27 and 29, 1950. Investigating 93 persons identified in the above records, not a single survivor was confirmed. Therefore, approximately 110 Jeju-origin inmates shown in the Busan Prison corrections records are presumed to have been taken out of the prison on July 27 and 29, 1950, and executed.

[^215]: Testimony of Kang Sang-mun (age 73, Wolp'yŏng-dong, Jeju; recorded Dec. 13, 2000).

[^216]: Kosung-hwa, a Jeju-origin person confined in Cell 19 of Busan Prison at the time, recalls that all the inmates in his cell with sentences of three or more years were taken away and massacred (Ko Sŏng-hwa, "My Memoir," Hanul Press, 2001, p. 153).

[^217]: Testimony of Kang Sang-mun.

**[p. 472]**

**□ Other Prisons (Gwangju, Jeonju, Mokpo, Jinju, and Bucheon Prisons)**

At the time of the Korean War's outbreak, Jeju 4·3 Incident-related prison inmates were also scattered and imprisoned in Gwangju, Jeonju, Mokpo, Jinju, and Bucheon prisons among other facilities. Due to lack of documentation, it is unclear how many were confined in these prisons and how they were processed after the war. Only through visiting records, correspondence, and other testimony has it been established that people definitely confined during wartime in each prison had no survivors.

Based on the following records, it is presumed that Jeju 4·3 Incident-related inmates confined in Gwangju, Jeonju, and Mokpo prisons were killed immediately after the war's outbreak:

> Convicts confined in Gwangju, Jeonju, and Mokpo prisons and those related to the People's League for National Salvation and other suspects should be processed by consultation directly among the responsible officials nationwide, police bureau chiefs, prison wardens, and prosecutors, with release for detention for minor theft and other common crimes to be handled through appropriate means. Those under police custody should also be processed based on the above.[^218]

This directive was given by Military Police Commander Song Yo-ch'an to the Gwangju Military Police Company Commander. Following this directive, executions of Jeju 4·3 Incident-related inmates in Honam region prisons are presumed to have occurred. Among Jeonju Prison inmates, those with three or more years of sentences were executed in a cemetery behind Jeonju Prison from the night of June 27 to the early morning of June 28, with pits dug for their burial. In the case of Gwangju Prison, from July 7–23, 1950, military police flocked to the prison, loaded inmates onto trucks, took them to the mountains, and executed them lined up in pits that had been dug in advance. Execution sites included the Pulgaeng'i Pass in Kŭknak-myŏn, Kwangsan-gun, Gwangju, Chisama village in Chisa-myŏn, Kwangsan-gun, Kogŭng Pass in Sŏkgo-myŏn, Hantoch'al in Taech'ŏn-myŏn, and Miryang Pass in Haktong 3-ku, Gwangju—five locations in all.[^219]

Kang Wit-ok, who was released from Gwangju Prison just before the war on June 23, 1950, testified that all inmates with sentences of three or more years were killed. He also recalled that a certain Hyŏn from Ŭigwi-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, originally from Gwangju Prison, was drowned but survived.[^220]

[^218]: Chosŏn Inmin-bo, September 5, 1950.

[^219]: Regarding the actual circumstances of prison inmate casualties in the Honam region, see Shin Kyŏng-dŭk, "Civilian Massacres Seen Through On-the-Spot Accounts from the Korean War," Salimtŏ Press, 2002, pp. 165–186.

[^220]: Testimony of Kang Wit-ok (age 86, Wolp'yŏng-dong, Jeju; people's organization member at the time; recorded Dec. 15, 2001).

**[p. 473]**

④ Deaths While Imprisoned

Among Jeju 4·3 Incident-related inmates, some died in prisons while confined due to harsh confinement conditions as the prison system experienced severe overcrowding. Specific investigation of these cases was difficult due to limitations in documentary evidence. However, through testimony and existing records, it is confirmed that many died under cruel conditions.

**[p. 474]**

| July 30 | Ko Ki-wŏn Kim Ch'ang-hui | |

Among the 1,402 individuals on this list, 142 individuals related to the Jeju 4·3 Incident are confirmed. These are 116 subjects of the "1948 Military Court" and 26 subjects of the "1949 Military Court." The dates on which they were transferred to the military and police were July 9, 27, 28, and 30—occurring over four days. The compiled list is shown in the table above.

Among these 142 individuals listed, not a single person was confirmed to have survived after the date of transfer to the military and police. Most were confirmed to have been shot and executed at places such as Kachang Gorge in Kyŏngsan, Kyŏngbuk Province, or in cobalt mines. In June 1960, Yi Bok-nyŏng, who participated in the National Assembly's Special Investigation Committee on the Civilian Massacre Incident, testified that when visiting Kachang Gorge on-site, it was filled with abandoned remains, scattered with countless spent cartridges, and contained thousands of buried remains in the ground.[^21]

[^21]: Kukmín Ilbo, April 11, 2002.

**[p. 475]**

□ Busan Penitentiary

Most of the approximately 300 Jeju natives who had gone to Taegu Penitentiary via the "1949 Military Court" were transferred to Busan Penitentiary on January 17 and 20, 1950. According to the record of prisoner transfers at Busan Penitentiary and the Busan Penitentiary correction statistics housed at the Government Records Preservation Office, the number of detainees, which was approximately 2,000 from January to July 1950, increased to approximately 4,000 by the end of August and to 5,500 by the end of September. Cells of only 3–4 pyŏng[^22] held as many as 42 detainees each.[^23]

When the war broke out, prisoners among the detainees sentenced to three years or more were assembled in the prison yard, loaded onto trucks, and taken away to be executed.[^24] The Jeju natives transferred from the Taegu Penitentiary were held in Ward 2; with the exception of Cells 28, 29, and 30, which were filled entirely with Jeju natives, the detainees in the remaining cells were all dragged out of the prison.[^25] Based on the Busan Penitentiary's prisoner roster, the number of Jeju natives identified is 255 total. Of these, 162 were confirmed to have survived through the war, with dates for transfer to Masan and Chinju penitentiaries or death in custody recorded. However, the remarks column for three individuals—An Tu-sŏn, Park Mun-ok, and Han Sŭng-taek—bears the stamp "transferred to Military Police," and no information is recorded for the remaining 90 individuals. Furthermore, examination of inmate statistical reports shows that as of the end of June 1950, those imprisoned under the National Defense Security Act (Jeju 4·3 Incident-related) numbered 231, but by end of July this had decreased to 119, meaning 112 individuals had disappeared. The Busan Penitentiary prisoner transfer records for 1950, which list reductions in the number of prisoners by date, show that those imprisoned under the National Defense Security Act decreased on July 27 and 29, 1950.

Investigation of 93 individuals whose names are confirmed in the above records revealed that not a single survivor was found. Therefore, it is estimated that approximately 110 Jeju natives among the Busan Penitentiary records were dragged out of the prison and executed on July 27 and 29, 1950.

□ Other Facilities (Kwangju, Chŏnju, Mokpo Penitentiaries)

At the time of the Korean War outbreak, Jeju 4·3 Incident-related prisoners in penitentiaries besides those noted above were dispersed and imprisoned in Kwangju, Chŏnju, Mokpo, Chinju, and Puch'ŏn penitentiaries. Due to lack of records, it is unclear how many were held in these penitentiaries or how they were treated immediately after the war. Based only on visiting records, correspondence, and other testimony, it is confirmed that these individuals were imprisoned during the war, and among those confirmed through these sources, not a single survivor has been found.

The Jeju 4·3 Incident-related prisoners held in Kwangju, Chŏnju, and Mokpo penitentiaries are estimated to have been executed shortly after the war's outbreak, based on the following records:

"For prisoners and National Guidance League members and other suspects detained in Kwangju, Chŏnju, and Mokpo penitentiaries, following discussion with relevant province chiefs, police chiefs, prison chiefs, and prosecutors, implement direct disposition; for theft and other common criminals, implement appropriate measures such as provisional release. Police keeping suspects in custody shall likewise be handled in accordance with the above."[^26]

This order was issued to the Kwangju military police commander by Military Police Commander Song Yo-ch'an. Following this directive, executions of Jeju 4·3 Incident-related individuals among prisoners in Honam region penitentiaries are estimated to have taken place. Among prisoners in Chŏnju Penitentiary, those sentenced to three years or more were executed from the night of June 27 through the early morning of June 28 in ditches dug at the cemetery behind Chŏnju Penitentiary. In the case of Kwangju Penitentiary, from July 7 to July 23, 1950, military police poured into the prison, loaded detainees onto trucks, drove them to mountain sites, and executed them by firing squad in pre-dug pits in a line formation. The execution sites were five locations: Bulkaengi Pass in Geungnak-myŏn, Kwangsan-gun, Kwangju suburbs; Changgo Peak in Chisa-myŏn, Kwangsan-gun; Tonung-go Pass in Sŏkko-myŏn; Handot Ridge in Taech'ŏn-myŏn; and Millyang-dong Pass in Haktong 3-gu, Kwangju City.[^27]

Kang Wi-ok, who was imprisoned in Kwangju Penitentiary and was released on June 23, 1950, just before the war, testified that all those sentenced to three years or more were killed. He also remembered that Hyŏn Amukaae, a native of Ŭi-gwi-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, who was among the Kwangju Penitentiary detainees, was drowned but survived.[^28]

④ Deaths in Custody Among Imprisoned Individuals

Among Jeju 4·3 Incident-related prisoners who were imprisoned in penitentiaries, some died due to harsh detention conditions.[^29]

[^22]: A traditional Korean unit of floor area, approximately 3.3 square meters.

[^24]: At the time, Ko Sŏng-hwa, imprisoned in Ward 19 of Busan Penitentiary, recalls that all inmates in the same cell sentenced to three years or more were dragged away and executed (Ko Sŏng-hwa, My Memorandum, Hanul Publishing, 2001, p. 153).
[^25]: Kang Sang-mun testimony.
[^26]: Chosŏn Inmin Po, September 5, 1950.
[^27]: For information on the victimization of prisoners in Honam region penitentiaries, see Sin Kyŏng-dŭk, "Civilian Massacres as Seen Through Korean Wartime Field Reports," Salim-t'ŏ Publishing, 2002, pp. 165–186.
[^28]: Kang Wi-ok (age 86, Wŏlp'yŏng-dong, Jeju City, leftist organization member at the time, recorded December 15, 2001), testimony.

**[p. 476]**

Among the Jeju 4·3 Incident-related detainees also imprisoned in Kwangju, Chŏnju, Mokpo, Chinju, Puch'ŏn, and other penitentiaries at the time of the Korean War outbreak. Due to lack of documentation, it remains unclear how many were held in these penitentiaries and how they were dealt with immediately after the war. However, through visits, correspondence, and other testimony, it is confirmed that they were definitely imprisoned in various penitentiaries during the war, and among those individuals confirmed through these sources, not a single survivor has been identified.

The Jeju 4·3 Incident-related prisoners held in Kwangju, Chŏnju, and Mokpo penitentiaries are estimated to have been executed immediately after the war's outbreak, according to the following records:

"For prisoners and members associated with the National Guidance League and other suspects held in Kwangju, Chŏnju, and Mokpo penitentiaries, following negotiation among relevant regional chiefs, police directors, prison chiefs, and prosecutors, implement direct disposition; for theft and other misdemeanors, implement appropriate methods such as provisional release. Similarly handle suspects in police custody according to the above."[^29]

This order was issued by Military Police Commander Song Yo-ch'an to the Kwangju Military Police commander. Following these instructions, executions of Jeju 4·3 Incident-related individuals among Honam region penitentiary inmates are estimated to have occurred. Among Chŏnju Penitentiary inmates, those with sentences of three years or more were executed from the night of June 27 through the early dawn of June 28 in graves dug at the cemetery behind Chŏnju Penitentiary. At Kwangju Penitentiary, from July 7 to July 23, 1950, military police rushed to the prison, loaded detainees onto trucks, drove them to mountains, and executed them by gunfire in pre-dug graves while lined up in rows. The execution sites included five locations: Bulkaengi Pass in Geungnak-myŏn, Kwangsan-gun, Kwangju suburbs; Changgo Peak in Chisa-myŏn, Kwangsan-gun; Tonung-go Pass in Sŏkko-myŏn; Handot Ridge in Daech'ŏn-myŏn; and Millyang-dong Pass in Haktong 3-gu, Kwangju.[^30]

Kang Wi-ok, who had been imprisoned in Kwangju Penitentiary and was released on June 23, 1950, just before the war, testified that all those with sentences of three years or more were executed. He also recalled that Hyŏn Amukaae, from Ŭi-gwi-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, among Kwangju Penitentiary inmates, was drowned but survived.[^31]

④ Deaths in Custody Among Imprisoned Individuals

Among the Jeju 4·3 Incident-related prisoners imprisoned in penitentiaries, some perished due to the deplorable conditions of detention.[^32]

[^29]: Chosŏn Inmin Po, September 5, 1950.
[^30]: For information on the victimization of prisoners in Honam region penitentiaries, refer to Sin Kyŏng-dŭk, "Civilian Massacres as Seen in Korean Wartime Field Records," Salim-t'ŏ, 2002, pp. 165–186.
[^31]: Kang Wi-ok (age 86, Wŏlp'yŏng-dong, Jeju City, former leftist organization member, recorded December 15, 2001), testimony.
[^32]: Ibid.

**[p. 477]**

Those detained or awaiting trial died frequently from the aftereffects of torture. At Incheon Prison, a dysentery epidemic broke out and many inmates perished. According to testimony from those who were imprisoned there and survived, inmates such as Yang Sang-won from Susan-ri, Aewol-myeon, and Kang Bong-nam from Yŏn-dong-ri, Cheju town, died of illness.[^222] Mun Dal-ch'ŏn was transferred from Kwangju Prison on February 24, 1949, to Incheon Youth Prison. From March 5, 1949, he received treatment for heart weakness and died on May 3, 1949, within the prison.[^223] Confirmed deaths of inmates at Mapo Prison include Kim Tae-kyŏng (10.15.1949), Kim Yong-saeng (8.13.1949), and Kim Yun-bok (9.8.1949) according to prisoner records and identity registers (dates in parentheses indicate death date). By September 1950, Pusan Prison became severely overcrowded with approximately 5,000 inmates following the outbreak of the Korean War in June. In cells where 4 or 5 inmates should be housed, approximately 40 inmates were confined, and they endured an entire summer in these conditions. ## d. Torture Practices

During the Jeju 4·3 Incident, young and middle-aged men who did not join rightist organizations were frequently hauled off to military units, police stations, and rightist organization offices for interrogation, where they underwent severe torture. Prior to the March 1 Incident on Cheju, torture by police had not been an issue. However, after March 1, police and leftist organization arrests, detentions, and interrogations of members such as those in the Northwest Youth Society became routine. Young leftists engaged in activist work fled to inland Japan to escape this persecution. Police persecution of leftist youth intensified through the February 27 Incident of 1948. In this process, the torture deaths of Kim Yong-ch'ŏl and Yang Ŭn-ha—which became the fuse igniting the April 3 Armed Uprising—occurred at local police stations. Autopsy results and eyewitness testimony revealed the police's brutal torture methods. In Kim Yong-ch'ŏl's case, his body was suspended upside down and beaten with clubs; Yang Ŭn-ha died from the impact of torture involving his testicles. The U.S. Military Government authorities referred all involved police to military court-martial and imposed prison sentences to contain the fallout from this incident. However, after the April 3 Armed Uprising began on April 3, suppression forces conducted preventive arrests and coercive interrogations of residents in mid-mountain villages with possible connections to armed guerrillas. Without exception, brutal torture accompanied these interrogations. Torture served as a preliminary stage for either summary disposition through confirmation of alleged criminal acts, or for transferring cases to trial to create criminals. Under extreme psychological terror and before overpowering state authority, residents were helpless. After undergoing severe torture, individuals lost their capacity for self-defense, and combined with physical impotence came mental instability, forcing them to confess to facts already recorded in interrogation records. The names of persons unrelated to the incident were sometimes unconsciously confessed. Those responsible for torture during the 4·3 Incident were mainly police officers, who were those who had learned torture techniques from Japanese High Police detectives during the colonial period. These High Police detectives had served as shock troops of Japanese colonial rule, and it is widely known that they engaged in surveillance and suppression of their own compatriots as their inherent duty, and conducted torture more severely and frequently against their own people than the Japanese themselves to fabricate crimes of independence activists and their organizations. These torture methods were transmitted to frontline police organizations after liberation and utilized in handling various criminal cases. Moreover, during the 4·3 Incident, torture was not applied only during the process of creating interrogation records before suspects were referred for trial; rather, residents with potential connections to armed guerrillas were collectively detained and regularly tortured during interrogations.

**[p. 480]**

A considerable number of 4·3 Incident victims were killed through armed guerrilla attacks, suppression forces' scorched-earth operations on mid-mountain villages, and collective revenge killings village by village. These deaths can be characterized as arbitrary summary disposition without interrogation or investigation procedures. The remaining victims were mostly executed in mass killings following classification and interrogation by military and police authorities, with some processed through investigative procedures and referred for trial. Certainly, some people were spared their lives when they were found to have no culpability during interrogation. Without exception, torture accompanied these interrogations. The 1949 Geneva Convention of August 12, "On the Protection of Civilians in Time of War," provides in Article 3 regarding state torture of civilians:

> In the case of armed conflict not of an international character occurring in the territory of one of the High Contracting Parties, each party to the conflict shall be bound to apply, as a minimum, the following provisions. Regardless of time and place, the following acts are prohibited: (a) violence to life and person, in particular all kinds of murder, maiming, cruel treatment and torture; (b) taking of hostages; (c) outrages upon personal dignity, in particular humiliating and degrading treatment; (d) the passing of sentences and the carrying out of executions without previous judgment pronounced by a regularly constituted court, affording all the judicial guarantees which are recognized as indispensable by civilized peoples.

That is, in armed conflict of non-international character such as the 4·3 Incident, torture of all kinds, murder, maiming, cruel treatment, and abuse are prohibited. However, during the 4·3 Incident, no evidence exists that such international agreements were observed or even partially reflected in practice. Torture is a prime example of abuse of state power and violation of human rights. Torture is not an individual problem but an immoral crime of the state as a whole. If torture persists or torture victims are abandoned, democracy and a human rights state become merely hollow echoes. If the state fails to protect its people from torture, why is the state necessary? One cannot help but feel fundamental doubts about what purpose state power serves.

During the 4·3 Incident, many victims were created through torture perpetrated by state authority. In victim reports received by the Jeju 4·3 Committee, a considerable number of people documented actual torture damage. In particular, among 142 survivors reporting subsequent disability, some 40 testified to suffering from torture. According to investigation by the Committee's testimony investigation team, many who experienced the 4·3 Incident vividly testified to torture damage. Not a few recounted cases of torture they directly experienced. These torture cases clearly illustrate the physical and mental suffering of people subjected to ruthless torment. This report documents such torture practices categorized by several types as cases revealed through testimony. It also reproduces in full the actual contents of victim reports recording the conditions of survivors enduring aftereffect disabilities. By recording these cases vividly, we seek to use them as examples of past human rights violations.

### 1) Types of Torture

#### ① Summary Disposition Procedures

During the 4·3 Incident, torture served merely as a procedure to coerce forced confessions from detainees and carry out summary disposition. Even when individuals were taken into custody due to mistakes or misjudgments by military and police intelligence and surveillance personnel, those who underwent torture were branded as criminals and dragged off to firing squads.

○ Case of Kang Do-hwa, her mother-in-law, and younger brother-in-law - A case where the mother-in-law and younger sister-in-law were detained and tortured on the pretext of contacting a son who had gone into the mountains (testimony from both mother-in-law and brother-in-law):

> "Did she just get beaten? Back then both head and body—they just beat everything. Her head split open and blood gushed out. Thinking about it even now makes living impossible. Even though my mother-in-law had her head beaten like that, bleeding so heavily, I couldn't tend to her wounds. I was too frightened back then. We weren't human beings then. Looking back, we were like mad dogs. But they took our mother-in-law and our younger brother to Yŏllae [Chung-mun-myŏn, Ye-rae-ri] and killed them that very day. After sleeping two nights, on the fourth day. Our younger brother was 24 years old, our mother-in-law was 54. Without the slightest guilt, they just took them and killed them. Thinking about it, even now I can't live with the bitter resentment."[^224]

○ Case of Song Ja-ŭn (testimony of Song Hŭi-ŭn) - A 1948 November case where Shinsan Police Station arrested a young man from Onp'yŏng-ri and tortured him to death:

> "They took him from the Shinsan Police Station, and after only 4 or 5 days took him to Sŏngsan Port and killed him. Both were arrested together, both killed together, and the bodies were handled together. Song Ja-ŭn's corpse was in an extremely mutilated state. Whether his face was torn by a knife or what, I don't know, but my brother had a gunshot wound to the chest—he was killed instantly. Looking at the corpse, torture marks were obvious. They had driven nails through both palms. There were marks from nails driven through the hands of the corpse. How much torture had been inflicted."[^225]

#### ② Procedures for Referral to Trial

**[p. 481]**

Detainees during the 4·3 Incident were principally held at military unit bases (in schools), CIC interrogation rooms, police stations, local police substations, and rightist youth organization offices. During the large-scale mass arrests of November-December 1948 and the mass descent period of July 1949 when refugees came down from the mountains, agricultural school tents, distillery plants, temples, and sweet potato storage warehouses were utilized as temporary detention facilities. Among those detained, those who escaped summary disposition were sometimes referred for trial. Before referral for trial, police authorities created false confessions and fabricated evidence through torture during interrogation record-taking procedures. According to testimony from those who survived imprisonment, they consistently point out that they confessed to false facts under police torture. Despite those tried in 4·3 Incident-related cases testifying to false confessions due to torture, no examples can be found where torture facts were accepted by trial courts resulting in sentence reduction or acquittal.

**Police Station Torture**

○ Case of Park Tong-u - A case where a false confession due to torture was submitted to the Supreme Court but rejected:

> "Police detectives abused authority, carrying out electrical torture and torture called 'Kim Il-sŏng Airplane,' depriving six detectives from sleeping even for a moment while interrogating day and night for five days until losing consciousness. Although the defendant denied the allegations, interrogation records were created based on fabricated testimony. Detectives from the Police Bureau Investigation Section Chief Kim Ch'ŏng-ryong received witness statements in the defendant's presence. At the time of first trial after prosecution, the above witness statements were removed and destroyed. The defendant's attorney Kim T'ae-jun re-obtained witness statements at the first trial court. In this manner, the prosecutors and detectives destroyed witness statements favorable to the defendant and did not even investigate witnesses who would appear favorable to the defendant when witnesses were requested. On that day, the investigation officer questioned the defendant, and since the defendant did not respond and denied allegations, the prosecutor heard two or three facts and left. When night fell, five or six detectives gathered, and since the defendant denied the allegations, they applied electrical torture to water and ordered the defendant to write a confession. Since sleep came, he said he couldn't write. They poured water from a kettle and forced him to wash his face, then ordered him to write while looking at the interrogation record, so unable to resist coercion, he wrote an unwilling confession. After this, the Assistant Prosecutor appeared during the daytime and detectives surrounded him while interrogating. Trembling in fear, after much torture and five days of day and night without sleep, he does not remember what he said in response. After being sent to the prosecutor, he denied the allegations in prosecutor's interrogation, but when the prosecutor's clerk insisted they had been present at the Hanllim-ri attack as a police official at the scene, raising enemy feelings and shouting, the defendant received no acceptance for his denial, but through overwhelming power lost consciousness. "[^226]

○ Case of Ko Yun-sŏp - A case where a detainee confessed to false facts through torture at a police station and received a guilty sentence:

> "After hiding in Dae-nao-reum for about two weeks, I heard rumors that suppression had eased and there were calls for surrender. One day soldiers discovered and surrounded our cave, and all of us were arrested. Initially at the Military Police office in Wŏn-jŏng-t'ong, I completed identity verification and since there was no serious crime, I was placed in a major gathering place. After one night, I was dragged to the Inspection Office. A police informant who had a poor relationship with my brother reported me as a suspicious person out of personal malice. While others were released, I underwent torture for more than 10 days. Having sustained gunshot wounds with which I couldn't move, I was subjected to all kinds of torture, leaving me completely out of my mind. I was held in the Cheju Police Station detention cell for about a year, then tried at the Kwangju District Court in February 1950 and sentenced to 7 years. I appealed to the Taegu High Court because I found it unbearably unjust, and was transferred to Taegu Prison. Then when 6·25 began, I was transferred to Pusan Prison, and after Seoul's recovery, transferred again to Taegu where I continued my sentence until June 1956 when I was released from Chinju Prison after completing my term. I abandoned my appeal during the war."[^227]

○ Case of Yang Kyŭ-sŏk - A case where a detainee confessed to false facts through torture at a police station and received a guilty sentence (Case 2):

> "I lived at Sŏguipo Police Station for about a month. Then I was transferred to Cheju Police Station. When I got to Cheju Police Station, they just kept asking me to confess, so I told them the facts. But they wouldn't accept my words. At that time, torture was indescribable. If they disliked even a little, they'd arrest you and beat you. It was a time when you'd get hit if you made even a small verbal mistake. I lived in the Cheju Police Station detention cell for about a month, then they seemed to think there was no need for me to stay longer, so they transferred me to trial. So we went to Kwangju. Torture was something unspeakable. At the Andŏk Substation they beat me with clubs like threshing rice. My entire body turned black. They also inserted a wooden pole between my legs and tortured me that way. At Sŏguipo Police Station they used electrical torture. They put electric rings on my fingers, and when the officer pushed a button, fire erupted in my internal organs. They also subjected me to water torture. There was a method of water torture—they'd put a large tub of water and submerge my face in it."[^228]

○ Case of Kang Sŏ-su - A case of prolonged detention as a pre-trial detainee at a police station undergoing torture:

> "(Just as we came out from hiding in a cave in Puk-ch'on-ri) Next to a mud-brick house we built, there was water, so we got water, mixed red pepper powder in a kettle, tied us with handcuffs, suspended us upside down, and forced red pepper water up our noses, stepped on us, and effectively killed us once. Now when I went to the Cheju Police Station, they seized us, put us in a cell, and after a while called us out. They spread cinders on the ground, had us kneel, and police officers just stepped on us. They tortured us like this, then opened the cell door and kicked us with their boots repeatedly. This went on for hours with breaks. I lived three or four months, maybe, at the Cheju Police Station. And they asked if we were married, if we had children, and tortured us. I was subjected to 'raccoon torture,' 'airplane torture.'"[^229]

○ Case of Ko Man-hyŏng - A case where torture led to affixing a seal to a false confession (Case 1):

> "I was taken to Sŏguipo Police Station, subjected to water torture and beaten severely. However I confessed somehow—I don't know how I maintained my composure, but it seems the police wrote that I received help from communists to do these things. At Sŏguipo they primarily used water torture. They'd force-feed water while tying me up, and beat me with rubber-like materials. Iron-wire clubs. When hit, the flesh sticks fast. I was transferred to Cheju Police Station, where they didn't let me sleep and beat me. People died here from torture too. That person from Hahyo, Song somebody-or-other, died from torture. So they investigated there and sent us to the High Military Court-Martial. At that time, they didn't even ask how many years imprisonment or what you did or didn't do—they just forwarded what the police had written. At the Cheju Police Station, they didn't let me sleep, beat me with a long rubber cord on the head during interrogation, and didn't give me food. They subjected me to water torture without letting me sleep. Whenever I tried to sleep, they beat me with that iron-wire club. My fingers—at the Cheju Police Station during torture they twisted my wrists so badly my wrists became twisted. I had nothing to hide. The seal was forcibly affixed by the police officers. They admitted to things I didn't do."[^230]

**[p. 482]**

○ Case of Kang Cho-haeng - A case where torture led to affixing a seal to a false confession (Case 2):

> "About 50 Kose-ri residents were taken to the Cheju Police Station. As we got out of the truck, we went to the furthest part of the cell. Those near the entrance were beaten severely by various people. One person was hit five times by a club-wielder, then another person took over when the first got tired. Every day we were interrogated about the Siŭm Substation attack, arson, and murder. They just kept saying 'confess to the attack.' If you haven't gone somewhere, why would you keep saying 'confess'? What can you say? They beat us, then also subjected me to electrical torture. At that time I only knew about putting electricity on my fingers—I didn't know what came next. The person from the neighborhood said, after I was released and came out, 'that person died and then came back to life.' It was said an American torture examiner came in and saved him.' I had no memory of the electrical torture. I had lost consciousness. I underwent such interrogation for 15 days. I just confessed to whatever they said to write and affixed the seal, so I became implicated in the Siŭm Substation attack. Afterward when I underwent prosecutor's interrogation, I absolutely said I hadn't done it. I told the prosecutor that the police just ordered me to write whatever and affix the seal—that's all I had done. The prosecutor apparently just recorded it as written."[^231]

○ Case of Kim Ok-nyŏ - A case where torture led to affixing a seal to a false confession (Case 3):

> "I didn't think about resisting when I went to trial. I just thought what I said during investigation was what I had to do. If I said I couldn't withstand torture and that's why I did it, my punishment might be lighter—but I couldn't think that way, I just did what I said there. I only thought 'I shouldn't lie.' I said 'I did it.' If someone asked 'did you kill a person,' I would have said 'I killed him.' That's how it was back then. I was very foolish too. Even at trial, I should have said I didn't do it. How could I say I didn't do it when they stripped all my clothes and tortured me severely? When they subjected me to electrical torture like that, I said I couldn't bear children anymore. But later my mother made good medicine for me, and fortunately I managed to have children. Due to beatings with an iron club at that time, even now both sides of my lower back stick out. Both sides of my buttocks hurt."[^232]

○ Case of Chong Dong-sŏk - A case where despite police torture, the individual did not confess to false facts:

> "I entered Cheju Police Station Cell 5. When I entered, I saw about 30 people in a cell of roughly 2 pyŏng. We sat with our knees raised. It was mid-June 1948. It was hot so we breathed through our mouths. When interrogation began, they said we had cut down the telephone pole in Kwakji and blocked the road. They immediately began beating, and also conducted electrical interrogation. At that time, coincidentally, about 50 Japanese-era detective-form investigators were dispatched from the center. They interrogated me. I was tortured for a week. They suspended me with rope tied around me and poured water, wrapped wire around my hands and subjected me to electrical interrogation, and broke wood on me. I was in good health with a strong build. If I had been weak, that beating would have killed me. I was interrogated at the Judo hall where blood flowed and the smell of fresh blood was overwhelming. Wounded people were covered with mats. It was a pitiful sight. I spoke the truth and was completely ignorant of the situation. Since I lived in Cheju town proper, I couldn't know about the telephone pole cutting in Kwakji or road blocking. Yet I was tortured on the assumption that I had done it. I couldn't endure such suffering. My whole body swelled with blood bruises turning pitch black. With blood gathering in two places, pus formed. The Red Cross hospital was at the site of the Chungang Inn. I went there for surgery and was carried around undergoing torture while recovering."[^233]

#### □ Torture in Agricultural Schools, Distilleries, and Other Detention Facilities

**[p. 483]**

In late 1948 and from March to June 1949, people detained in agricultural school tents, distillery plants, temples, and sweet potato storage warehouses underwent military court-martial proceedings without trial procedures. Without legal trial review process, police torture-accompanied interrogation records alone were used to condemn these people as criminals. Interrogations at temporary detention facilities were directly conducted by military officers from unit intelligence sections and police surveillance detectives. Using these interrogation results, police arbitrarily created criminal status reports. At the time, for refugees who came down from the mountains detained at the Cheju distillery, temple, and sweet potato warehouse, surveillance-assigned police established separate investigation team offices and conducted interrogations. The police officer who served as special investigation team leader and conducted interrogations at the distillery testified: "When we tortured, since they were hometown people, we tried to avoid it when possible. Police who came from the mainland tortured frequently. There were various torture methods—some police stripped women naked and suspended them from the ceiling to torture them."[^234] Police subjected detainees to all manner of torture to force false confessions of connection with armed guerrillas, compiled interrogation records, and transferred them to the military.

○ Case of Kang Sang-mun:

> "I underwent interrogation and lost consciousness several times before coming to. Interrogation isn't much—it's just individuals, out of personal feelings, arresting and beating people. The record-writing is done at their discretion. The police interrogate while the military just watches. They write at their own discretion. Looking back calmly at the sentence I received this way, I don't think the sentence was determined by that person's actual guilt. There were people who had been armed guerrillas in the mountains, and completely ignorant people from the village who just dug earth are mixed together. There's no distinction. So I think they just roughly filled the quota of people."[^235]

○ Case of Kang Sun-ju:

> "The neighbors detained at the Agricultural School, unable to withstand torture, dragged us in as well. Later when I received an interrogation at the Tŏngch'ŏk Company, those people had expressions like Yŏmna Taewang [King of Hell] had sent them to bring someone. They asked me what I had done—I said I hadn't done anything. Then they said 'aren't you lying?' 'I am innocent. Why would I lie about anything?' they said, suspending me from a desk and removing the desk—torture in midair. Then later they subjected me to electrical torture. Even with electrical torture, since I hadn't actually done anything, I thought: rather than endure this pain, let them kill me, and I said 'kill me.' How could I say I had done something I hadn't done?"[^236]

○ Case of Kim Ch'un-bae:

> "I was arrested around October 1948. It was an extremely frightening time back then. The Agricultural School had dozens of tents where people were detained. We saw many people dead from torture. Once interrogation ended, people went into the tents and couldn't get up. Eventually they couldn't get up and died. The interrogators seemed drunk or something—they didn't appear to be in their right minds. They smelled bad. A company commander seemed to be in charge. They asked if I had distributed leaflets. My head wasn't beaten and I didn't get tortured with leaflets. I also underwent torture there, and for two days I couldn't get up. I thought I was dying. They forcibly confessed to joining the Citizens' League under torture."[^237]

○ Case of Yang Kong-ok:

> "(At the Cheju Police Station) I underwent electrical torture severely. So from the aftereffect now my back hurts—I can't use my back and I'm receiving hospital treatment these days. They beat me with wooden sticks. Electrical interrogation was when they tied an electrical cord around you and you'd get shocked—your nerves, your organs would be paralyzed. If you underwent interrogation for an hour, a person's limbs would swell up. There were people who insisted they hadn't done anything wrong and endured and got out, and there were many who died from beating because they said it was false. Because it was said if you lied you'd die, I thought 'if I want to live without dying, shouldn't I confess to at least this level?' and so I confessed."[^238]

#### ③ Procedures for Arresting Accomplices

**□ Interrogation to Track Down Fugitives**

○ Case of Kim Gye-saeng (testimony of younger brother Kim Kap-kil) - Interrogation to determine whereabouts of a younger brother who had fled to Japan:

> "Our older sister thought that if you cut her flesh she would tell the truth, but she just kept saying she didn't know. Her flesh was torn and later she was dragged and killed by her at Chŏngbang Waterfall. I saw flesh cut away. Going there was indescribable. Her thighs and such were cut everywhere—how much."[^239]

**□ Interrogation to Obtain Names of Accomplices**

○ Case of Yang Kyŏng-su:

> "After coming down from being scattered here and there, exhausted, I made up my mind to volunteer for the police. I knew someone with quite a high position at the Sŏguipo Police Station, and since he had encouraged me, I went directly. But the moment I arrived at the police station, screams reached my ears, and all kinds of torture were unbearable to watch with open eyes. Seeing that scene, my heart completely sank and I gave up on the police volunteer plan. Women were immediately stripped completely and torture began. The women were stripped, beaten, and interrogation began. At that time there was a popular saying 'don't let your name be taken.' That is, when someone was being dragged away, if you went ahead or were nearby, don't let yourself remain in that person's memory. There's no escaping the beating. When people are subjected to harsh torture, any name can pop out of your mouth. Then that person becomes labeled a rebel."[^240]

#### ④ Torture Methods

**□ Various Torture Methods**

○ Case of Kim Saeng-min:

> "I was arrested around January 1948. No matter who says they underwent what torture and how much, I probably underwent more than anyone. Living is a miracle. I was tortured over a full week. I was tortured starting from home. They inserted wood blocks between my legs and two people sat on each end and sawed back and forth like a seesaw to torture. They laid clam shells on the ground and had me sit on them until my bones were damaged. At the Choch'ŏn Substation I was beaten with an iron club, subjected to severe water torture, and brought nearly to death. Then at the Cheju Police Station I was tortured without sleep. The types of torture I experienced were tremendous—airplane riding, electrical torture, hot water torture—indescribably numerous. It wasn't done all at once but continuously every day without rest. Later I was bloated to such extent that my skin tore and ripped."[^241]

**[p. 484]**

**□ Water Torture**

○ Case of Kim Pong-ik:

> "I spent 40 days at the police station detention cell. Torture was extremely severe back then. They suspended me upside down and poured water from a kettle into my nose. So even if you hadn't actually committed leftist activities, in a moment of suffering you'd confess you had done them. To escape the pain."[^242]

○ Case of Kim Kyŭ-baek:

> "After going down to Ora 3-gu I was arrested by police in three days. I was arrested at what they called the Chil-sŏng-t'ong mobile unit base at Banghilchŏm. Water torture was being conducted at the mobile unit office. Seeing that, I thought dying by gunshot would be better. They conducted water torture by forcing water up my nose while interrogating. Inside there were over 100 people arrested. They beat people in turn with clubs without even asking right or wrong."[^243]

**□ Electrical Torture**

○ Case of wife of Paek Ch'ang-wŏn (testimony of Kim Gye-sun):

> "At Siŭm there was a person named Paek Ch'ang-wŏn engaged in leftist activities. His wife had given birth to a daughter and was in the mountains. Paek Ch'ang-wŏn was shot dead and his wife was arrested. Police interrogated relentlessly in Japanese fashion. Stripping her completely naked, they subjected her to electrical interrogation almost daily. When a person undergoes electrical torture two or three times daily, one can't tell if they're dying or living."[^244]

○ Case of Shin T'ae-ho:

> "5 or 6 soldiers and I went down to Hamŭk. That night they conducted torture interrogation. Now electrical interrogation is done with electricity, but back then they wrapped iron wire around my fingers. When they let the iron wire go and spun it from there, a person would just fall and spin."[^245]

○ Case of Shin Su-yong (testimony of Shin Su-yong):
