> "When my older brother was taken in the 3·1 Incident, he underwent electrical torture. Electrical torture—now there's a telephone rotating device, there's a military one. They call it a 228 telephone. When you tie it and spin it, electricity goes crashing through."[^246]

○ Case of Kang T'ae-jung:

> "Torture came in many types. I also underwent much torture. On the first lunar day of 1949, I was arrested at the Hagŭi police substation and underwent electrical torture. They removed telephone wire. Field telephone wire. I thought to myself, 'So this is electrical torture.' I had only heard the name; I didn't know what it actually was. I said I was going to the bathroom. They said go ahead. Thinking I might make a mistake, I did. When they spun the telephone wire, in one minute I'd collapse. Then I'd bite my tongue and fall. Around five minutes they'd hit me to wake up, then apply electricity again. They repeated this 5 or 6 times."[^247]

**□ Beatings**

○ Testimony of Kim Ch'ang-sŏk:

> "Around December '48 I was also taken to the Andŭk Substation. At that time the support unit did the interrogating. From the support unit I was slapped so hard my teeth broke, and one molar fell out. Blood poured from my mouth. At that office when they were heating the stove with pine wood, the platoon commander struck me with a stick so hard my neck tore and blood covered the office. Then I lost consciousness. That's how they put me in the detention cell. I couldn't walk and couldn't move a single finger or toe. Even now I can't use this leg and I can't use one arm. Back then it was fine, but I got cerebral infarction and now I can't use my leg and arm well and my head is dizzy."[^248]

○ Testimony of Kim Ki-saeng:

> "At the Tŏngch'ŏk Company, they conducted interrogation. Uniformed officers seized people on both sides and beat them with this-sized clubs. They beat everywhere without regard. Some people were beaten nearly to death."[^249]

○ Testimony of Kim In-pyŏng (January 22, 1948):

> "In the middle of the night I was taken into custody by police, loaded on a truck, taken to the Inspection Office, and beaten nearly to death. I lived for ten days and came out; my younger brother lived for two weeks and came out. I underwent every kind of torture. After putting Soviet airplanes on me and bringing me down, they said 'Did you join the South Korean Workers' Party or didn't you?' They brought two pine wood sticks and beat me relentlessly, then took out something with a seal on it and said 'You see this joining the South Korean Workers' Party? Why won't you speak truthfully?' It was a South Korean Workers' Party membership application. I had never affixed a seal and had no idea what the South Korean Workers' Party or the North Korean Workers' Party were at that time. Even now I bear a grudge. Back then my shoulder broke. I didn't die so I lived. When beaten with wood sticks, something called the shoulder blade cracked and I suffered for several years."[^250]

○ Case of Yang Kwan-pyo (testimony of Ko Kyŏng-hŭp):

> "Yang Kwan-pyo didn't go into the mountains. The rest of the people fled to Japan and such. But Yang Kwan-pyo was arrested by a special company made up of the Northwest Youth Society. I was also arrested around the same time. They made barriers in every classroom at the elementary school to create interrogation rooms—if you put your ear to the wall you could hear everything. The people under suspicion were beaten so badly their bodies couldn't move, packed in there. I witnessed the scene myself, and it was tear-jerking—I can't speak. That scene! How could fellow Koreans do that to each other? The Northwest Youth just beat with clubs so bones broke. So when people were dying it was like that—breathing rough and rattling. When lying down, lifting an arm, it was wobbly and wobbly. His bones were all broken. Just beating with fresh wood, beating and hitting so hard. (Yang Kwan-pyo died from this torture)."[^251]

**[p. 485]**

**□ Airplane Torture**

○ Case of Kang In-ok:

> "There was a sweet potato plant run by Han Jae-kil. I went there and underwent torture. They said 'It's not Kim Il-sŏng airplane, but since you're a unit commander, you should ride the Stalin airplane,' and suspended me upside down. Suspended upside down wearing only underwear, they beat me—how could blood not spurt? So even now I'm going to the hospital because of that. My head gets dizzy because of it. They beat my head with a wooden club, so while legs and back and such were okay since I was young back then, my head still has aftereffects from that beating back then."[^252]

#### ⑤ Women's Torture

○ Case of Yi Ch'un-hyŏng:

> "Around January 1949, I and several others were dragged to the police station and military unit. At the police station they tied my hands behind my back, suspended me from the ceiling, and beat me. After being transferred to the military unit, I underwent electrical torture by placing electrical wire on my thumb or cheek."[^253]

○ Case of woman daughter-in-law of Ko Nan-hyang:

> "I was 42 years old then. The police officer immediately demanded, 'Where did your husband go? He went to commit rebellion, didn't he?' My husband stayed in Japan after liberation. Even when I said that, fists came. While the police were frightened, my daughter-in-law hiding in the inner room was put on a stool, a long stick of wood was placed on her belly, and two people sat on the ends of the stick saying 'Tell where your husband went' and tortured her. I was also hit on my cheek while I tried to help. It was truly incredible."[^254]

○ Case of Chwae (Joa-bong case):

> "On May 30, 1948, the support police unit raided Hanlim-myeon-cheong 2-gu. They dragged out the villagers and assembled them in the schoolyard. 'Communist bastards,' they said and beat them severely. 'The suppression forces beat with large wood sticks mercilessly. Then whether women or men, they stripped everyone naked. I was 40 years old at the time—was there any shame to preserve? They just said to take off clothes so I had to take them off. The suppression forces beat them with sticks while stripped. Then after tiring of this, after a while they singled out a single young unmarried woman and a bachelor, brought them to the front of everyone, and forced them to engage in sexual acts in front of all the people. They weren't human beings. Then as day turned to dusk, they dragged 4 residents and shot them dead."[^255]

○ Testimony case of Kim Ho-gyŏm:

> "Around November 1948 I was on night duty at the police station when I heard a woman's scream. I couldn't sleep at all. Going to the interrogation room, there was a naked woman suspended upside down undergoing torture. I went with a Japanese sword in hand to express my anger. The investigation team member conducting the interrogation ran away."[^256]

○ Case of Ch'ŏng Sun-hŭi:

> "When my second older brother went missing, we were labeled a 'bandit family.' My mother, older sister, and I were dragged away by the suppression forces and subjected to unspeakable torture. They stripped us and suspended us upside down, pouring red-pepper-water up our noses and into our mouths. When I clamped my mouth shut, metal instruments were used to forcibly pry my teeth apart causing them all to break."[^257]

○ Case of Ch'a Kyŏng-gu:

> "I was confined for two weeks near 'Kŭsŭn-saem-oreum' and underwent much torture. With a club shoved through my hands from behind, I was forced to kneel while they stepped on me from above. Even now I can't walk well. I was pregnant at the time. I begged saying I was pregnant, but it didn't help. I eventually miscarried."[^258]

○ Testimony case of Hong Kyŏng-t'o:

> "Inside the warehouse were people from several villages. Along with merciless beating, unspeakable scenes took place. They called out men and women, beat them, and forced them into sexual acts. Women's genitals were burned with fire. At night the putrid smell was so bad we couldn't sleep. I don't believe those people possessed their right minds."[^259]

○ Testimony case of Ko Sŏng-jung:

> "(At Sŏngsan Port) Near the distillery warehouse, women's and girls' screams never ceased. The Northwest Youth assaulted the women and then pushed sweet potatoes into them while laughing."[^260]

○ Case of Han Kyŏng-ja (wife of Kim Min-hak):

> "My husband Han Kyŏng-ja during the 4·3 had her breasts burned with fire by the Northwest Youth."[^261]

○ Case of wife of Yi Sam-ryong (testimony of Yi Sam-ryong):

> "Around mid-February 1948, around the time I was arrested, my wife was also dragged to the police station and tortured. Due to this, she miscarried the fetus."[^262]

### 2) Torture and Beating Aftereffect Disabilities

Among 135 aftereffect disability reporters to the 4·3 Committee, 40 victims suffered harm from torture and beating. Representative cases (name, then-residence, birth year) are introduced as follows:

○ Chae Gye-sun (Cheju City, Idŏ-2-dong, born 1925): In December 1948, told there would be a school speech and to gather with neighbors, after going out, arrested by police on suspicion of providing food and information to armed guerrillas. At six months pregnant at the time, due to considerable torture and beating, miscarried at eight months. Suffering from aftereffects for 50 years, enduring physical and mental pain.

○ Chŏng T'ae-hwa (Sŏngsan-ŭp, Kosung-ri, born 1930): While living with family farming at Sinyang-ri, in January 1948 Cheju Police detectives came to his home and detained him at the Cheju City Prosecutor's detention facility on charges of being a South Korean Workers' Party liaison contact. During 52 days of interrogation, severe torture weakened his body and incurable pulmonary tuberculosis developed. After 5-6 years of treatment, continued recurrence leaving no cure to date, suffering from aftereffects.

○ Im Bok-sun (Kugua-ŭp, Kimnyŏng-ri, born 1923): While operating a general goods shop in Kimnyŏng-ri on November 21, 1948, forcibly taken by the Northwest Youth Society to Kimnyŏng Substation and severely beaten, sustaining gunshot wounds to the left leg.

○ Yi Yŏng-ho (Hanlim-ŭp, Kwidŏk-ri, born 1928): As liaison for the Citizens' League, on December 5, 1948 went to Hanlim Substation to report a village attack by armed guerrillas, but the suppression forces said the guerrillas' accomplice and tortured extensively. On December 8 of the same year, shot in a field south of Suwŏn Elementary School but fortunately sustained only hand injury and survived.

○ Yi Pok-hŭi (Taejŏng-ŭp, Hamŏ-ri, born 1932): Her brother Yi Tong-wŏn who died at Sŏch'al-oreum went missing, so military personnel dragged her to Daech'on Military Camp, demanding her to tell her brother's whereabouts while beating and striking, forcing red-pepper-water into her nose and also subjecting her to electrical torture. During ten days of torture while imprisoned at Daech'on Military Camp, she now suffers trembling limbs and feels as if something is crawling on her body.

**[p. 486]**

○ Case of Kim Ok-nyŏ
- Case of placing a seal on a false confession due to torture 3

I could not endure it even at trial. I simply thought I had to go along with what was said during the investigation. If I had mentioned that I suffered such things because I could not resist torture, the sin would have been lighter, but I could not think that way, so I only went along with what was said there. I simply thought that I should not tell lies.

I said "I did it." If they asked "Did you kill a person?" I was ready to say "I killed." That was how it was back then. I was very foolish too. I should have said I didn't do it even at trial. But how could I have said I didn't do it when my clothes were all stripped off and I was subjected to severe torture? Because I underwent electric torture like that, I even said I could not bear children. But later my mother treated me well with medicine and somehow I was able to bear a child. Now, from the beating I received with an iron rod back then, both sides of my lower back have protruded. Both sides of my buttocks ache.[^1]

○ Case of Chang Tong-sŏk
- Case of not admitting false facts despite police torture

I entered the fifth detention room at the Cheju Police Station. When I entered, I saw that about 30 people were sitting in a room of about 2 pyŏng. We sat with our knees raised. It was mid-June 1948. It was hot so I breathed through my mouth. When the interrogation began, they said that I had cut down utility poles and blocked roads in Kwakchi and started beating me right away, and they also gave electric interrogation. At that time, about 50 detectives from the center during the Japanese period had been dispatched. They interrogated me. I was tortured for a week. They tied me up and poured water, wrapped wires around my hands and gave me electric interrogation, and beat me with firewood. I was healthy and had a good build. If I had been weak, I would probably have died on those blows. I received interrogation at the judo hall, blood came out and the smell of fresh blood permeated the air. Wounded people were covered with burlap. It was a miserable reality. I spoke the truth and truly had no knowledge of the circumstances. Since I lived in the town of Cheju, I could not know about the severing of utility poles or the road blockade in Kwakchi. But I was tortured on the charge that I had done it. I could not endure the pain. My entire body became swollen with blood, turning completely black. When blood pooled in two places, an abscess formed. There was a Red Cross Hospital where the Central Inn used to be. I went there and had surgery and was carried around receiving torture.[^2]

**[p. 487]**

□ Torture in Detention Centers such as the Agricultural School and Distillery

At the end of 1948 and from March to June 1949, people detained in the agricultural school tent, the distillery cellar, sweet potato storage, and other facilities were summarily processed in the form of a military court-martial without any trial procedure. Without any legal trial process, they were driven into becoming criminals based only on confession documents obtained through police interrogation accompanied by torture.

Interrogations at the temporary detention center were conducted directly by military personnel attached to the army information section and detectives from the surveillance division. On the basis of these interrogation results, the police arbitrarily prepared criminal status reports. At that time, with regard to those detained in the distillery cellar and sweet potato storage in Cheju town, police officers in charge of surveillance established a separate investigation unit office and conducted interrogations. A police officer who served as the special investigation unit head at that time and handled interrogations at the distillery testified: "We tried to avoid torture when possible because they were fellow townspeople. Police from the mainland tortured a lot. There were several torture methods. Some police stripped the women's clothes and hung them from the ceiling to torture them."[^3] The police conducted all manner of torture on the detainees, forcing them to confess connections with the armed guerrillas, and submitted the confession documents to the military.

○ Case of Kang Sang-mun

I was interrogated and lost consciousness several times but came to. Interrogation is nothing special. They just grabbed you out of personal grudge and beat you, but they write the statement however they please. The police interrogated, and the military just watched. They write as they please. When I think calmly about having received a sentence this way, I do not believe the sentence was determined based on that person's crime. Some people participated as armed guerrillas in the mountains, and some people who were completely ignorant in the village and just did farm work were also included. There was no distinction. So I believe they simply filled the number of people required without any real basis.[^4]

○ Case of Kang Sun-ju

Those fellow villagers detained at the agricultural school could not bear torture and dragged us in too. Later when I received a statement at the Tongjik Company, they had expressions as if Yama King had sent them to seize us. When they asked me what I had done, I said I had done nothing. Then they said, "Aren't you telling lies?" I said, "I am innocent. Why would I tell a lie?" And they hung me on the desk and removed the desk—torture by suspension in mid-air. Then later they applied electric torture. Even with electric torture, since I had done nothing, I said rather than do it that way, let them kill me.[^5]

○ Case of Kim Chun-bae

I was arrested around October 1948. It was a very terrifying time. They pitched dozens of tents at the agricultural school and detained people there. Many people tortured to death appeared before our eyes. Once interrogation ended and went into the tent, people could not get up. As a result, they died unable to get up. Whether the interrogators had been drinking or whatever, they did not appear to be in a normal state of mind. There was a smell. It seemed Lieutenant Colonel Tak was the commanding officer. They asked if I had distributed leaflets. I was tortured there too, and for two days I could not get up. I thought I was dying. I was forced to confess that through torture I had joined the Democratic Youth Association.[^6]

○ Case of Yang Gong-ok

(At the Cheju Police Station) I received severe electric torture. As an aftereffect I now have lower back pain and cannot use my lower back, and I am currently receiving treatment at a hospital. They beat me with wooden clubs. In electric interrogation, they wrapped an electric cord around and rotated it, and the nerves and organs were paralyzed. If I was interrogated for an hour, a person would swell up. Some people without having done anything wrong endured and got through, but many people who resisted saying they had done nothing were beaten to death. They said if you tell lies like that you will die. So thinking "if I want to live without dying, shouldn't I say things to this extent," I made a confession.[^7]

③ Interrogation Procedures for Apprehending Related Persons

□ Interrogation for Apprehending Fugitives

○ Case of Kim Gye-saeng (Testimony of younger brother Kim Kap-gil)
- Interrogation to confirm the whereabouts of younger brother who had fled to Japan

Elder sister (Kim Gye-saeng) would speak the truth if we cut her flesh, but she said she did not know. Her flesh was torn, and later they took her and killed her at Chŏngbang Waterfall. I saw flesh being carved out. When I went and saw it, it was indescribable. Her thighs were sliced everywhere.[^8]

□ Interrogation to Extract Names of Related Persons

○ Case of Yang Kyŏng-su

After I came down from the mountains, I was tormented this way and that, and tired out, I made up my mind to volunteer for the police. I knew someone with a fairly high position at the Sŏguipo Police Station, and since he suggested it, I went there right away. But as soon as I arrived at the police station, screams tore at my ears, and all kinds of torture were unbearable to watch. Seeing that spectacle, my heart sank and I gave up my plan to volunteer for the police. Women were stripped naked all at once and torture began. At that time there was a slang phrase: "Don't let your name be taken." That meant when a person was being dragged away, you should go ahead of him or stay near him so that your name would not remain in his memory. In beating, there is no expert. When subjected to severe torture, any name comes spilling out. So suddenly you become a rebel.[^9]

④ Methods of Torture

□ Various Torture Methods

○ Case of Kim Saeng-min

I was arrested around January 1948. Even if someone says who received what torture and how much, there is probably no one who received as much as I did. My survival is a miracle. I was tortured over a full week. The torture began when I was grabbed at home. They stuck a wooden block between my legs and two people sat on each side and rocked like a seesaw to torture me. They spread conch shells on the ground, sat me on them, and all my bones were damaged. When I went to Choji Police Box, they beat me with an iron rod, applied water torture tremendously, and I was beaten almost to death. Then at the Cheju Police Station they tortured me without letting me sleep. To mention the kinds of torture, I suffered enormous torture—airplane torture, electric torture, scalding water torture, and more that I cannot describe. And it was not done all at once but continuously without rest every day. Later they swelled me up so much that my skin split open.[^10]

**[p. 488]**

○ Case of Strong Sun-ju (continued from previous page)

I said I am innocent. I am not guilty of anything. But they hung me on the desk and removed the desk—suspension torture. Later they applied electric torture. Even receiving electric torture, since I had genuinely done nothing, "I would rather be killed than confess to something I did not do," I said.[^5]

**[p. 489]**

When my older sister (Kim Kye-saeng) cut the flesh, I thought she would tell the truth, but she only said she didn't know. The flesh was torn, and later they took her and killed her at Chŏngbang Falls. I saw flesh being carved away. When I went to see, it was indescribable. I don't know what they used to carve away the flesh, but her thighs and elsewhere were slashed all over.[^239]

**Extracting Admissions of Related Persons' Names During Interrogation**

**Case of Yang Kyŏng-su**

After I came down from the mountains, I was harassed here and there, and worn out, so I made up my mind to volunteer for the police. I knew someone who held quite a high position at the Sŏgwipo Police Station, and when he happened to recommend it, I went there right away. But the moment I arrived at the police station, screaming tore my ears, and all kinds of torture was unbearable to witness with open eyes. Seeing that scene, my heart completely sank, and I gave up my plan to volunteer for the police. Women were stripped completely naked first, and then torture began. At that time, there was a popular saying: "Don't let your name be taken away." That is, when someone was being dragged away, you were supposed to stay away or keep your distance so that they wouldn't remember your name. There's no escaping a beating. When subjected to severe torture, any name at all will come pouring out. Then you immediately become a "rebel."[^240]

**Various Torture Methods**

**Case of Kim Saeng-min**

I was arrested around January 1948. No matter who says they were tortured and how much, I doubt anyone was tortured as much as I was. It's a miracle I'm alive. I was tortured continuously for a full week. I was seized at home and torture began. They inserted a wooden block between my legs, and two people sat on either side and rode it like a seesaw to torture me. They spread conch shells on the floor and sat me on them, and my bones were all damaged. When I went to the Choch'ŏn Police Substation, they beat me with an iron pipe, and they water-tortured me tremendously. In short, I was tortured nearly to death. Then I was taken to the Cheju Police Station and subjected to torture without sleep. As for the types of torture—I experienced tremendous torture: riding an airplane, electric torture, hot water torture—it's indescribable. Moreover, they didn't do it all at once but did it every day without rest. Later, I was swollen so much that my skin became taut and would split wide open.[^241]

[^239]: Kim Kap-kil (79 years old, Pongae-dong, Cheju City, Buddhist believer at the time, recorded April 23, 2002). Testimony.

[^240]: Jemin Daily News 4·3 News Investigation Team, 4·3 Speaks ⑤, Jŏnyewŏn, 1998, pp. 158-159.

[^241]: Kim Saeng-min (77 years old, Yŏn-dong, Cheju City, contact person for the organizational department of the South Korean Labor Party, Cheju Island branch, at the time, recorded July 13, 2001). Testimony.

---

**[p. 490]**

□ Water Torture

○ Case of Kim Pong-ik

I spent 40 days in the police station detention room. The torture was severe then. They hung me upside down and poured water from a kettle into my nose. So even if someone had not actually (engaged in leftist activities), to escape momentary suffering, they would say they had done it. To escape the pain.[^11]

○ Case of Kim Kyŏ-baek

After I went down to Ura 3-ku, three days later I was caught by police. I was caught and taken to what was called the "advanced squad" at Chilsŏng Station. They were applying water torture at the advanced squad office. Seeing that, I thought it was better to be shot dead. They poured water into my nose while interrogating. More than 100 people were detained inside. They beat people in turn with clubs. They did not even ask what crimes you had committed.[^12]

□ Electric Torture

○ Case of wife of Paek Chang-wŏn (testimony of Kim Gye-sun)

There was a person named Paek Chang-wŏn in Shin'am who had engaged in leftist activities. His wife had borne a daughter and was in the mountains. Paek Chang-wŏn was shot and killed, and his wife was arrested. The police interrogated her in the brutal way of the Japanese. Her clothes were stripped completely and almost daily electric interrogation was applied. If a person received electric interrogation two or three times a day, no one knew whether they would die or live.[^13]

○ Case of Sin Tae-ho

Five or six soldiers and I went down to Hamdŏk. That night they conducted torture interrogation. Now electric interrogation is done with electricity, but back then they wrapped iron wire around my fingers. When they wound the wire and twisted it over there, a person would just collapse and spin around.[^14]

○ Case of Sin Su-yong brother (testimony of Sin Su-yong)

My brother was taken away during the March 1st Incident. He received electric torture. Electric torture—there is now something like turning a telephone dial, but there is a military one. Model 228 telephone—when you wind it, electricity comes out suddenly.[^15]

○ Case of Kang Tae-jung

There were several kinds of torture. I experienced many tortures too. On the lunar new year's day of 1949, I was caught and taken to the Hagwi Police Box, where I received electric torture. They removed a telephone wire. A field telephone wire. Internally I thought "it is electric torture." I just heard the name but did not know what it was. When I said I was going to the bathroom they said "go ahead." I was worried I might make a mistake. When they turned the telephone wire, in one minute you collapse. Then you bite your tongue and fall. After about 5 minutes they beat you to wake you up and turn on the electricity again. This is repeated 5-6 times.[^16]

**[p. 491]**

□ Beatings

○ Testimony of Kim Chang-sŏk

Around December 1948 I was also taken to the Andŏk Police Box. At that time the support corps conducted the interrogation. I was hit so hard on the cheek I broke teeth and lost one molar. Blood poured from my mouth. It was when the stove was being fired with pine wood. A platoon commander beat me hard with firewood. As a result my neck split open and blood filled the office. I lost consciousness at that point. That's how they put me in the detention room, but I could not walk and could not move a single finger or toe. Now this leg cannot be used and one arm cannot be used either. It was fine back then, but I got a stroke and now my leg and arm do not work well and my head is dizzy.[^17]

○ Testimony of Kim Ki-saeng

They conducted interrogation inside the Tongjik Company, and regular police held a person on each side and beat them with a club about this big. They beat everywhere without regard for where. Some people were even beaten to the point of death.[^18]

○ Testimony of Kim In-pyŏng

(January 22, 1948) I was taken away by police at night and put in a truck to the Inspection Office where I was beaten nearly to death. I was there ten days and came out; my younger brother was there fifteen days and came out. I underwent every kind of torture. After they gave the "Soviet airplane" torture and let me down, they talked about "Did you join the South Korean Labor Party or not?" They held two pine logs and beat me, then took out a stamp and said "You have this kind of joining certificate—why don't you tell the truth?" It was a South Korean Labor Party application form. I had never stamped it and at that time I did not know what the South Korean Labor Party or North Korean Labor Party was. Even now my resentment lingers. My shoulder was broken then, and because I did not die, I lived. When beaten with firewood, the scapula cracked and I suffered for years afterward.[^19]

○ Case of Yang Kwan-pyo (testimony of Ko Kyŏng-hŭp)

Yang Kwan-pyo did not go to the mountains. The other people fled to Japan and elsewhere. But Yang Kwan-pyo was caught by the special company made up of Sŏbuk Youth. I was also caught at the same time. Classrooms at the elementary school were partitioned into interrogation rooms, and if you pressed your ear to the wall you could hear everything. The suspects were being beaten, so many could not move their bodies. I actually witnessed the scene but was too tearful to speak. When I see that scene. How could they do this to fellow people? The Sŏbuk Youth simply beat with clubs so bones would break. As a result when people died the breathing was harsh and gurgling. When lying down their arms would dangle when lifted. (Yang Kwan-pyo's) bones were all broken. They just beat him with green wood. (Yang Kwan-pyo died from this torture).[^20]

□ Airplane Torture

○ Case of Kang In-ok

There was a sweet potato factory run by Han Chae-gil. I went there and received torture. While saying I was not riding Kim Il-sŏng's airplane but as a commander you should ride Stalin's airplane, they tied me up. They tied me upside down, dressed only in underwear, and beat me. How could blood not come out? That's why I am still going to hospitals now.

My head is dizzy—it's all because of that. They beat my head with wooden clubs. Legs and back, those kinds of places were fine when I was young back then, but my head that was beaten then still has aftereffects.[^21]

**[p. 492]**

**Testimony of Kim In-p'yŏng**

(January 22, 1948) I was taken by police at night, put in a truck, and went to the Inspection Agency where I was beaten nearly to death. I survived ten days and came out; my younger brother survived fifteen days and came out. I endured every conceivable torture. After they simulated a Soviet airplane ride, they set me down and asked, "Did you join the South Korean Labor Party or not?" They produced two pine logs they had beaten me with, stamped them with a seal, and said, "Here's proof you joined—why won't you tell the truth?" It was a South Korean Labor Party membership application. I had never stamped anything, and I didn't even know what the South Korean Labor Party or North Korean Labor Party was at that time. I'm still bitter about it. At that time my shoulder was broken, and the fact that I didn't die means I lived. When they beat me with logs, what they call the scapula cracked, and I suffered for several years afterward.[^250]

**Case of Yang Kwan-p'yo (Testimony by Ko Kyŏng-hŭp)**

Yang Kwan-p'yo didn't go to the mountains. The rest of the people fled to Japan and elsewhere. But Yang Kwan-p'yo was caught by a special company made up of Sŏbuk Youth Corps members. I was also caught around the same time. In each classroom of the elementary school, they made partitions and created interrogation rooms where you could hear everything if you put your ear to the wall. The suspects were beaten so badly that there were many who couldn't move their bodies. I saw the scene myself, and I can't speak about it for the tears. When you see that scene, how could you do such things to your own people? The Sŏbuk Youth Corps members just beat with clubs, and bones shattered. So people died like that. Their breathing—gargling, gargling. Lying down, even lifting their arms—they hung limp. (Yang Kwan-p'yo's) bones were all shattered. They beat him mercilessly with green wood. (Yang Kwan-p'yo died as a result of this torture).[^251]

**Airplane Torture**

**Case of Kang In-ok**

There was a sweet potato distillery operated by Han Chae-gil. I went there and was tortured. They said, "This isn't Kim Il-sŏng's airplane; you're a commander, so you have to ride Stalin's airplane," and they tied me up. They tied me upside down, and with only my underwear on, they beat me—how could blood not come out? So I still go to the hospital now because of it.

My head has been dizzy about everything because of that. Those guys beat my head with wooden beams, and while my legs and back were okay because I was young then, my head—which they beat at that time—still has aftereffects.[^252]

[^250]: Kim In-p'yŏng (77 years old, Sinch'on-ri, Choch'ŏn-ŭp, ship captain at the time, recorded October 11, 2001). Testimony.

[^251]: Ko Kyŏng-hŭp (88 years old, Hallim-ri, Hallim-ŭp, civil servant at the time, recorded December 14, 2001). Testimony.

[^252]: Kang In-ok (86 years old, Sihŭng-ri, Sŏngsan-ŭp, Min-bo Corps commander of Sihŭng-ri at the time, recorded March 28, 2002). Testimony.

---

**[p. 493]**

⑤ Women Torture

○ Case of Yi Ch'un-hyŏng

In January 1949 I was dragged to the police station and the military police with several other people. At the police station, with my hands bound behind my back, I was hung from the ceiling and beaten. After being transferred to the military police, I underwent electric torture with the cord applied to my thumb or cheek.[^22]

○ Case of widow of Ko Nan-hyang

I was forty-two years old then. A police officer suddenly said "Where did your husband go? He went to engage in banditry," and pressed me. My husband continued to live in Japan even after liberation. Even when I said that, they just punched me. In the meantime the police became afraid and hid, and my daughter-in-law who was hiding was laid on a chair. A long piece of wood was placed on her belly and two men sat on either side of the log and tortured her saying "Tell where your husband went." Rather than doing that to me, I tried to rush forward, and they beat my cheek and continued doing that. It was truly outrageous.[^23]

○ Case of Chwabong

On May 30, 1948 the support police corps raided Hansŭp 2-ku of Hallim-myŏn. They dragged out villagers and assembled them in the elementary school yard. Called them "communist bastards" and beat them severely.

"The suppression forces beat brutally with large logs. Then whether woman or man, they stripped everyone completely naked. I was forty years old at that time—there was no way to preserve dignity. They just ordered me to remove my clothes so I had to remove them. The suppression forces beat us with logs while naked. Then they seemed to tire of it, and after not long they designated one young girl and one unmarried youth and ordered them to come forward, and in front of all the people they forced them to engage in sexual activity. They were not humans. Then as the day grew late they took four residents away and shot them."[^24]

○ Testimony case of Kim Ho-gyŏm

Around November 1948 I was on night watch at the police station when I heard a woman's scream and could not fall asleep. I went to the interrogation room and saw a woman suspended naked and being tortured. I went in with a Japanese sword and expressed my anger, and the investigation unit member ran away.[^25]

○ Case of Chŏng Sun-hŭi

When my second oldest brother went missing, we were labeled a "bandit family." My mother, older sister, and I were taken away by the suppression forces and subjected to unspeakable torture. They stripped our clothes and hung us upside down, pouring red pepper water into our nose and mouth. When we closed our mouths, they forcibly pried our teeth open with metal and all our teeth were broken.[^26]

○ Case of Cha Kyŏng-ku

I was confined to a tent near "Kŏsŭn Saemi Peak" for two weeks and received much torture. A stick was inserted through from behind, forcing me to kneel, and they trampled on me from above. Even now I cannot walk well. I was pregnant at that time. I pleaded that I was pregnant but it did not work. As a result I had a miscarriage.[^27]

○ Testimony case of Hong Kyŏng-to

Several village people were confined in the warehouse. Along with ruthless beatings, scenes unbearable to watch occurred. They called out men and women, beat them, and forced sexual intercourse. They also burned women's genitals with fire. At night with the stench unable to be endured, sleep was impossible. I did not think those people were in their right minds.[^28]

○ Testimony case of Ko Sŏng-jung

(Near the Sŏngsŏng distillery warehouse) The cries of girls and women did not cease. The Sŏbuk Youth assaulted women and then thrust sweet potatoes in, snickering.[^29]

**[p. 494]**

○ Case of Han Kyŏng-ja (wife of Kim Min-hak)

During the 4.3, the Sŏbuk Youth seized wife Han Kyŏng-ja and burned her breasts with fire.[^30]

○ Case of wife of Yi Sam-yong (testimony of Yi Sam-yong)

Around mid-February 1948 when I was about to be arrested, my wife was also taken to the police station where she was tortured. As a result she miscarried the fetus.[^31]

2) Aftereffects of Torture and Beatings

Among the 135 people reporting handicaps from torture and beatings to the 4.3 Incident Commission, 40 are victims of torture and beatings. Representative cases (names, places of residence at the time, birth years) are as follows:

○ Chae Gye-sun (Cheju City, Ido 2-dong, born 1925): In December 1948, people in the neighborhood were summoned to a school assembly, but was arrested by police on suspicion of providing food and information to armed guerrillas. At the time she was six months pregnant, and through considerable torture and beatings, she had a miscarriage after eight months. She has suffered both physical and mental pain for about 50 years from the aftereffects at that time.

○ Chŏng Tae-hwa (Sŏngsan-ŭp, Kosŏng-ri, born 1930): While farming with her family in Sinyang-ri, around January 1948 detectives from the Cheju Police Station came to her house and arrested her at the Cheju City Prosecutor's detention facility on suspicion of being a South Korean Labor Party liaison official. During 52 days of interrogation, severe torture weakened her body, causing tuberculosis, a terminal disease, which she received treatment for over 5-6 years but the condition kept recurring and is still not cured, and she continues to suffer from aftereffects.

○ Im Pok-sun (Kujwa-ŭp, Kimnyŏng-ri, born 1923): While running a general store in Kimnyŏng-ri, she was forcibly taken to Kimnyŏng Police Box by the Sŏbuk Youth on November 21, 1948, subjected to severe beatings, and received a gunshot wound to her left leg.

○ Yi Yŏng-ho (Hallim-ŭp, Kwidŏk-ri, born 1928): As the liaison officer of the Democratic Youth Association, on December 5, 1948, to inform the Hallim Police Box of a village attack by armed guerrillas, but the suppression forces claimed she was a collaborator with the armed guerrillas and subjected her to all kinds of torture. On December 8 of the same year she was shot in a field south of Suwŏn Elementary School but fortunately received only a hand wound and saved her life.

○ Yi Pok-hŭi (Taejŏng-ŭp, Hamori, born 1932): When the dead brother Yi Tong-wŏn went missing at Sŏk'al Peak—

**[p. 495]**

**Case of Han Kyŏng-ja (Wife of Kim Min-hak)**

The wife Han Kyŏng-ja was captured by the Sŏbuk Youth Corps during 4·3 and tortured by burning her breasts with fire.[^261]

**Case of the Wife of Yi Sam-ryong (Testimony by Yi Sam-ryong)**

Around mid-February 1948, when I was about to be arrested, my wife was also dragged to the police station and tortured, and as a result she miscarried.[^262]

**Aftereffects of Torture and Beating**

Among 135 persons with aftereffect disabilities reported to the 4·3 Incident Committee, 40 were victims of torture and beating. Representative cases (name, place of residence at the time, year of birth) are introduced as follows:

**Chae Kye-sun (Ido 2-tong, Cheju City, born 1925):** In December 1948, people were gathered to hear a speech at school, but when I went there, I was taken into police custody on suspicion of providing food and information to armed units and was tortured. At that time, I was six months pregnant, and because of the severe torture and beatings, I miscarried after eight months. As an aftereffect, I have suffered physical and mental pain for more than fifty years.

**Chŏng Tae-hwa (Koseong-ri, Sŏngsan-ŭp, born 1930):** I was living with my family farming in Sinyang-ri when, around January 1948, detectives from the Cheju Police Station came to my home and arrested me at the police prosecution detention facility with the charge of being a contact person for a South Korean Labor Party liaison office. During the 52 days of interrogation that followed, I became weakened from severe torture, and incurable pulmonary tuberculosis developed. Although I received treatment for 5-6 years, it continued to recur, and I have not been cured to this day and continue to suffer from aftereffects.

**Im Bok-sun (Kimnyŏng-ri, Kujwa-ŭp, born 1923):** In November 1948, while operating a general store in Kimnyŏng-ri at the time, I was forcibly taken by the Sŏbuk Youth Corps to the Kimnyŏng Police Substation and subjected to severe beatings and suffered a gunshot wound to my left leg.

**Yi Yŏng-ho (Kwidŏk-ri, Hallim-ŭp, born 1928):** At the time, serving as a contact person for the Min-bo Corps, on December 5, 1948, I went to the Hallim Police Substation to report news of an armed unit attack on the village. The suppression forces accused me of being an accomplice of the armed unit and subjected me to all manner of torture. On December 8 of that year, I was shot in a field south of the Sŭnnam Elementary School, but fortunately suffered only a hand wound and survived.

**Yi Bok-hŭi (Hamŏ-ri, Taejŏng-ŭp, born 1932):** (At the time) regarding the whereabouts of my younger brother Yi Tong-wŏn, who died at Sŏt'al Peak,

[^261]: Jeju 4·3 Research Institute, This is How 4·3 Speaks I, Hanuul Publishing, 1989, p. 59.

[^262]: Yi Sam-ryong (79 years old, Arakawa Ward, Taitō Ward, Tokyo, Japan, leftist organization member at the time, recorded July 11, 2002). Testimony.

---

**[p. 496]**

○ Shin Ch'un-do (Kugua-ŭp, Tongbok-ri, born 1933): In 1948 taken into custody by police, shot in the legs and subjected to severe torture and beating, suffering severe leg pain to this day with daily hospital treatment.

○ Kim Ok-nyŏ (Choch'ŏn-ŭp, Hamgyŏk-ri, born 1927): During the 4·3 living with her mother farming at Hamgyŏk-ri, one day came out of Hamgyŏk Substation and took her mother, a village grandmother, and her son—4 people total—saying her mother and she had provided food to her uncle in the mountains, tortured them asking them to reveal her uncle's whereabouts. Stripped and subjected to electrical torture on fingers and nipples, water torture and beating of her entire body with clubs, fell into unconsciousness and was transferred to the Kwandŏk-jŏng Police Investigation Section. On November 14, 1950 tried and sentenced to three years imprisonment for violation of the National Security Act, imprisoned at Mokp'o Prison.

○ Kim Bok-nam (Sŏngsan-ŭp, Onp'yŏng-ri, born 1940): Around December 25, 1948 (lunar), arrested by the 9th Regiment, beaten on the left eye with a rifle butt by soldiers, causing blindness in the left eye.

○ Kang Sun-dŏk (Choch'ŏn-ŭp, Choch'ŏn-ri, born 1940): At age eight during the 4·3 while hiding with family in a mountain cave, discovered by suppression forces, her left leg and arm became paralyzed while running away through a crowd of people. Dragged by soldiers and beaten, left leg and arm remain paralyzed from aftereffects.

## e. Genealogical Penalty (Yŏnjwa-je)

### 1) Genealogical Penalty Damage Conditions

Another pain of the Jeju 4·3 Incident is that the innocent sacrifice of those who died or went missing during the incident did not end with that generation but was transmitted to their bereaved families. Bereaved families of victims who died or were judicially processed simply because they were killed by military and police suppression forces underwent genealogical penalty, were under surveillance, and faced severe restrictions on social activities. The nightmare of the 4·3 Incident, which left enormous scars on Cheju society, extended for nearly half a century to the bereaved families. Cheju residents and bereaved families of victims, unable to enjoy normal social life due to genealogical penalties lacking legal basis, had to suffer from Red Complex.

Since the modern period, criminal responsibility in law is confined to the perpetrator alone, establishing clear limits. In traditional society before the establishment of modern law, genealogical penalty (yŏnjwa-je) referred to cases where not only the criminal but also parents, wives, children, siblings, and relatives were punished together simply because some family member or close relative had committed a crime.[^263] In Korean society, genealogical penalty was institutionally abolished during the 1894 Kabo Reforms. However, genealogical penalty did not disappear but continued through Japanese colonial surveillance lists and post-liberation "personal background investigations" in the context of inter-Korean ideological confrontation, filtering out "suspicious persons" as a social practice. Mainly carried out through personal background investigation discrimination and surveillance, genealogical penalty functioned as a serious obstacle to victims' social life. The targets of genealogical penalty application were bereaved families of the 4·3 Incident victims and other civilian massacre incidents, families of those who went North or collaborated, and families of those suspected of espionage under the National Security Act. Through genealogical penalty implemented without legal basis, these were the greatest victims of the divided system.

**[p. 497]**

In the case of the 4·3 Incident, by August 1950, 27,000 members of the People's Guidance League and approximately 50,000 incident-related family members were already separately managed and surveilled by surveillance authorities. Separate lists of related persons were maintained by Cheju Police administrative authorities and used as reference materials for various personal background investigations. What police managed but did not destroy and was recently externally leaked—such as execution lists and conviction records lists remaining in each township office—demonstrates this. Execution records lists noted the names of those executed, imprisoned, or transferred by township for 4·3 Incident charges, the date and place of "purge," and separately recorded complete information on bereaved family situations. This list's composition and maintenance purpose was for managing 4·3 Incident family members, namely application of genealogical penalty. When the National Assembly Civilian Massacre Incident Investigation Group visited Cheju on June 6, 1960, to conduct a fact-finding investigation of the 4·3 Incident, bereaved family member Chang Kap-sun stated: "I went as a student soldier, returned with honorable discharge on November 24, 1951. I went to the police station to obtain a teaching credential certificate. When I requested personal identification, the personal identification said 'suspected of leftist ideology.' It was written that way in my household registry. So I couldn't obtain the personal identification."[^263] This was the first public disclosure of genealogical penalty damage conditions. The vast majority of Cheju residents have experience of genealogical penalty damage from the 4·3 Incident. According to a survey conducted on August 13, 2000, by the "Cheju 4·3 Truth Clarification and Honor Recovery Citizens' Alliance" of 75 bereaved families of 4·3 Incident victims, 86% responded that they had experienced genealogical penalty damage. The genealogical penalty damage experienced by these bereaved families was investigated as follows (multiple responses):

| Damage Cases | Ratio (%) |
|---|---|
| Disadvantage in civil service examination | 26 |
| Disadvantage in entrance exams for military academies, etc. | 23 |
| Disadvantage in employment or promotion at public/private enterprises | 18 |
| Disadvantage in promotion, etc., in military/police | 16 |
| Restriction on domestic/foreign travel and border crossing procedures | 8 |
| Surveillance in daily life | 30 |
| Various personal background investigations | 60 |

Such implementation of genealogical penalty expanded and reproduced victim consciousness by adding present suffering on top of the painful memories of the past 4·3 Incident for bereaved families. Since Cheju is a region with strong blood kinship community character, the adverse effects genealogical penalty had on Cheju society were immense. Though apparently late, when the South Korean government recognized the harm of genealogical penalty, on August 1, 1980, the "National Security Emergency Countermeasures Standing Committee" announced that it would systematically organize personal records that had caused harm and inconvenience to the people while abolishing genealogical penalty.[^264] In the announcement at that time, the targets for personal record organization were: ▲records regarding 6·25 made without verification based on error or exaggerated rumors ▲situations of unavoidable collaboration for life preservation or where the crime was minor; ▲cases where whether having such records, the person or family faithfully served the nation upon release ▲other records where present threat to national security is judged weak, etc. On March 24, 1981, the Interior Ministry announced subsequent measures through genealogical penalty abolition directives, officially ending the application of genealogical penalty. Based on these directives, the Cheju Police Department stated it had destroyed materials related to the 4·3 Incident.[^265]

**[p. 498]**

In the Fifth Republic Constitution enacted in 1980, an article stating "No citizen shall be treated unfavorably due to the acts of their relatives rather than their own acts" (Article 12, Section 3) explicitly prohibited genealogical penalty application. The Sixth Republic Constitution reconfirmed genealogical penalty prohibition in Article 13, Section 3, with only minor textual modifications. Despite such legal and institutional prohibition, genealogical penalty harms persist. Not only do customs of personal background investigation in public official appointment remain, but among Cheju residents who directly experienced the 4·3 Incident, some still suspect that genealogical penalty is being implemented. Hong Mun-p'yŏng, including his sons and nephews, says genealogical penalty was applied to his son even during the Civilian Government era, and suspects it continues to be implemented now. During the Kim Young-sam administration, his son couldn't enter ROTC. They said they had something to investigate. He was caught under genealogical penalty. Our family three generations had difficulties. My father died. Not only did I give up being a pilot but couldn't move an inch. My son couldn't enter ROTC. We never received notice that genealogical penalty was abolished. My older cousin wanted to enter the Naval Academy. I talked him out of it. When traveling abroad, there might be problems so I took measures in advance. Though they say genealogical penalty no longer exists, if a personal background investigation is conducted, won't it show up? Red pen writing doesn't get erased, does it?"[^266] Additionally, the following recent case where it was difficult to obtain a visa for foreign travel due to being labeled a 4·3 Incident related person shows that genealogical penalty practices still persist in Korean society. "I went to Beijing in 2001 from a senior citizen university, and I was quite resentful at that time. Other people's visas came through, but mine alone didn't come. They said it was because of a suspected ideologue from the time of 4·3. I spent 5 years and 5 months faithfully in the military, but if they still call me an ideologue now, what should I do? I protested."[^267]

**[p. 499]**

Those who suffered extreme genealogical penalty damage are confident that record cards still remain. Emphasizing that completely destroying these base documents is a prerequisite for their honor recovery, they demand that they be submitted. "Until now what remains unjustly is—my record card has red lines still drawn on it. My older brother is the same, but when honor is recovered, this must be resolved first, and since we suffered unjustly our whole lives, we need to be provided a way to live."[^268]

Through the genealogical penalty damage situation, we confirm that fundamental human rights protection and honor recovery for survivors alongside honor recovery of victims who died from the 4·3 Incident is an urgent task. This report seeks to record such genealogical penalty damage conditions as cases revealed through testimony of victims and bereaved families. By vividly recording these cases, we seek to use this as an occasion for reflection on the errors of past human rights violations and to liquidate the remaining residue of genealogical penalty persisting as a current practice.

### 2) Genealogical Penalty Damage Cases

#### ① Damage from "Security Surveillance" and "Watchlist" Status

Those who underwent trial related to the 4·3 Incident and served time in prison, upon returning alive after completing their sentences, bore the label "security surveillance" or "watchlist" target. Particularly those convicted under rebellion charges or violations of the National Defense Security Law and National Security Act became targets of rigorous surveillance. While returning to their families after completing prison terms, they could not conduct normal social life due to endless public security surveillance, leaving damage consciousness in their families.

○ Case of Kim Ch'un-bae:

> "After I was released from my second imprisonment on August 20, 1963, I went to the police station and they told me to report wherever I went. When I asked how long I had to do this, I remember them saying, 'For people like you, there's probably no statute of limitations. Until you die.' Even during that period and afterward, detectives came by every four days or a week asking how I was doing, where I had been, and interrogating me."[^269]

<Testimony of his daughter Kim A-ja>:

> "From my childhood it went on continuously. They said we were being watched, police were watching us. My mom got so frustrated she asked what was going on—how could someone who was acquitted with no suspicion be continuously treated like this—and demanded proof. She even grabbed people by the collar and made a scene. So those people showed her a document in 1982 that said 'Security Surveillance Target.' Now we have the original (see appendix below). When I made a fuss, they gave it to me then. Looking at it, it said this person had joined the Citizens' League, things like that."

<Appendix: Security Processing Request>:

> The person being requested for security processing (Kim Ch'un-bae) was born February 10, 1926, at original residence Cheju City Pongkae-dong 2067-bŏnji as the eldest son among two sons and two daughters of late father Kim Gye-yŏng. After completing four years at Cheju City Tongbosŏ in 1941, engaged in firewood and lumber trade in Cheju City. In February 1948, joined the Citizens' League at Cheju City Ildo-ri 2-dong. On April 3, 1948, arrested for involvement in the Cheju Rebellion and on December 13, 1948, sentenced to 20-year imprisonment for rebellion by Supreme Military Court-Martial. While serving at Mapo Prison, escaped during 6·25 Chaos and continued hiding in areas like Jŏngan-myŏn, Kongju County, South Ch'ungch'ŏng, until arrested by military authorities on July 20, 1962, re-imprisoned at Mapo Prison. While serving, remaining term was cancelled by Supreme Military Court-Martial decision on August 20, 1963, and released as security processing target. Given that there exists a 20-year long-term sentence fact, continued hiding after 6·25 recovery without voluntary surrender, and re-imprisonment, present unstable living situation as fruit stand vendor after release with no stable occupation, non-participation in anti-communist rally activities and lack of response to national policies, and expression of dissatisfaction that past punishment was unjust, etc., combined assessment shows re-offense danger exists; therefore security processing is deemed necessary.[^270]

○ Case of Yang Kŭn-bang:

> "After we came out and returned to Cheju, we came to our hometown mountain village. Coming back there was a devastating blow. There's Hamŭk Substation, Choch'ŏn Substation, Cheju Police Station Intelligence Section, Surveillance Section—about once a week someone from Choch'ŏn would come, or from Hamŭk, or from the Intelligence Section. At that time, if any spy just came to Seoul, we were the first targets. In Cheju, when there was an investigation, we were treated as special cases first. We're people branded by the state so there's nowhere to complain, and even if we starved to death, among siblings or family, I never disclosed anything. (From the police) there's always vigilance, and wherever I go, I have to report it."[^271]

○ Case of Ko Tae-sŏp (testimony of Ko Pong-wŏn):

> "Death would have been much better. If I had died in prison, I wouldn't have suffered this bitter cruel punishment after coming to society. Among our travel companions, there was a man named Ko Tae-sŏp from Namwŏn-ri. After coming out of prison not long after, the Sŏguipo Police Station told him to come for re-investigation once or twice a year. He went twice then they said they released him and treated him worse than before, so he hanged himself on a pine tree on the upper field."[^272]

○ Case of Yi Mun-gyo:

> "After that I went to the military, but I was a watchlist target person then. I was deployed to the front lines at the 3rd Division as an infantryman, but surveillance extended even there. By the military counterintelligence. So counterintelligence would occasionally come to meet me. They'd come asking if there were complaints, gathering information on trends. They periodically gathered information on trends."[^273]

○ Case of Yang Kyŭ-sŏk:

> "After I served my sentence, close relatives had difficulty doing anything because of me. And when a person serves a sentence and comes out, it should end there, but even under Noh T'ae-u it was once a month I went to Sŏguipo Police Station. When at home, the Sŏguipo Police Station would send a letter saying they had something to ask and come. So what would they ask? Whether what they had done before was done or not—just that kind of game-play with nothing much to ask. So I got angry saying, after suffering unjustly in the past and coming through such hardship, that's enough. Why do you keep doing this, asking if you did things before or didn't do them, and trying to make me unjustly charged again? When I complained like that, they said I was defiant to them."[^274]

○ Case of Park Nam-jin:

> "A little bit of anything and investigations came frequently. Back then I resisted. You all really do this? When I was a child, both parents passed away. Why are you investigating them? I fulfilled my national defense duty faithfully. I'm responsible for village matters whatever the circumstances. If you're going to do this, I won't cooperate with you, I'll fight back. After that they stopped investigating. I became a village chief in 1970. When my term ended in '74, investigations came again. I was furious. When they came they asked my parents' situations one by one. I even talked my third son out of applying to the military academy. Even if he passed, he wouldn't be admitted, so he gave up. He went into physical education and is teaching."[^275]

#### ② Disadvantage in Entrance, Employment Tests, and Workplace—Damage from Personal Background Investigation

○ Case of Kang T'ae-jung:

> "Now that I talk about it, in reality because I was caught under genealogical penalty, there were considerable obstacles raising children for education. In 1978 my oldest son passed the military academy entrance exam but was caught in personal background investigation. Two passed from Cheju but were caught in personal background investigation. I even told him not to go to the military academy. After that, if my children went to government offices it was complicated so they entered public enterprises instead."[^276]

○ Case of O Yong-sŭng:

> "My older brother was arrested while building a house, and with no crime, family wasn't particularly worried. But soon after we heard he had died. When I was young seeing my mother wail like that, I wondered 'Why did they take the man building and kill him?' Later as an adult while in public service, once a year personal background investigation was conducted asking lots of questions. It was genealogical penalty from the personal background investigation. So this was quite bad for my work life and a big blow to promotion. It seriously troubled me."[^277]

○ Case of Kim Hong-sŏk:

> "There was one strange incident. My oldest child graduated from Cheju University and tried taking the civil service exam, saying 'Father, I can't take the civil service exam.' 'What do you mean?' That's all I said. A while later, Yang Pong-ch'ŏn came to our village and seeing the genealogical penalty list said 'Older brother, you're also caught in genealogical penalty.' I was shocked. My father wasn't genealogical penalty, me and my wife, my oldest and second child—my daughter had no name but was on the list as 'Kim Mŭmyŏng.' I don't know what reason there was for this."[^278]

**[p. 500]**

○ Case of Ko Sŏng-hwa:

> "After that genealogical penalty followed me. When my cousin entered the military, he was a second-year Cheju Middle School student. Later when trying to become an officer, his death date was falsified forward by 20 years. He received help from the village chief at the time. Without such help, it would have been difficult to live."[^279]

○ Case of Chae Se-byŏng (testimony of Ko Nam-jun):

> "Direct family had no damage. But my older brother-in-law Chae Se-byŏng was slandered. He was definitely a rightist shouting 'Banzai Korea.' From Hanlim, on December 20, 1948, he died at Jayun-dang in the Koanae boundary of Aewol-ŭp. I heard 13 people died together at that time. Then 35 years old and a provincial Education Bureau grant officer. The 9th Regiment was just about to leave when he was arrested and confined at the Agricultural School for about two weeks. He had overall control of elementary school personnel affairs. Perhaps his reluctance to listen to rightist personnel recommendations became grounds for slander. He wasn't tried or investigated. Chae Se-byŏng graduated first in Agricultural School. He passed the elementary school certificate examination. He served as principal of Jaenŭng School. Chae Se-byŏng had two sons. Labeled 'communist bastards' and 'children of ideologically impure persons,' his sons suffered genealogical penalty damage, blocking their children's future. Though one passed for the Blue House Security Service, it hit a personal background investigation barrier. They couldn't hold any public office at all. This was in the 1970s."[^280]

○ Case of Kang T'ae-ryong:

> "I couldn't get a job. My children couldn't get jobs either. Later under President Ch'ŏn Tu-hwan when it was said this would be lifted, we did something, but before that, because of this we couldn't do anything. So my children suffered a lot. Because of their father. My son only got out of Cheju Middle School. Whenever he tried to find any kind of job, didn't background investigation already happen as soon as he submitted a resume? I myself, after leaving prison, worked 12 years at the Cheju Volunteer Fire Department. After working 12 years there, after 13 years I finally got promoted. I was selected as an exemplary member and made a firefighter. One month later the fire chief Ko Sŭng-ok called me. Go call me, he asked if I had a prior record. Even so, I didn't think of it as a prior record. But apparently the law thinks differently. So I had to quit. I was supposed to become a formal firefighter but quit after just one year."[^281]

○ Case of Mr. Kim (testimony of Hyŏn Ki-man):

> "There was one person who came out of prison alive, has lots of children and lives well, but never talks about time spent in prison. That becomes a defect—his children study well, but whenever they try to get a job somewhere it gets caught. He has seven sons. Each time a son enters the workplace, personal background investigation catches it. As it keeps happening, he tells me not to report the dead from 4·3. The sons all study well but can't find jobs anywhere. Personal background investigation catches them. Even trying to go to Japan it's caught, trying to find a workplace is caught. This person hid in the mountains. From hiding, when hungry, he'd come to civilian houses and steal cattle or horses, etc. He had no choice."[^282]

○ Case of Chang Ch'ang-wŏn:

> "4·3 gave damage not just at that time but also to us as bereaved families. After graduating from Aewol High School, I served in the Marine Corps. After discharge I got a job at the Central Intelligence Agency through a cousin's introduction. But I was fired after just one week due to ideological matters. My younger brother passed the Naval Academy exam but failed due to personal background investigation. I wanted to know my father's crime charges. So during President Kim Young-sam's administration, among classmates there was a friend in the police with three orchids rank. I said, 'Shouldn't there be a 4·3 fact-finding investigation now that democratization has occurred?' and asked, 'Is there an interrogation record in the police?' My friend said, 'If there is there might be, if there isn't there might not be.'"[^283]

○ Case of Yi Sun-jŏng:

> "At that time in Aewol, only two people went to girls' middle school, and it was the feeling of going to university back then. Still, since my mother was educated, no matter how difficult, she said we must attend school. After finishing school, some attended a teacher training institute for a few months. To get in there, there was personal background investigation. Because of my father's passing, I had the barrier applied. How frustrating could that be? Not only did it happen unjustly, but my children also had genealogical penalty obstacles, so my younger siblings said there's no need for children to study much."[^284]

○ Case of Hong Mun-p'yŏng:

> "When the armistice came, I was suddenly transferred backward. When I was at Kwangju, a major who went together said, 'You've already ridden airplanes.' After that I was transferred to Mosŭlp'o Training Center on Cheju Island and served as company commander for one year. At that time I learned I had been caught under genealogical penalty. They excluded me from flying saying I'd go North if I flew, they said. Was it because of my father? After participating in 6·25, even after the armistice continued serving faithfully as a pilot, if they treat me like this, what should I do? If this is how it is, kill me with a gun or send me North, I said. Then they said I was ideologically suspicious and ordered me to special intelligence. Released on February 5, 1956, I wasn't given a discharge certificate or severance pay. I tried the police exam but was caught in personal background investigation and couldn't even become a police officer."[^285]

○ Case of Mr. Chŏng, teacher:

> "A bereaved family of pre-emptive detention victims. Bypassed personal background investigation and served as military officer. Later while teaching at a certain high school in Cheju, became unfoundedly implicated in a 'classroom arson incident,' and his identity was exposed. Arrested by the Central Intelligence Agency and suffered torment near madness. But when an arsonist (student) attempted a second arson and was arrested on the spot by plainclothes detectives, he was freed from the nightmare of torture."[^286]

#### ③ Damage from Entry Restrictions—Damage from Exit and Entry Procedures

○ Case of Yi Po-yŏn:

> "I tried to avoid genealogical penalty and worked earnestly for the village. I worked well and tried to earn merit in the village to wash away the stigma somehow. So I'm still poor even now. Honestly speaking, I'm a person who worked sacrificially. In 1981, my older sister living in Japan invited my younger brother to at least see Japan, but I was on hold due to personal background investigation for 2 to 3 years. A police officer who was close enough to know almost my whole history asked me to write my history from age 17 onward—the village chief position, youth society chairman, sanitation committee chairman, everything. They asked to write even commendation certificates. Said to submit to the Central Intelligence Bureau and the Public Security Bureau. So I wrote about 5 pages of double-sided paper and filed a petition. The Central Intelligence Bureau responded it wasn't their department so file with the Public Security Bureau. A few days later, the Public Security Bureau sent a postcard. It had no other content—just 'Apply for Passport.' With that process, I got passport permission. It was before genealogical penalty abolition. Even after abolition, my son applied for Cheju University ROTC but was caught in personal background investigation and couldn't become an officer. Embarrassing to see my son."[^287]

○ Case of Mun Ch'ang-hae:

> "I didn't suffer genealogical penalty damage severely, but my cousins suffered a lot. In 1970 I tried to attend an expo that was being held in Japan but my passport wasn't issued. I went directly to the Central Intelligence Bureau then: 'Before I die I must travel internationally. If I can't, my descendants will never travel internationally. I'm a national merit person and have even received a medal. If genealogical penalty is applied like this, among Cheju people who's not caught in genealogical penalty? There'd be no one to do social activities.' I made a fuss. After much trouble, just before the expo closed I went. After that I couldn't endure my cousins being troubled. A cousin went to Japan illegally to make a living, so they constantly harassed him, saying hadn't he met another cousin in the Chosoren circle? So I went to Cheju Police Bureau Major Crimes Division. 'I'm a person who should receive preferential treatment from the nation. I've traveled to and from Japan frequently and know people, so ask me, not my cousin who just went to make a living and hasn't met the person and never learned.' I complained, so after that there was no such thing."[^288]

○ Case of Chang Si-yŏng:

> "After the war ended, I discharged in 1955 and resumed business again from 1956, but about 5 years later 5·16 happened. Before 5·16, I had been to the military and came back, and was conducting activities clearly. Among doctors who had been to the military, I was the only one. 5·16 happened and the police ordered me to appear. Strange, so I went—'What relation do you have with your older brother?' After that when I traveled, I had to report at the police, so I had the Central Intelligence Bureau take charge. After 5·16 more resistance developed. I met the Central Intelligence Bureau regional chief and asked 'What did I do wrong?' So I said 'Check this thoroughly.' From then the Central Intelligence Bureau regional chief had investigators examine it, and my older brother's file was registered as 'South Korean Workers' Party fraction.' How is this lawlessness? Even to kill someone, there should be some basis. How can you do something without basis? Shouldn't there be clear justification? That can't be done so unconditionally words or stories are manufactured properly? All those people who died had to be, so as those people also had basis remaining, everything became 'South Korean Workers' Party,' everything was. How could my older brother become 'South Korean Workers' Party'? So I had the Central Intelligence Bureau regional chief use his authority to have it lifted."[^289]

**[p. 501]**

<Testimony of daughter (Kim A-ja)>

I have been experiencing this continuously from childhood. We were under surveillance by the police, we were told. My mother was so frustrated—why did you do that? You are a person with no suspicion who was released, but if you continue like this, how can it be? She demanded evidence and grabbed me by the collar and made a scene. So in 1982 those people showed us documents that said "security surveillance target." I now have the original (see attached materials below). When she made a scene, they gave it then. Looking at it, this person joined the Democratic Youth Association, such stories are written.

<Attached: Security Disposition Request>

The respondent (Kim Chun-bae) was born on February 10, 1926 at address 2067, Pongkae-dong, Cheju City, original registration district of Cheju Province, as the eldest son among 2 sons and 2 daughters of deceased father Kim Kye-yŏng. In 1941 he completed four years at Cheju City Tong Po Dormitory and engaged in firewood and lumber business in Cheju City. In February 1948 he joined the Democratic Youth Association in Ido 2-dong, Cheju City. On April 3, 1948 he was arrested for the crime of participating in the Cheju Revolt and on December 13, 1948 was sentenced to 20 years imprisonment for the crime of rebellion at the Army Headquarters General Military Court. While serving his sentence at Mapo Prison, he escaped during the June 25 disorder and continued hiding in Chŏnan-myŏn, Sa-hyŏn-ri, Kongju-gun, Chungnam while working as a laborer. On July 20, 1962 he was arrested by military authorities and placed back in Mapo Prison. While serving his sentence, on August 20, 1963 the sentence suspension was canceled by the Army Headquarters General Military Court and he was released. He is a security disposition subject who received a sentence of 20 years imprisonment. Having been convicted of escaping during the June 25 disorder and not surrendering even after the September 28 restoration, being placed back in prison, even after release there is no stable livelihood and he lives unstably as a street vendor, does not participate in anti-communist rallies and national policies, and has expressed dissatisfaction that his past sentence was unjust. Considering all the above factors, there is a possibility of reoffending so security disposition is deemed necessary.[^32]

○ Case of Yang Kŭn-bang

When we came out and came to Cheju, entering our hometown Wasan. When we entered, the impact was indescribable. There is Hamdŏk Police Box, and Choji Police Box, and Cheju Police Station Information Division, Surveillance Division. For example once a week now investigators come from Choji Police Box, from Hamdŏk, from the Information Division. At that time if spies just entered Seoul we became the first target. In Cheju Province when investigators came, we were handled specially from the start. We are people branded by the state so there is nowhere to appeal and even if we starve to death among brothers or family I never revealed myself. (The police) constantly watch. Wherever I go I have to report everything.[^33]

○ Case of Ko Tae-sŏp (testimony of Ko Pong-wŏn)

Death would have been much better. If I had died in prison I would not have suffered this bitter torture in society after coming out. Among our group, a person named Ko Tae-sŏp from Namwon came out of prison and soon after, one or two times a year he was summoned to Sŏguipo Police Station for re-interrogation. He was my cousin and relation by marriage, and after two trips there, they said they released him, but then they bothered him more than before. He could not stand the annoyance and hung himself from a pine tree in the upper field.[^34]

○ Case of Yi Mun-gyo

After that I went to the military, and even then I was a surveillance target. I was assigned to the First Line Division of the 3rd Division as an infantryman, but surveillance reached all the way there. The military counterintelligence unit. So from the counterintelligence unit they sometimes come to meet with me. Coming, they ask in the manner of "What difficulties are there?" gauging the situation and going. They periodically gauged the situation and left.[^35]

○ Case of Yang Kyu-sŏk

Because I served time, my close relatives cannot do anything without being harmed because of me. And, if a person has served time and comes out, it should end there, but I was the kind of person who went to Sŏguipo Police Station at least once a month until the Noh Tae-u period. When I was at home, if Sŏguipo Police Station said they had things to ask, I would receive a letter asking me to come. So what is it—did you do the things you did in the past or not—this kind of nonsense, and there is really nothing to ask. So I became angry and said, "I suffered so much unjustly in the past, let that be enough. Why do you keep calling me like this asking if I did the things in the past or not? Are you trying to pin unjust crimes on me again?" When I put up a fight they said I was being rebellious to them.[^36]

○ Case of Park Nam-jin

There were many times when something minor would happen and investigators would come. At that time I put up resistance. I said, "Are you really going to do this? When I was young all my parents passed away—are you going to investigate that? I properly fulfilled my national defense obligations and I am responsible for village affairs. If you are going to do this, I will not cooperate with you," and I put up a fuss. After that they did not conduct investigations. I served as village chief in 1970. When the position ended in 1974, investigators came again. At that time my anger rose sharply. When they came they asked in detail how my parents had fared. I even prevented my third son from applying to the Military Academy. Even if he passed, he would not be admitted, so he gave up. He went into physical education and is now teaching.[^37]

② Various Entrance Exams, Job Placement Tests and Disadvantages in the Workplace - Damages from Background Checks

○ Case of Kang Tae-jung

Now that it's become this time, I can talk about it and such, but actually in terms of being subjected to guilt by association, there were considerable obstructions while putting my children through school. My oldest son passed the Military Academy exam in 1978, but was hit by background checks. Two people passed in Cheju Province but were hit by background checks. I also told my oldest son not to go to the Military Academy. My oldest daughter did not have obstruction entering the civil service. After that when my children tried to go into government offices, it was complicated so they entered state enterprises.[^38]

○ Case of O Yong-sŭng

My older brother was arrested while building a house during the 4.3 incident. Since he had no crime, the family was not particularly worried. But after a while we heard he had died. When I was young, seeing how my mother wailed with deep sorrow, I thought "Why did they take away the person who was building houses and kill him?" After becoming an adult while working for the government, once a year background checks were conducted and they dug into everything. It was a background check due to guilt by association. Because of this, it was quite harmful to my career and the impact on my promotion was great. It caused me considerable suffering.[^39]
