○ Case of Kim Hong-sŏk

But there was one strange thing. My oldest child graduated from Cheju National University and when trying to take the civil service exam said, "Father, I cannot take the civil service exam." "What are you saying?" That was the end of it. A while later a person named Yang Pong-ch'ŏn came to our village and while looking at the guilt by association list, said, "Brother, you are also caught in guilt by association." I was shocked. My father does not fall under guilt by association, but I, my wife, and our oldest child and second child are daughters. The second daughter does not even have a name, but is listed with the name "Kim No-name." I do not know for what reason they did this.[^40]

○ Case of Ko Sŏng-hwa

After that guilt by association followed me. When my cousin was going to join the military, he was a second-year student at Cheju Middle School. Later when he tried to become a military officer, he reported my older brother's death date 20 years earlier. I received help from the village chief at that time. Without such help, it would have been difficult to live.[^41]

○ Case of Chae Se-byŏng (testimony of Ko Nam-jun)

There are no damages in the direct family line. But my older cousin Chae Se-byŏng was slandered. He was definitely a right-winger who shouted "Long live the Republic of Korea." From Hallim, on December 20, 1948, he died at Chaun-dang on the border of Konaê, Aewŏl-ŭp. I heard that 13 people died together at that time. He was 35 years old at the time and was a scholarship officer at the Provincial Education Bureau. When the 9th Regiment was about to depart he was arrested and detained for about two weeks at the agricultural school. He had worked overseeing the personnel affairs of elementary schools. Perhaps the fact that he did not listen to right-wing personnel requests became the basis for slander. There was no trial or investigation. Chae Se-byŏng graduated first in agricultural school. He passed the elementary school Type 2 certification exam. He served as the principal of Jae-energy School. Chae Se-byŏng has two sons who suffered guilt by association damages, being called "communist bastard" and "child of ideologically impure person." They were blocked from their future. He was even admitted to the Blue House Security Division but caught by background checks. He could not do any public service. This was in the 1970s.[^42]

○ Case of Kang Tae-ryong

I could not find employment. My children could not find employment either. Later, during President Chun Doo-hwan's time, I think, when this was said to be lifted, we did it, but before that we could not do anything because of this. So our children suffered greatly. Because of their father.

My son only completed Cheju Middle School, but whenever he sent in a résumé trying to get a job anywhere, the background check would already be conducted, right? After I came out of prison I worked 12 years for the Cheju Volunteer Fire Department. After 12 years of work and 13 years of that, we both got a promotion. High Officer Ko Sŭng-ok was chief at that time. I worked for one year. I was selected as a model member to be a station member but Ko Sŭng-ok the chief told me to come. When I went he asked, "Do you have any prior criminal record?" Even so I did not consider it a criminal record. But the law seems to say otherwise. So I quit. I was going to become a proper fire department member but after one year I quit.[^43]

○ Case of Mr. Kim (testimony of Hyŏn Gi-man)

There was one person who came out of prison alive. He has many sons and lives well, and does not tell any stories of prison life. But that becomes a flaw so when his sons study and try to go to work somewhere, it catches up with them. He has seven sons. Every time a son tries to enter the workplace, when they conduct a background check, they catch it. Since it keeps happening like this, people tell me: "4.3 Incident deceased person, don't report them to me." Even though the sons have all studied well, none could find any employment. They get caught on background checks.

Even if they try to go to Japan they get caught. Even if a son tries to get a job, he gets caught. This person was someone who hid in the mountains. He hid and when he became hungry would come to civilian houses, eat cattle, and eat horses. There was no other way.[^44]

○ Case of Chang Chang-wŏn

The 4.3 gave great damage not only at that time but also to the bereaved families of us. After graduating from Aewŏl High School, I completed military service with the Marines. After discharge, through my cousin's arrangement, I got a job at the Central Intelligence Agency. But because of ideological matters I was fired after just one week. My younger brother passed the Navy Academy exam but due to background checks was eliminated. I wanted to know my father's crime name. So during President Kim Young-sam's time, among my classmates a friend who was a police officer with three mugunghwa rank insignia—"Since there has been democratization, shouldn't there be an investigation into the 4.3 truth?" I asked: "Is there an interrogation statement by the police?" Then my friend said, "If there is, there might be, and if there isn't, there might not be."[^45]

○ Case of Yi Sun-jŏng

There were two people who went to girls' middle school in Aewŏl in those days. In those days it would have felt like going to college. Even so, since my mother was a person who had gone to school, no matter how difficult, I had to continue school. After finishing school, some people went to teacher training institutes for a few months. To enter there, there was a background check. Because my father passed away, I first worked as a substitute teacher at Aewŏl Elementary School and when receiving a full-time position, the police chief came and told me to come. He said I was caught because of my father. How could my feelings not explode? It was unjust enough that he passed away. But with my children also under guilt by association disadvantage, we told even our siblings that there was no need to study much.[^46]

○ Case of Hong Mun-pyŏng

When the armistice came, I suddenly got orders to the rear. When I was in Kwangju, the same captain who went with me said, "You have already ridden the airplane." After that, I was reassigned to the Mosul training camp in Cheju Province and worked as a company commander for a year. At that time I learned I had been caught by guilt by association. They said if I rode an airplane I would go north, so they exempted me. I wondered if it was because of my father that I was caught.

I participated in the 6.25 war and after the armistice continued to work hard as a pilot, but if you are processed this way, is it right? Rather than this, shoot me to death or send me north, I objected. Then they said my ideology was suspicious and told me to come to the special intelligence unit. On February 5, 1956, I was discharged but was not given a discharge certificate or severance pay. I tried to take the police officer exam but got caught on background checks and they said I could not even become a regular officer.[^47]

○ Case of teacher named Chŏng

A bereaved family member of a pre-arrest victim. She bypassed background checks and served as a military officer. She later worked as a teacher at a high school in Cheju Province when she was unfortunately implicated in an "arson incident" and her identity was revealed. She was taken to the Central Intelligence Agency and suffered torture that nearly drove her mad. But when the arsonist (a current student) attempted a second arson and was arrested on the scene by undercover detectives, she was finally freed from the torture nightmare.[^48]

③ Damages from Restrictions on Entry and Exit

○ Case of Yi Po-yŏn

I worked hard in my neighborhood to avoid guilt by association after coming out of prison. I did my work well, hoping to gain some credit for the neighborhood to clear my name. So even now I am poor. I honestly say I was a sacrificing worker. In 1981 my oldest sister living in Japan, wanting at least to show my younger sibling Japan, sent an invitation, but for about 2-3 years I was subject to background checks and received a ruling of suspension. But a detective major was so close he heard almost my entire history, and told me to write all my history from age 17. He told me to write everything I had done—village director (village chief), youth association president, environmental sanitation committee chair, etc. He told me to write all the certificates of commendation I had received. To submit to the Central Intelligence Agency and the National Security Bureau. So I wrote about five sheets of double-sided paper and filed a civil petition. The Central Intelligence Agency sent back notice saying it was not their jurisdiction; submit it to the National Security Bureau. A few days later a postcard came from the National Security Bureau. There was no other content, just "Please apply for a passport."[^49]

**[p. 502]**

**Case of Ko Tae-sŏp (Testimony by Ko Bong-wŏn)**

It would have been better to die. If I had died in prison, I wouldn't have suffered this bitter ordeal coming out into society. Among my fellow inmates from prison, there was a person named Ko Tae-sŏp from Namwŏn-ri who came out not long after, and he was ordered to go to the Sŏgwipo Police Station for reinvestigation once or twice a year. He was a distant relative on my father's side, and twice he went and came back, but then he said they harassed him even more, so he hanged himself from a pine tree on the upper field.[^272]

**Case of Yi Mun-gyo**

Then later I went into the military, and at that time I was also on the surveillance list. I was assigned to the 3rd Division front line as an infantryman, and surveillance continued even there—by the military counterintelligence corps. So the counterintelligence corps occasionally came to meet me. They'd come and ask in the manner of "Are there any difficulties?" and they'd grasp my circumstances and leave. They regularly grasped information about my circumstances.[^273]

**Case of Yang Kyu-sŏk**

Since I served time in prison, close relatives suffered harm because of me, no matter what they tried to do. And when a person has served a prison sentence and comes out, it should be over, but even through the Roh Tae-woo period, I was a person who went to the Sŏgwipo Police Station once or twice a month. When I was at home, the police station would send a letter saying they wanted to ask me something and telling me to come. So what they asked was just about things I did in the past—did I do this or didn't I do that—just pointless tricks. So I got angry and said that I was wrongly convicted in the past and suffered so much, and when I came out, wasn't that enough? Why do you keep calling me and asking if I did this or that in the past, pointless tricks? Or are you trying to pin false charges on me again? Then they complained that I was being defiant.[^274]

**Case of Pak Nam-jin**

Whenever something happened, investigations would come frequently. At that time, I was defiant. I said to them, "Is this really necessary? My parents died long ago when I was small—what will investigating them accomplish?" I fulfilled my national defense duties faithfully and took responsibility for village matters in whatever way I could, but when I pushed back on this in anger, after that there were no more investigations. In 1970, I served as village headman. When my term as headman ended in 1974,

[^272]: Ko Bong-wŏn (72 years old, Hannam-ri, Namwŏn-ŭp, evacuee to the mountains and prisoner at Taegu Prison at the time, recorded December 14, 2000). Testimony.

[^273]: Yi Mun-gyo (65 years old, Yŏn-dong, Cheju City, activist in the 4·3 Truth Clarification Association after April 19, 2002, recorded May 16, 2002). Testimony.

[^274]: Yang Kyu-sŏk (79 years old, Hwasun-ri, Antoek-myŏn, farmer at the time and prisoner at Kwangju Prison, recorded December 15, 2000). Testimony.

---

**[p. 503]**

investigations came again. At that time, I was very angry. When they came, they'd ask in detail: what happened to your parents? My third son was trying to apply to the Military Academy, but I discouraged him. I told him that even if he passed, he wouldn't be admitted. He gave up. He went into athletics and is now a teacher.[^275]

**Disadvantages in Various School Admissions and Job Placement Tests and in the Workplace—Damages from Background Checks**

**Case of Kang Tae-jung**

Now that it's this time period, I can talk about it, but when the guilt-by-association system was actually applied, there were significant obstacles in sending children to school. My oldest son passed the Military Academy exam in 1978, but was caught in a background check. Only two people from Cheju Island passed, and he was caught in a background check. I also told him not to go to the Military Academy. My oldest daughter had no problem entering the civil service. After that, when children went to government offices and such, it became complicated, so they entered state-run enterprises.[^276]

**Case of O Yong-sŭng**

My older brother was caught while building a house (during the 4·3 incident), and since he was innocent, even his family wasn't particularly worried. But shortly after, we heard he had died. When I was young, seeing my mother like that in such great distress, I thought, "Why did they take away a man who was just building a house and kill him?" Later, when I became an adult and worked in government service, I had a background check once a year, and they would inquire very carefully. It was a background check due to the guilt-by-association system. Because of this, it was quite unfavorable for my work life, and the impact on promotion was significant. It caused me considerable suffering.[^277]

**Case of Kim Hong-sŏk**

But there was one strange thing. My oldest son graduated from Cheju National University and was about to take the civil service exam when he said, "Dad, I can't take the civil service exam." I asked, "What do you mean?" and that was the end of it. A while later, when our village person Yang Bong-ch'ŏn was looking at the guilt-by-association system list, he said, "Brother, you're also on the guilt-by-association list." I was shocked. My father was not subject to the guilt-by-association system, but I, my wife, my oldest son, and my second child, who is a daughter, were. The daughter didn't even have a name—she was listed on the roster as "Kim Munyŏng." I don't know what reason they had for doing this.[^278]

[^275]: Pak Nam-jin (67 years old, Kŭmak-ri, Hallim-ŭp, student at the time, recorded November 27, 2001). Testimony.

[^276]: Kang Tae-jung (70 years old, Hagwi 2-ri, Aewŏl-ŭp, student at Tanguek Middle School at the time, recorded August 22, 2001). Testimony.

[^277]: O Yong-sŭng (61 years old, Myŏngwŏl-ri, Hallim-ŭp, student at the time, recorded December 27, 2001). Testimony.

---

**[p. 504]**

**Case of Ko Sŏng-hwa**

After that, the guilt-by-association system followed me. My cousin entered Cheju Middle School in the 2nd year when I went into the military, and later when he was trying to become an officer, he reported my (Ko Yong-hwa's) death date as twenty years earlier. He received help from the township head at the time. Without even that kind of help, it would have been difficult to live.[^279]

**Case of Chae Se-byŏng (Testimony by Ko Nam-jun)**

There was no direct family damage. Only my older brother's wife Chae Se-byŏng was falsely accused. He was certainly a rightist who shouted "Long live the Republic of Korea." He was from Hallim, and on December 20, 1948, he died at Charundang near Aewŏl-ŭp. I heard thirteen people died at the same time. He was 35 years old at the time and was a scholarship officer in the Provincial Education Bureau. He was arrested just as the 9th Regiment was about to leave and was detained at the Agricultural School for about two weeks. He was in charge of overseeing personnel affairs at the elementary school, and I suspect that his reluctance to heed rightist-affiliated personnel recommendations may have been the pretext for his framing. There was no trial, and there was no investigation. Chae Se-byŏng graduated first in his class from the Agricultural School. He passed the elementary school second-class certification exam. He had been principal of Jaenung School. Chae Se-byŏng had two sons, and they were called "communist bastards" and "children of ideologically impure persons," and they suffered from the guilt-by-association system. They were blocked from their future paths. They passed the Blue House security exam, but were caught in a background check. They couldn't work in any government positions. This was in the 1970s.[^280]

**Case of Kang Tae-ryong**

I couldn't get employment. My son couldn't get employment either. Later, during President Chun Doo-hwan's time, I heard it was lifted, and we did get it, but before that, we couldn't do anything because of this. So our children suffered a lot. Because of their father.

My son only got through Cheju Middle School, but when he tried to find any job, didn't background checks get done immediately when he submitted his resume? I came out of prison and worked twelve years at the Cheju Volunteer Fire Department. After working there for twelve years, we were promoted together after thirteen years. At that time Ho Sŭng-ok was the station chief. I worked for a year. I was selected as a model corps member to be a fire station employee, but Ho Sŭng-ok called me in after just a month. When I went, he asked, "Do you have a criminal record?" Even so, I didn't think of it as a criminal record. But the law apparently did. So I quit. I was going to become a formal fire station employee but quit after just a year.[^281]

[^278]: Kim Hong-sŏk (65 years old, Ŭigwi-ri, Namwŏn-ŭp, child at the time and evacuee to the mountains, recorded April 2, 2002). Testimony.

[^279]: Ko Sŏng-hwa (71 years old, Ara 1-tong, Cheju City, student, recorded July 10, 2001). Testimony.

[^280]: Ko Nam-jun (78 years old, Yongdam 1-tong, Cheju City, teacher at the time, recorded August 8, 2001). Testimony.

---

**[p. 505]**

**Case of Kim M. (Testimony by Hyŏn Ki-man)**

There was one person who came out of prison alive and was doing well, with many sons. But he never told the story of having lived in prison. That became a flaw, and when his sons tried to find jobs, they were caught. He has seven sons. Every time each son tries to enter a workplace, when background checks are done, he gets caught. Since it kept happening like that, he tells me not to report him to anyone in the 4·3 incident or bereaved family records. Even though all his sons studied well, none of them could find employment anywhere. They're caught in background checks.

Even when trying to go to Japan, they're caught. Even when sons try to get jobs, they're caught. This man was someone who hid in the mountains. He hid while moving about, and when he got hungry, he'd come to civilian homes and eat cattle and horses and such. He couldn't do otherwise.[^282]

**Case of Chang Ch'ang-wŏn**

4·3 caused great harm not only at the time but also to us bereaved families. I graduated from Aewŏl High School and completed my military service in the Marine Corps. After demobilization, through my cousin's introduction, I got a job at the Central Intelligence Agency. But due to ideological issues, I was fired after just a week. My younger brother passed the Naval Academy exam but was rejected in a background check. I wanted to know my father's charge. So during President Kim Young-sam's time, I had a college classmate who was a police officer with the rank of brigadier general, and I said to him, "Shouldn't the 4·3 truth investigation be done now that democratization has occurred?" and I asked, "Is there a police interrogation record?" Then my friend said, "If there is, there is, and if there isn't, there isn't."[^283]

**Case of Yi Sun-jŏng**

In those days at Aewŏl, only two girls went to girls' middle school, and it must have felt like going to university at that time. Still, my mother, being an educated person, said that no matter how difficult, school had to be attended. After school, some people attended a short-term teacher training institute. To enter there, a background check was required. Because of my father's death, I first worked as a teacher at Aewŏl Elementary School and when I received assignment as a regular teacher, the police substation chief called me and said it would be a problem because of my father. How could my heart not burst with anger? My father died unjustly, and now because my children are subject to the guilt-by-association system, there are complications. I even told my younger siblings that they didn't need to study much.[^284]

[^281]: Kang Tae-ryong (72 years old, Yongdam 2-tong, Cheju City, farmer at the time and prisoner at Inch'ŏn Prison, recorded June 11, 2002). Testimony.

[^282]: Hyŏn Ki-man (95 years old, Sumang-ri, Namwŏn-ŭp, evacuee to the mountains at the time, recorded May 3, 2002). Testimony.

[^283]: Chang Ch'ang-wŏn (61 years old, Taitō Ward, Taitō Ward, Tokyo, Japan, native of Aewŏl-ri, Aewŏl-ŭp, recorded July 12, 2002). Testimony.

[^284]: Yi Sun-jŏng (68 years old, Aewŏl-ri, Aewŏl-ŭp, Cheju Girls' Middle School student at the time and daughter of Principal Yi Kwan-sŏk, recorded September 14, 2001). Testimony.

**[p. 506]**

**Case of Hong Mun-p'yŏng**

When the armistice came, I was suddenly transferred to the rear. When I was in Kwangju, a captain I had gone with said, "You rode an airplane." After that, I was assigned to the Mosŭlp'o Training Base on Cheju Island and worked as a company commander for a year. At that time, I realized I was subject to the guilt-by-association system. It was said that if you rode an airplane, you would defect to the North, so I was excluded. I wondered if it was because of my father.

I participated in the Korean War and even after the armistice continued to work diligently as a pilot, but I protested: is this how to treat someone like me? I said to them, either shoot me with a gun or send me to North Korea. Then the special operations unit said my thinking was ideologically suspicious and ordered me to come. As of February 5, 1956, I was discharged, but they didn't give me a discharge certificate or severance pay. I tried to take the police officer exam, but I was caught in a background check and wasn't allowed to become even a police constable.[^285]

**Case of Chŏng M., Teacher**

He is a surviving family member of a person killed in preemptive preventive detention. He evaded the background check and served as a military officer. Afterward, while working as a teacher at a high school in Cheju Island, he was unfortunately implicated in a "classroom arson case" and his identity was revealed. He was taken by the Central Intelligence Agency and endured such ordeal that he was nearly driven mad, but when the arsonist (a current student) was caught red-handed at a stakeout while attempting a second arson, he was able to escape the torture nightmare.[^286]

**Limitations on Entry and Exit Due to the Guilt-by-Association System**

**Case of Yi Po-yŏn**

To escape the guilt-by-association system, after coming out of prison, I worked hard in the village. I worked well so that I could at least make contributions to the village and clear my name. Because of this, I'm still poor now. To be honest, I was a person who worked sacrificially. In 1981, my older sister living in Japan invited me, wanting to let a younger sibling see Japan too, but I was put on hold for about 2-3 years in a background check. At that time, there was a police officer I had become quite close to—he had heard almost my entire history—and he told me to write down my entire history from age seventeen. He told me to write down everything: village headman (or chairman), youth association chairman, sanitation committee chairman, and so forth. He said to write down all the commendation certificates I had received. He said to submit to the Central Intelligence Agency and the Public Security Service. So I wrote about five pages on both sides and filed a petition. The Central Intelligence Agency responded that it was not the competent agency and told me to file with the Public Security Service. A few days later, I received a postcard from the Public Security Service. There was no other content, just the words "Please apply for a passport." Through such a process, I received passport permission
[^285]: Above, Hong Mun-p'yŏng testimony.

[^286]: Yi Do-yŏng, Preemptive Detention Deaths, Monthly Mal, 2000, p. 80.

**[p. 507]**

○ Case of Yi To-yŏng:

> "The son of Yi Hyŏn-p'il who was victimized through pre-emptive detention. Whenever trying to study abroad in the United States in 1979, personal background investigation was applied and passports weren't issued normally. Wife Ko Ch'un-hŭi went insane from this and developed severe depression, dying."[^290]

## 3. Property Damage

### a. Village Community Damage

#### 1) Damage Conditions

The Jeju 4·3 Incident brought severe damage to Cheju's village communities that had continued for hundreds of years. In particular, a considerable number of mid-mountain villages after the incident became abandoned villages unreconstructed. Among reconstructed villages, many did not have all the original village residents fully return. Since armed guerrillas primarily engaged in activities in mountain regions and mid-mountain villages, these villages inevitably suffered great damage during the armed conflict and suppression operations. In particular, after the proclamation on October 17, 1948, of the 9th Regiment Commander's order, the evacuation order for mid-mountain village residents made the damage situation even more severe. Immediately after completing the evacuation operation, the U.S. Far East Command records describe the situation as follows:

> To eliminate all resistance, assuming all mid-mountain village residents were providing support and convenience to guerrillas, a "mass killing plan" was adopted. Most of the killings were conducted by the 9th Regiment until December 1948 when it controlled the island. (Omitted) Approximately one-third of houses on the island were destroyed, and 1/4 of the 300,000 residents had their villages destroyed and were evacuated to the coast. From 45 completely destroyed villages and 43 partially destroyed villages, refugees moved to coastal village reception facilities.[^1]

In other words, as the suppression military evacuated mid-mountain village residents, they completely destroyed every civilian house, resulting in entire villages being scorched earth. In retaliation for the suppression military's evacuation operations, armed guerrillas sometimes attacked coastal villages, committing arson and burning. Governor Yim Kwan-ho pointed this out as follows:

> The number of houses burned by rebels reached 3,000, with the most tragic destruction being 300 houses in Namwŏn and 400 houses in Wimi-ri, Namwŏn-myŏn, all burned by rebels around late November, completely destroyed in one instance. And the rebels' atrocities were most vicious in November, with arson of 1,100 houses in coastal villages including Kwisupo Public Middle School and township offices—all was perpetrated that previous month (November).[^2]

Such property damage from village communities through violent conflict on both sides and suppression operations was compiled variously by time period and data source. Comprehensive official agency announcements of such conditions include the _Cheju Province Yearbook (1953)_ published by the Provincial Office after the 4·3 Incident was settled, and the _Refugee Resettlement—4·3 Incident Damage Survey (1955)_. The 4·3 Incident village damage situation appearing in these materials is as follows:

**□ Displaced Property and Details**

| Incident Type | Pre-Incident | Displaced Situation | Destroyed Houses | Farming | Non-Farming | Total Displaced |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| | Households | Population | Households | Population | Households | Population | Households | Population |

#### 2) Village Reconstruction and Displaced Person Resettlement

Mid-mountain village residents, evacuated under the evacuation order, came to live in coastal villages. Those who came to coastal villages were collectively housed in reception facilities utilizing elementary school classrooms, meeting halls, and warehouses, or were dependents of relatives. These displaced persons endured daily life with cold treatment from coastal village residents, food shortages, and surveillance by military and police authorities. Representative Chugki Yongki's account published in the _Freedom Daily_ after a 10-day inspection of Cheju in March 1949 describes conditions well:

> Regarding the displaced person situation: including mountain areas and livestock areas, even coastal village areas where 4·3 Incident destroyed houses in Namwŏn, Puk-ch'on, Tongbok, Sŏguipo—houses destroyed—16,177 homes, 30,461 structures, civilian casualties 1,193 persons, wounded 419 persons, displaced 86,797 people—these displaced persons, without time to transport food and clothing, fled resulting in literal pig-pen-like houses with straw scattered on the ground floor, getting by day to day on seaweed and wild greens while everyone suffers from hunger, inside and outside reeking unbearably. Visiting their homes makes one look up at heaven and shed tears only at the pathetic sight—impossible to witness without tears.  Cheju traditionally produces limited grain—including potatoes about 250,000 sŏk per year, a down year harvest combined with summer and fall crops. To feed over 300,000 residents, in normal times about 60,000 sŏk were imported from outside the province. This incident caused grain damage of 58,219 sŏk. Adding displaced person food requirements for three months of 29,280 sŏk, approximately 140,000 sŏk would be needed. Without importing grain, people would starve before next harvest. Beyond food, without clothing and bedding, nakedly dressed, epidemic disease invaded adding unspeakable suffering.[^3]

With the dissolution of the Cheju District Combat Command on May 15, 1949, and the withdrawal of support police on May 18, the Cheju Incident entered a phase of subsidence. Accordingly, comprehensive resettlement projects began targeting mid-mountain village residents who were evacuated or had returned from mountain refuge. By August 31, 1949, "7,379 surrendered persons and 97,703 displaced persons were to be aided with 35 'reconstruction villages' established, but only about 30,000 could be housed," according to an _Seoul Daily_ reporter's account.[^4] However, reconstruction projects did not proceed smoothly. Beginning reconstruction with completely burned villages, reconstruction was delayed due to difficulties in timber cutting and transport. Temporarily, corrugated metal houses or straw huts were built to house residents. The "reconstruction villages" were not the original residences of mid-mountain village residents, but were constructed at specific locations as "strategic villages" to prevent attacks by remaining armed guerrillas and isolate them from villages. Accordingly, residents were forcibly mobilized to build stone walls surrounding villages. A reporter's account investigating the reconstructed coastal villages depicts the displaced person situation:

> Since grain supply is extremely insufficient, the residents' staple foods were coarse grains—millet, beans, sweet potatoes, barley—by which they were able to sustain themselves through gruel, and so had lived. But residents whose own villages were reduced to ashes with nowhere to go, dwelling in so-called constructed villages designated by authorities, at least lacked even those coarse grains. So how do they live? In the Kŏro constructed village observed by this reporter, about 200 households with about 1,000 displaced persons live. Their housing, built by their own hands bringing stones and earth, forming walls with directly placing roofs on top, is extremely simple. Bedrooms literally on the ground floor spread with straw, dry grass, fallen leaves, etc., existing there. No separate kitchen is possible—that itself is bedroom and kitchen. Truly this resembles less housing for modern humans than ancient primitive human dwellings. They get by day after day on coarse grain or sweet potato gruel, and fortunately eating one or two meals daily, not three."[^5]

Meanwhile, on December 20, 1949, the Cheju Provincial Reconstruction and Revitalization Committee was organized with the provincial governor as committee chair, enlisting persons from every sector. This committee established subcommittees for fishing, industry, culture, and public security, launching comprehensive reconstruction efforts. To raise funds needed for reconstruction, it began negotiations with the government.[^6]

However, when the 6·25 War broke out in June 1950, before Cheju could recover from 4·3 Incident damage, the island was inundated with refugees from throughout the country. Just 20 days into the war, on July 16, some 10,000 persons entered through Hanlim, Sŏngsan, and Hwasun ports. After the January 4, 1951 retreat northward, tens of thousands of northern residents became the backbone of refugee waves to Cheju. Refugee numbers were just 16,000 by January 3, 1951, but reached 87,000 by January 15, exceeding 148,000 by May 20—surpassing half the original Cheju population. The sudden refugee increase created housing and food crises. Particularly, the grain shortage crisis from autumn 1952 through spring of the next year pushed Cheju society to crisis. Alone in the North Cheju County region, 30,000 people faced food shortages. Though government relief grain was allocated, serious competition between refugees and 4·3 Incident displaced persons over grain distribution occurred. Such emergence of 6·25 refugee relief problems slowed the progress of village reconstruction and displaced person resettlement from the 4·3 Incident. Additionally, reconstruction was delayed due to remaining armed guerrillas' village attacks and police military substation installations in mountain areas during operations. However, reconstruction continued step by step even amid war. In March 1952, residents of Sanghyo-ri Eastern Village, Sŏguipo-myeon (57 households, approximately 270 persons) returned to their original village.[^7]

After the war ended, village reconstruction activities accelerated. Residents of Nohyŏng-ri and Topyŏng-ri in Cheju Town submitted joint petitions to Cheju Town authorities for village return. Cheju Town submitted these to North Cheju County and police authorities. Nohyŏng and Topyŏng-ri residents had lived collectively in temporary ramshackle housing on cramped land for five years since the 4·3 Incident.[^8] Authorities delayed approval due to security conditions but beginning January 1954, accepted petitions and issued reconstruction permits. Residents of Chosugŭi and Chuja-dong, Hanlim-myeon held an entrance ceremony commemorating original village return on January 28, 1954.[^9] Residents of Chŏji-ri Myŏngri-dong, Hanlim-myeon submitted a petition on March 8 requesting original village resettlement to North Cheju County.[^10] Residents of Tŏk-ch'ŏn-ri, Kugua-myeon began returning to their original village on March 12 after seven years of displaced life.[^11]

As of end-1953, statistics from a survey by the Cheju Provincial Social Section on reconstruction return conditions by township are as follows:[^12]

**[p. 509]**

**□ Reconstruction Return Situation**

| Return | Displaced Households |
|---|---|
| Township | Original Households | Original Households Complete | Original Households Incomplete | Temporary Housing | Leasing | Total | Displaced Households | Total Returned |
| Cheju Town | 341 | 456 | 1,345 | 22 | — | 2,164 | 3,261 | — |
| Choch'ŏn-myeon (North Cheju County) | 217 | 208 | 281 | 43 | — | 743 | 1,327 | — |
| Kugua-myeon | 180 | 60 | 191 | 10 | — | 441 | 598 | — |
| Aewol-myeon | 596 | 614 | 393 | 85 | — | 1,188 | 2,180 | — |
| Hanlim-myeon | 476 | 160 | 50 | 76 | — | 1,462 | 2,125 | — |
| Sŏguipo-myeon (South Cheju County) | 102 | 123 | 102 | 39 | — | 367 | 533 | — |
| Chungmun-myeon | 83 | 139 | 90 | 75 | — | 387 | 665 | — |
| Andŏk-myeon | 196 | 211 | 116 | 69 | — | 592 | 933 | — |
| Taejŏng-myeon | 55 | 235 | 36 | — | — | 326 | 717 | — |
| Pyosŏn-myeon | 57 | 269 | 178 | 118 | — | 622 | 966 | — |
| Sŏngsan-myeon | 24 | 80 | 10 | 58 | — | 172 | 311 | — |
| Total | 2,620 | 3,093 | 4,320 | 595 | — | 10,628 | 15,228 | — |

That is, as of end-1953, of the total 15,228 displaced households, 10,628 (70%) had returned to their original villages. Meanwhile, 4,600 households remained unsettled, showing difficulties in complete reconstruction.[^9]

**[p. 510]**

Buildings set on fire by rebels totaled some 3,000 houses, and the worst damage occurred at 300 houses in Namwŏn and 400 houses in the Ŭmi-ri settlement of Namwŏn-myŏn, which were completely burned in arson by rebels around the end of November. The rebels' atrocities were most severe in November, with 1,100 houses in coastal settlements including Guipo Public Middle School and the sub-office being burned down in the cruel arson attacks conducted in that month (November).[^50]

The damage situation to village communities resulting from these armed conflicts on both sides and the suppression process was compiled in various ways according to different time periods and sources.

The official announcement of such conditions by official institutions after the 4.3 incident was settled includes the Cheju Province Overview (1953) published by the Cheju Provincial Government and the Refugee Settlement - 4.3 Incident Damage Investigation (1955). The village damage situation from the 4.3 incident shown in these materials is as follows:

**[p. 511]**

2) Village Reconstruction and Displaced Persons Resettlement

The residents of mountain villages were moved down to coastal settlements through the resettlement operation. Those who came down to coastal villages were housed collectively in accommodation facilities utilizing elementary school classrooms, community halls, and storehouses, or entrusted themselves to relatives. These displaced residents lived day to day amid the cold treatment of coastal village residents, scarcity of food supplies, and surveillance by military and police authorities. Provincial Assemblyman Chumyong in his article "A Record of Cheju Province Investigation" published in the Jayushinmun newspaper after visiting Cheju Province for about ten days in March 1949 and returning, well described the conditions of that time:

Regarding the condition of displaced persons: Whether mountainous, livestock raising areas, or even coastal settlements, first in Namwŏn, Pukch'on, Tŏngbok, and Sŏguipo after the 4.3 incident, 16,177 houses were lost with 30,461 residential units; 1,193 civilian victims and 419 wounded; with 86,797 displaced persons, these displaced persons, having fled without time to transport food and clothing, literally in pigsty-like shelters, spread straw on the ground and there they lived. With seaweed and wild plants they barely scraped by day to day. Everyone was groaning with hunger. Inside and outside the houses the stench was unbearable. When I visited, the scene I could observe—looking up at the sky, wiping away tears—was so pitiful that no one could watch without tears.[^51]

Cheju traditionally produced only 250,000 sŏk of grain including sweet potatoes as the annual crop, combining autumn and winter crops, but to feed over 300,000 residents, about 60,000 sŏk normally had to be imported from outside the province. But due to this incident, the grain damage was actually 58,219 sŏk. Moreover, calculating in the food required for 80,000 displaced persons for three months—29,280 sŏk—the total comes to about 140,000 sŏk. Without importing this amount, the population would face starvation before the summer crop. Not only grain but without clothing and bedding, people went about in rags and epidemic disease invaded, causing suffering that cannot be described in words.[^52]

On May 15, 1949, with the dissolution of the Cheju District Combat Command and the withdrawal of the support police corps on May 18, the Cheju incident entered a period of stabilization. Accordingly, large-scale resettlement operations began for mountain village residents who had been relocated or had fled to the mountains and come back down. As of the end of August 1949, "7,379 returnees and 97,703 displaced persons were aided and 35 'reconstruction villages' were established, but only 30,000 residents could be accommodated," according to a news report by a Seoul Shinmun reporter.[^53]

However, the reconstruction work did not proceed smoothly. First, work began on completely burned villages, but due to difficulties in felling and transporting timber, temporarily metal-roofed houses and thatched shelters were built to house residents. The "reconstruction village" was not the original residence of mountain village residents but was built at certain locations as a "strategic village" to prevent attacks by remaining armed guerrillas and to isolate the armed guerrillas from the villages. Accordingly, residents suffered the hardship of being forcibly mobilized to build stone walls surrounding the villages. A news report of a reporter who investigated the reconstructed residential villages reveals the living conditions of these displaced persons.

Food was extremely scarce, so the staple diet of residents consisted of millet, beans, sweet potatoes, barley and other miscellaneous grains, on which they could certainly have lived, and had lived. But those displaced persons whose own villages had been reduced to ash and had nowhere to go, concentrated in the so-called "construction villages" designated by the authorities, lacked even these miscellaneous grains. So how did they live?

At the Kŏro "construction village" that a reporter saw, about 200 households, over 1,000 displaced persons lived. Their houses were of extremely simple construction—they themselves brought stones and earth and built walls, and put roofs directly on them. Rooms were on the bare ground with straw, grass, fallen leaves spread down, and people stayed there. There could be no separate kitchen or anything—it served as both bedroom and kitchen. This truly appeared more like the living conditions of primitive humans than housing for modern people. They sustained themselves day by day on miscellaneous grain or sweet potato gruel, and it was unusual even to eat this once or twice a day, let alone three times a day.[^54]

**[p. 512]**

Meanwhile on December 20, 1949, with the Governor as chair, the Cheju Province Reconstruction and Development Committee was established with an assembly of people from all sectors. This committee established subcommittees for fishing, industry, culture, security and other areas and launched full-scale reconstruction activities. It also began adjustments with the government to secure necessary reconstruction funds.[^55]

But when the June 25 war broke out in 1950, before the damage from the 4.3 incident could even be recovered, refugees from all over the country poured into Cheju Province. Within twenty days of war on July 16, about 10,000 people entered through Hallim, Sŏngsan, and Hwasun ports in Cheju. After the "January 4 retreat" in 1951, tens of thousands of refugees centered on northern residents came to Cheju Province. The number of refugees was only about 16,000 as of January 3, 1951, but by January 15 reached 87,000, and by May 20 reached a tremendous 148,000. It exceeded half the original population of Cheju Province.

With the rapid increase in refugees, housing shortages and food shortages arose. Particularly, the grain shortage crisis that lasted from the fall of 1952 into the following spring drove Cheju society into crisis. In the Northern Cheju District area alone, 30,000 people were found unable to obtain meals. Although government relief grain was allocated, serious competition for it broke out between refugees and displaced persons from the 4.3 incident.

This emergence of the June 25 refugee relief issue thus slowed the progress of village recovery and resettlement operations for displaced persons from the 4.3 incident. The construction of suppression force military outposts in mountain areas and combat operations in response to attacks by remaining armed guerrillas also delayed recovery work.

But reconstruction work proceeded step by step even in the midst of war. In March 1952 the residents of Sange-ri Dongbu settlement in Sŏgwi-myŏn (57 households, about 270 residents) returned to their original village.[^56]

With the end of the war, village reconstruction work became more active. When residents of Nohyŏng-ri and Topyŏng-ri in Cheju Town jointly submitted a petition to return to the villages to the Cheju Town authorities, Cheju Town forwarded the petition to the North Cheju District and police authorities. The residents of Nohyŏng and Topyŏng-ri had been living collectively for five years after the 4.3 incident in temporary shacks on cramped land.[^57] The authorities initially withheld approval due to security conditions but from January 1954 accepted the petition and issued reconstruction permits.[^58]

On January 28, 1954, residents of Chosŭ-ri, Chusŏ-dong of Hallim-myŏn held a ceremony commemorating their return to their original location.[^59] Displaced persons of Chŏji-ri, Myŏngni-dong of Hallim-myŏn submitted a petition on March 8 to North Cheju District requesting reconstruction and return to original location.[^60] From March 12, 1954, residents of Tŏkch'ŏn-ri of Kujwa-myŏn ended seven years of displacement and returned to their original villages.[^61]

Based on statistics from an investigation of reconstruction and return conditions in Cheju Province as of the end of 1953 by the Cheju Province Social Section, the situation by town and village is as follows:

□ Reconstruction Return Situation

| Town/Village | Displaced Households | Completed New Construction | Incomplete New Construction | Temporary Housing | Rented Housing | Total | Total Households |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Cheju Town | 341 | 456 | 1,345 | 22 | 0 | 2,164 | 3,261 |
| Northern Cheju District | | | | | | | |
| Choji-myŏn | 217 | 208 | 281 | 43 | 0 | 743 | 1,327 |
| Kujwa-myŏn | 180 | 60 | 191 | 10 | 0 | 441 | 598 |
| Aewŏl-myŏn | 596 | 614 | 393 | 85 | 0 | 1,188 | 2,180 |
| Hallim-myŏn | 476 | 160 | 50 | 76 | 0 | 1,462 | 2,125 |
| Southern Cheju District | | | | | | | |
| Sŏgwi-myŏn | 102 | 123 | 102 | 39 | 0 | 367 | 533 |
| Chungmun-myŏn | 83 | 139 | 90 | 75 | 0 | 387 | 665 |
| Andŏk-myŏn | 196 | 211 | 116 | 69 | 0 | 592 | 933 |
| Taejŏng-myŏn | 55 | 235 | 36 | 0 | 0 | 326 | 717 |
| Pyŏngson-myŏn | 57 | 269 | 178 | 118 | 0 | 622 | 966 |
| Sŏngsan-myŏn | 24 | 80 | 10 | 58 | 0 | 172 | 311 |
| Total | 2,620 | 3,093 | 4,320 | 595 | 0 | 10,628 | 15,228 |

As of the end of 1953, 70% of the total 15,228 displaced households—10,628 households—had returned to their original villages. Meanwhile, 4,600 households remained unsettled, indicating difficulties in complete recovery.

**[p. 513]**

Meanwhile, on December 20, 1949, the Cheju Island Reconstruction and Revitalization Committee was established with the provincial governor as chairman and including representatives from all sectors. This committee established subcommittees in areas such as fisheries, industry, culture, and public order, and launched full-scale reconstruction activities. It also undertook negotiations with the government to secure the necessary funds for reconstruction projects.[^6]

However, when the June 25 War broke out in 1950, refugees from all over the nation poured into Cheju Island before the damage from the 4·3 incident could even be recovered. Just twenty days into the war, on July 16, approximately 10,000 people arrived at Hallim, Sŏngsan, and Hwasun Ports on Cheju. After the "January 4 Retreat" in 1951, tens of thousands of refugees, centered on residents from North Korea, came to Cheju. The refugee population, which numbered only about 16,000 on January 3, 1951, had grown to approximately 87,000 by January 15, and reached as many as 148,000 by May 20. This exceeded half of Cheju Island's original population.

With the sudden increase in refugees, housing shortages and food shortages ensued. In particular, the grain shortage crisis that lasted from the autumn of 1952 to the following spring pushed Cheju society into crisis. It was reported that 30,000 people in the North Cheju County area alone could not eat enough to get by. Although relief grain was allocated by the government, serious competition over it broke out between refugees and 4·3 incident disaster victims.

Such the emergence of the June 25 refugee relief problem slowed the progress of village reconstruction and the resettlement of 4·3 incident disaster victims. Additionally, reconstruction work was delayed because of attacks on villages by remaining armed units and the establishment of police unit outposts in mountain areas in response, as well as operations to combat them.

However, even in the midst of the war, village reconstruction work proceeded step by step. In March 1952, residents of Sanghyo-ri Tangbuk-rak in Sŏgwi-myŏn (57 households, population of approximately 270) returned to their original village.[^7]

When the war ended, village reconstruction work took on momentum. When residents of Nohyŏng-ri and Topyŏng-ri in Cheju Town submitted a petition for village return to the Cheju Town government with signatures from all villagers, the Cheju Town government petitioned the North Cheju County and police authorities. The residents of Nohyŏng-ri and Topyŏng-ri had been living in collective temporary housing in cramped lots since the 4·3 incident five years earlier.[^8] The authorities initially withheld approval due to security conditions, but after accepting the petition from January 1954 onwards, they issued authorization for reconstruction.

[^6]: Han'sŏng Daily News, February 16, 1950. On February 10, 1950, the revitalization committee chair (Cheju Island Provincial Governor Kim Ch'ung-hŭi) directly requested government guaranteed loans of 4.8 billion won for Cheju Island reconstruction funds from the Prime Minister (Ministry of Government Administration Documents, "Matter of Requesting Cheju Island Reconstruction Loan," February 10, 1950).

[^7]: Cheju News, March 14, 1952.

[^8]: Cheju News, November 3, 1953.

---

**[p. 514]**

Residents of Josu-ri Juju-dong in Hallim-myŏn held a ceremony on January 28, 1954 commemorating their return to their original residence.[^9] The disaster victims of Myŏngni-dong, Chŏji-ri in Hallim-myŏn submitted a petition to North Cheju County on March 8 requesting reconstruction and return to their original village.[^10] Residents of Dŏkch'ŏn-ri in Kujwa-myŏn ended their seven-year period as disaster victims on March 12 and returned to their original village.[^11]

**[p. 515]**

The Jeju Provincial Police authorities decided on the following measures to permit the return and resettlement of disaster victims to the midmountain villages starting from April 1, 1954. Regarding the resettlement issue: The suppression of communist bandits is almost complete, and in order to overcome the tragic living conditions of the provincial residents, starting from April 1, we plan to permit the return and resettlement of midmountain villages throughout the province. Residents are requested to apply for resettlement at their respective police stations by March 25, and established or newly created villages are asked to strengthen coordination between autonomous security and police-civilian cooperation.[^1] Subsequently, on September 21, 1954, six years and six months after the outbreak of the Jeju 4·3 armed uprising, Provincial Police Director Shin Sang-muk declared the lifting of the Mount Halla prohibited zone and opened it to full access. The wall defense and watch duties previously shouldered by local residents were no longer necessary. From this point onward, restoration and resettlement projects for the midmountain villages that had been evacuated became more actively developed. On November 17, 1954, for the purpose of establishing a 1955 refugee resettlement plan, each eup and myeon conducted precise basic surveys and submitted their results. This survey was intended to serve as basic data prior to a joint on-site survey conducted in early December between the Jeju Provincial authorities and KCAC (Korea Civil Assistance Command) authorities regarding refugee resettlement projects. The survey items at that time were as follows:

a. Population and residential distribution prior to the 4·3 Incident
b. Population and household residential distribution as of the end of October 1954
c. Reconstruction conditions
d. Data survey for needed resettlement plans
e. Survey of distribution of materials for farming settlement

Through this survey, the damage situation by eup and myeon and the number of households and population requiring reconstruction were as follows:

According to the situation of 4·3 Incident displaced persons returning to original villages (Jeju-do No. 8) published by Kim In-hwa, Director of Social Affairs at the Jeju Provincial Office in 1963, of the 15,228 displaced households (80,065 people) as of 1962, 7,524 households (39,916 people) returned by their own efforts; and apart from these, among the remaining 7,704 households (40,149 people), the Jeju Provincial Office announced that it was promoting return and resettlement projects for 3,965 households (17,915 people) desiring to return over a two-year plan. It is confirmed that the damage to village communities from the 4·3 Incident persisted for more than fifteen years.

**3) Reality of "Lost Villages"**

After the Mount Halla prohibition was lifted in September 1954, a considerable number of midmountain village residents went back to their original villages. For residents returning to their villages, clearing farmland and building new houses was not an easy task. Moreover, the midmountain regions were still frequently designated as "areas where communist bandits appear," and since there were remaining traces of collective deaths of residents during the incident, many residents were unwilling to return. Among those who had lived in the midmountain villages, many had already died, and residents who had been evacuated to coastal areas and resettled were scattered throughout different regions, so it was not easy for them to return to their original villages. By 1962, fifteen years after the 4·3 Incident occurred, the number of displaced persons who had not returned to their original villages was 7,704 households, comprising 40,419 people. Despite the government and Jeju Provincial authorities' active reconstruction projects, nearly half of the total displaced population avoided returning to their original villages. Subsequently, it is difficult to confirm through lack of documentation how many of them later returned to their original villages. Rather, among those who had been displaced, 6·25 war refugees who had flowed in from the mainland through government reconstruction projects did not return home once the war ended and instead became established as new residents of the midmountain regions. In the end, despite the implementation of long-term refugee resettlement and reconstruction projects following the 4·3 Incident, the failure of original residents to return resulted in the emergence of abandoned villages throughout Jeju island. Villages that disappeared due to the 4·3 Incident—so-called "lost villages"—came into being. "Lost villages" are a portion of villages that suffered intensive damage during the 4·3 Incident; they refer to cases where residents did not return and were unable to restore the villages to their former state, and the villages either became abandoned or were converted to simple farmland, ceasing to exist as villages and disappearing.[^2]

The Jeju 4·3 Incident Working Committee surveyed and confirmed 84 "lost villages" scattered throughout Jeju island over two years in 2001 and 2002. The number of villages surveyed by four cities and counties is as follows:

| City/County | Number |
|---|---|
| **Total** | **84** |
| Cheju City | 31 |
| Seogwipo City | 4 |
| Buk Cheju-gun | 35 |
| Nam Cheju-gun | 14 |

Additionally, survey results of the household numbers, resident numbers, and other conditions at that time, as well as the number of victims in these villages, are as follows:

[^2]: Jeju 4·3 Incident 50th Anniversary Academic and Cultural Project Promotion Committee (ed.), *Finding the Lost Villages*, Hakmunsa, 1998, p. 9.

**[p. 518]**

Despite implementation of resettlement operations, nearly half of all displaced residents were reluctant to return to their original locations. How many of these subsequently returned to their original locations is difficult to confirm due to lack of records. Instead, among those resettled through government reconstruction operations, June 25 refugees who had moved in from the mainland, rather than returning to their hometowns after the war, established themselves as new highland residents.[^62]

As a result, despite the implementation of long-term refugee resettlement and reconstruction operations following the 4.3 incident, villages became ruins as original residents did not return. Villages destroyed as a result of the 4.3 incident—what might be called "lost villages"—were created in various places throughout Cheju Province. "Lost villages" are a portion of villages that suffered concentrated damage during the 4.3 incident where residents did not return and villages were not restored as before but became abandoned or changed to simple farmland, and disappeared without further existing as villages.[^63]

The 4.3 Incident Executive Committee conducted investigations over two years, 2001 and 2002, and confirmed 84 "lost villages" scattered throughout Cheju Province. The number of villages investigated by each of the four cities and districts is as follows:

**[p. 521]**

The Jeju 4·3 Incident Working Committee selected typical villages that underwent detailed surveys for each eup and myeon among these "lost villages" and pursued marker-raising projects over two occasions. Specifically, from March 29 to April 3, 2001, markers were installed at six locations: O U-neul in Ora-dong, Cheju City; Deu Reu Gu Reung in Nohyeong-dong, Cheju City; Yeongnam Village in Dae-cheon-dong, Seogwipo City; Da Rang Swi in Seohwa-ri, Gujwa Eup; Gwae Teu Reu in Wa-heul-ri, Jocheon Eup; and Mu Deung Yi Wat in Dong Gwang-ri, Andeok Myeon. Subsequently, from March 27 to April 2, 2002, markers were installed at six more locations: Ri Saeng Yi in Hae-an-dong, Cheju City; Bil Re Mot in Myeong Wol-ri, Hallim Eup; Ja Ri Wat in Bong Seong-ri, Aewol Eup; Bil Re Ga Reum in Han Nam-ri, Namwon Eup; Sae Na Mot Gos in Sin Do 3-ri, Daejeong Eup; and Sae Ga Reum in Ga Si-ri, Pyoseon Myeon. Inscribed on these markers are the formation background of the villages, the residential situations of residents at that time, and information regarding victim damage from the 4·3 Incident and the circumstances of village abandonment. The following is an excerpt from the marker inscriptions, presenting only village-specific damage situations and circumstances of village abandonment:

○ Yeongnam-dong: "Of the 90-odd residents in 16 households, some 50 who could not flee suffered the misfortune of being sacrificed."

○ Mu Deung Yi Wat: "This is the site of Mu Deung Yi Wat in Andeok Myeon, Nam Cheju-gun, a lost village that burned completely on November 21, 1948, in the midst of the 4·3 Incident. (omitted) During 4·3, approximately 100 people from Mu Deung Yi Wat (130 households), approximately 50 people from Sam Bat Gu Seok (46 households), and 6 people from Jo Su Gwae (6 households) were sacrificed."

○ Deu Reu Gu Reung: "The people of Deu Reu Gu Reung Village had to leave their familiar homes and move elsewhere in the midst of 4·3, and had to bear witness with tears in their eyes to the sacrifice of parents, siblings, and neighbors."

○ O U-neul: "This is the site of O U-neul Village, a natural hamlet in Ora-dong, Cheju City, that burned completely and became a lost village in the early winter of 1948 due to the 4·3 Incident. (omitted) In this village, among the approximately 100 residents (23 households) at that time, approximately 13 were sacrificed."

○ Gwae Teu Reu: "During the midst of 4·3, on November 13, 1948, houses burned, and residents had to either move to coastal villages or flee to the hills. In this process, many residents were sacrificed."

○ Da Rang Swi: "This is the site of Da Rang Swi Village in Gujwa Eup, Buk Cheju-gun, a lost village that burned completely in the midst of the 4·3 Incident around November 1948."

○ Ri Saeng Yi: "This is the site of Ri Saeng Yi Village in Hae-an-dong, Cheju City, a lost village that burned completely due to the 4·3 Incident. However, the tempest of 4·3 did not spare this village either, for on November 20, 1948, an evacuation order was issued, and after residents went down to a lower village without even properly gathering their belongings, the village burned completely and became ashes. In this process, some 50 residents scattered like dewdrops, and after survivors settled in Hae-an-ri and began living there, they never returned to Ri Saeng Yi Village, so now only weeds overgrow the area, and bamboo alone testifies to the history that has passed in the empty house sites."

○ Bil Re Ga Reum: "This place is the site of Bil Re Ga Reum in Han Nam-ri, Namwon Eup, Nam Cheju-gun, a lost village that burned completely on November 7, 1948, due to the 4·3 Incident. However, the tempest of 4·3 blew ruthlessly upon this village as well, burning it completely, and residents hid themselves scattered along the slopes of Geо Rin Oreum or near Seo Jung Cheon, struggling in despair unable to see even one step ahead. In this process, 25 people were sacrificed, and as many as 5 households had their family lines cut off. When Han Nam-ri was reconstructed in 1953 centered on the current township office, the remaining 100-odd residents, not wishing to relive the tragedy of that time, never returned to Bil Re Ga Reum, and this has continued to the present day."

○ Bil Re Mot: "This is the site of Bil Re Mot Village in Myeong Wol-ri, Hallim Eup, Buk Cheju-gun, a lost village that burned completely in the midst of 4·3. This village too was not spared from the tempest of 4·3 that swept across all of Jeju in 1948. Around November 20, following an evacuation order, houses burned completely and became ashes, and residents had to scatter in all directions to coastal villages. And more than that, over 20 precious lives were sacrificed, becoming wandering ghosts drifting on the wind and cloud paths. After settling in Myeong Wol-ri Gorim-dong in the spring of 1949 and building temporary structures to live in, residents never returned to Bil Re Mot, so now only the pond site and bamboo remain to testify to people once having lived here."

○ Sae Ga Reum: "This place is the site of Sae Ga Reum Village in Ga Si-ri, Pyoseon Myeon, Nam Cheju-gun, a lost village that burned completely on November 15, 1948, due to the 4·3 Incident. However, the tempest of 4·3 blew ruthlessly upon this village as well, burning it completely and reducing it to ashes, and residents scattered in the vicinity and surrounding villages, struggling to eke out a living. Among the displaced persons accommodated at Pyoseon Elementary School, 17 lost their lives near the so-called Beodeul Mot, and in the course of the 4·3 Incident, 25 people were sacrificed. From February 1949 onward, as Ga Si-ri was reconstructed centered on the current township office, 2 households returned again to Sae Ga Reum, but unable to bear the loneliness, they left again, and in the end failed to restore the village to its former state."

○ Sae Na Mot Got: "This is the site of Sae Na Mot Got Village in Shin Do-ri, Daejeong Eup, Nam Cheju-gun, a lost village where even the traces of the village were lost in the midst of 4·3. However, the tempest of 4·3 did not spare this village either, for in 1948 in November, for the sake of maintaining public order, Sae Na Mot Got residents were evacuated to Shin Do 1-ri, and in this process, 4 residents passed away. After building walls in the evacuation site and taking up sentry duty to establish a foothold, when a reconstruction order came in 1950, residents never returned to Sae Na Mot Got, making it a lost village."

○ Ja Ri Wat: "This is the site of Ja Ri Wat Village in Eo Do 2-gu, Aewol Eup, Buk Cheju-gun, a lost village where the village burned completely in the midst of 4·3. However, the tempest of 4·3 shook these villages fiercely without mercy, for around the middle of November 1948, an evacuation order was issued, and after residents moved to lower villages, the village burned completely and became ashes, and in this process, 5 people were sacrificed. After residents took up a foothold at Shin Myeong-dong at the entrance of Bong Seong-ri and began living, they never returned to Ja Ri Wat and other places."

Above are the damage situations and circumstances of village abandonment introduced on the markers of 12 "lost villages." However, in the case of some villages, the timing of village abandonment and circumstances of loss are omitted. Even in cases where it is written that villages were lost due to evacuation orders or burning, the actors responsible—who issued the evacuation order and burned the villages—are not identified. Due to these problems, during the "marker-raising" process, bereaved families of victims from Gon Heul Village in Hwabuk-dong, Cheju City, protested that the circumstances of village loss and the causes of damage were treated insufficiently.[^3] Taking this into account, the timing of village loss, reasons for loss, number of victims, and responsible parties were supplemented based on existing survey materials and testimony, and compiled in a table as follows. Although markers were not raised for Gon Heul Village, since the Jeju 4·3 Incident Working Committee conducted a survey of damage to Gon Heul Village in preparation for raising a marker there, it is included together.

[^3]: An Myeong-ho (age 70, Hwabuk-dong, Cheju City, former member of the Civil Guard, recorded on August 12, 2002).

**[p. 522]**

|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| O U-neul | 48.11 | Evacuation Order | 13 | 9th Regiment | Survey/Testimony |
| Deu Reu Gu Reung | 48.11.19 | Evacuation Order | | 9th Regiment | Literature |

Regarding "lost villages" due to the 4·3 Incident, beyond the currently surveyed 84 villages, there is a need for additional surveys. More precise and systematic surveys are also required regarding village-specific damage situations. Following the completion of the full report in the future, systematic and detailed follow-up truth investigation work is anticipated.

**b. Public Facility Damage**

During the course of the 4·3 Incident, damage to public facilities such as schools, government buildings, and police substations was primarily caused by attacks by armed guerrillas. In particular, schools were mainly utilized as quarters for suppression force units. Conversely, during the period of strong suppression operations spanning November-December 1948, schools and government buildings in midmountain villages were primarily burned and destroyed by military units in accordance with the strong suppression operations.[^4]

The first officially compiled data on public facility damage from the 4·3 Incident after the incident was quelled is the Damage Overview Table for Jeju Province's Riot Incident submitted on February 10, 1950, when Jeju Provincial Governor Kim Chung-heui requested reconstruction loans from the Prime Minister. Among these, the damage situation for public facilities is shown in the chart above. In 1953, survey results of damage conducted by Jeju Provincial authorities were included in the Jeju Province Yearbook, and regarding public facility damage, the condition of school damage was recorded as follows:

| Division | Schools Affected | Classrooms | Damage Degree | Damage Amount | Remarks |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Buk Cheju-gun | 31 | 134 | Completely Destroyed 30, Damaged 1 | 312,340,000 | Elementary Schools |
| Nam Cheju-gun | 45 | 180 | Completely Destroyed 43, Damaged 2 | 379,713,000 | |
| **Subtotal** | **76** | **314** | **Completely Destroyed 73, Damaged 3** | **692,053,000** | |
| Nam Cheju-gun | 1 | 7 | Completely Destroyed 1 | 12,386,000 | Seogwi Middle School |
| **Subtotal** | **1** | **7** | **Completely Destroyed 1** | **12,386,000** | |
| **Grand Total** | **77** | **321** | **Completely Destroyed 74, Damaged 3** | **704,439,000** | |

The tally of school facility damage from the 4·3 Incident shown in this table is not entirely accurate. In the case of middle schools, in addition to Seogwi Middle School, Jungmun Middle School and Namwon Higher Civic School were also completely destroyed.[^5] The destruction of school facilities made school closures unavoidable, and the resulting interruption of studies caused significant educational losses. In addition to the destruction of school facilities, numerous government offices including police substations were destroyed during mutual armed clashes between armed guerrillas and suppression forces and police, resulting in the paralysis of public administration. In particular, the town offices of Andeok Myeon, Gujwa Myeon, Jungmun Myeon, and Jocheon Myeon were burned, resulting in the loss of family registers,[^6] which consumed considerable manpower and budget to restore. Beyond the destruction of public facility buildings, there was also considerable material damage from road blockades and destruction, telegraph pole cutting and destruction, and loss of transportation means.

[^5]: Yoon Seok-tsan, op. cit., pp. 60-61.

[^6]: Jungmun Township Office was burned by armed guerrillas on November 5, 1948, and Jocheon Township Office on November 11, 1948, resulting in the loss of family registers, family separation records, and other related documents (Republic of Korea Official Gazette No. 98, May 27, 1949, Supreme Court Announcement No. 11; No. 168, September 2, 1949, Supreme Court Announcement No. 21). Prior to this, around the May 10 election period, armed guerrillas set fire to Andeok Township Office, resulting in the loss of family registers and such (Testimony of Song Mun-su, age 81, Deok-su-ri, Andeok Myeon, recorded July 19, 2002), and on May 11, armed guerrillas set fire to Gujwa Township Office, resulting in the loss of family registers (Testimony of Kim Byeong-su, age 72, Pyeong-dae-ri, Gujwa Myeon, recorded November 20, 2001). On the other hand, of the 20 volumes of family registers at Daejeong Township Office, 14 volumes were lost through theft on May 24, 1948 (Republic of Korea Official Gazette No. 99, May 30, 1949, Supreme Court Announcement No. 13).

**c. Industrial Damage**

In the course of the 4·3 Incident, with frequent attacks by armed guerrillas and suppression by military and police suppression forces, various industrial facilities were destroyed and their operations halted. The mutual armed clashes resulted in restrictions on agricultural production, losses of cattle and horses grazing in pasture areas, and prohibitions on fishing and shellfish collection by fishermen and women divers, forcing Jeju residents to suffer considerable material losses. Furthermore, as residents were required to join the Civil Guard and Youth Defense Corps and participate in suppression operations or village defense, they were unable to engage in their regular livelihoods, incurring immeasurable material losses. Breaking down industrial sector damage from the February 1950 Damage Overview Table for Jeju Province's Riot Incident presented earlier, the situation is as follows:

Regarding "lost villages" from the 4.3 incident, in addition to the 84 villages currently investigated, there is a need for additional investigation. More precise and systematic investigation of damages in each village is required going forward. In the future, after the overall full report is completed, systematic and detailed follow-up truth clarification work is expected.

b. Public Facility Damages

Damages to public facilities such as schools, government offices, and police stations during the 4.3 incident were caused mainly by attacks by armed guerrillas. Schools in particular were mainly used as camping sites by suppression military units. On the other hand, during the period of strong suppression operations spanning November-December 1948, schools and government offices in mountain villages were mainly burned and destroyed by military forces as a result of strong suppression operations.[^64]

| Category | Livestock | | Forestry | | Crops | | | | |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| | | | | | Grains | Sweet Potatoes | Cotton | Vegetables | |
| **War Casualties & Logging/Arson** | 17,860 Head | 6,009 Head | 22,989 Head | 1,173 Jeongbo | 135,415 Households | 4,196,113 Gwan | 97,356 Geun | 899,860 Gwan | |

**Source:** Kim In-hwa, "Return of 4·3 Incident Displaced Persons to Original Settlements," *Jeju-do*, No. 8, 1963.

The interruption of production and distribution in various industrial sectors directly triggered unemployment among the productive labor force of Jeju residents. As the incident continued over a prolonged period, Jeju residents' unemployment situation deteriorated further due to flight to the mountains, accommodations in coastal areas, village defense, and dispatch to suppression operations. This fact is clearly confirmed by the following table of unemployed persons by industry and province tabulated by the Social Affairs Bureau Labor Division as of May 1949.[^7] Specifically, the agricultural sector exceeded the national average, and in the fisheries, manufacturing, and commercial sectors, the proportion of unemployed relative to population also exceeded other regions. Given that Jeju Province's population at that time represented 1.26% of Korea's total population,[^8] the 73,263 unemployed in Jeju accounted for 8.24% of Korea's total unemployed. Furthermore, the fact that 28.8% of Jeju Province's total population was in a state of unemployment demonstrates the severity of the unemployment crisis. In other words, alongside human casualties from the 4·3 Incident, the surviving residents of Jeju Province suffered from severe economic hardship.

[^7]: Republic of Korea, Statistical Summation, No.5, May 1949, American Embassy Despatch No.416, July 6, 1949.

[^8]: As of May 1, 1949, the total population of the Republic of Korea was tallied at 20,188,641, with Jeju Province's population at 254,589, representing 1.26%.

**[p. 528]**

| Category | Facility | Unit | Complete Destruction |  | Partial Destruction |  | Total Damage |

The cessation of production and distribution in various industrial sectors immediately caused unemployment among the productive population of Cheju islanders. As the crisis prolonged, the situation worsened with refugees fleeing to the mountains, people undergoing internment in coastal areas, village patrols, and conscription for suppression operations. This unemployment situation deteriorated further. The following table compiled by the Social Bureau's Labor Division as of May 1949 clearly demonstrates this fact.[^1]

In other words, the agricultural sector exceeded the national average rate, and the unemployment rate by population in fishing, manufacturing, and commerce also exceeded that of other regions. Given that Cheju's population at the time represented only 1.26% of the entire Korean population,[^2] the 73,263 unemployed persons in Cheju constituted 8.24% of Korea's total. Furthermore, the fact that 28.8% of the entire Cheju provincial population was unemployed demonstrates the severity of the unemployment crisis. In other words, alongside the loss of life caused by the Jeju 4·3 Incident, the surviving Cheju residents endured extreme economic hardship.

## V. Conclusion of the Truth Investigation Report

The Jeju 4·3 Incident, which occurred on Cheju Island during the American Military Government period, was a tragic event with severe loss of life, second only to the Korean War in modern Korean history. Nevertheless, even after fifty years had passed since the incident, no comprehensive and detailed truth investigation had been conducted, leaving no end to civil complaints. It was only when the Jeju 4·3 Special Act was enacted and promulgated on January 12, 2000, that a government-level truth investigation was finally undertaken.

The background to the incident was extremely complex, involving a variety of intertwined causes that cannot be explained by a single factor. Cheju Island, with its geopolitical significance as a key region in Northeast Asia, became a strategic base where some 60,000 Japanese soldiers were stationed to prevent American landings in the final stages of the Pacific War. Immediately after the war's conclusion, there were rapid population fluctuations due to the withdrawal of Japanese forces and the return of approximately 60,000 Cheju residents from outside the island. Contrary to initial hopes regarding liberation from colonial rule, returning migrants faced unemployment, shortages of necessities, the loss of hundreds to cholera, and severe famine. Compounding these hardships were the failure of rice policy, the transformation of colonial police into military government police, and the corrupt profiteering of military government administrators, which emerged as major social issues. In this atmosphere, the March 1 shooting incident of 1947 erupted, further exacerbating public sentiment.

The March 1 shooting incident was an event in which police fired on demonstrators, killing six and seriously wounding eight. It was later determined that most of the victims were ordinary civilians who were merely observing the protest. This incident served as the spark that ignited the Jeju 4·3 Incident. At this time, the South Korean Workers' Party (SKWP) Cheju branch conducted organized anti-police activities. The "March 10 General Strike" in protest of the police shooting involved over 95% of workplaces throughout Cheju—government offices, private enterprises, and more—in what was an unprecedented joint labor-management strike unprecedented in Korea's history.

Treating the situation seriously, the American Military Government dispatched an investigative team to Cheju, analyzing that the general strike was rooted in public resentment against police firing, amplified by SKWP agitation. However, the post-incident response placed greater emphasis on "SKWP agitation" than on "police firing" and pursued a hard-line policy. The provincial governor and the military government leadership were completely replaced by outsiders, and reinforcement police and Sŏbuk Youth Corps members flooded into Cheju to conduct mass arrest operations against the strike's instigators. Within a month of arrests, over 500 people were detained, and by the time of the April 3 uprising, 2,500 had been imprisoned over the course of a year. Terrorism and torture continued.

**[p. 534]**

In March 1948, three torture-death incidents occurred in rapid succession at police stations across the island. Cheju society was transformed into a crisis situation on the verge of explosion.
